Hanafi Position on Flogging and Exile for the Bikr
Hanafi argument against combining jald and nafy for the bikr

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on combining flogging and exile for the bikr, explain the Hanafi proof from the Quran and the abrogation argument, and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument.

Core Idea
The Hanafi school holds that flogging alone is the hadd for the bikr; exile is tazir, not hadd.

This passage addresses a key inter-madhhab dispute over the punishment for unmarried fornicators. While the Shafi'i school combines flogging and exile as a fixed hadd, the Hanafi school firmly separates them. Understanding the proofs and reasoning behind each position will sharpen your ability to evaluate evidence and appreciate the structure of Hanafi fiqh.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify what the Hanafi school rules regarding flogging and exile for the bikr. Note that the passage begins with the author stating the Hanafi view: no combination.
The Hanafi view is that jald alone is the hadd; nafy is not part of the hadd.
2
Identify the opponent and their view
Who disagrees and what do they say? Al-Shafi'i combines both as hadd. Locate the opponent's proof: the hadith and the maslaha argument.
The Shafi'i proof is a hadith: 'al-bikru bil-bikri jaldu mi'atin wa taghribu 'am'.
3
Analyse the Hanafi response to the opponent's proof
How does the Hanafi school rebut the Shafi'i proof? The Quranic verse 'fajlidu' is interpreted as making flogging the exclusive punishment. The hadith is claimed to be abrogated. Also, exile is argued to cause more harm than good.
The Hanafi response uses the particle 'fa' to indicate exclusivity and the principle of naskh.
4
Examine the Hanafi rational argument
Beyond textual proof, the Hanafi school argues that exile leads to increased zina by removing shame and cutting off livelihood. This is a rational (istihsan) argument that preponderates the Hanafi view.
The rational argument is supported by the statement of 'Ali: 'Exile is sufficient as a trial.'
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
What is the practical difference between the two schools? The Hanafi school allows the ruler to exile as tazir, while the Shafi'i school mandates exile as part of the hadd. This affects the ruler's discretion.
Practical consequence: In Hanafi law, exile is not automatic; it is at the imam's discretion based on maslaha.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قَالَ وَلَا يُجْمَعُ فِي الْبِكْرِ بَيْنَ الْجَلْدِ وَالنَّفْيِ وَالشَّافِعِيُّ يَجْمَعُ بَيْنَهُمَا حَدًّا لِقَوْلِهِ عَلَيْهِ السَّلَامُ: «الْبِكْرُ بِالْبِكْرِ جَلْدُ مِائَةٍ وَتَغْرِيبُ عَامٍ» وَلِأَنَّ فِيهِ حَسْمَ بَابِ الزِّنَا لِقِلَّةِ الْمَعَارِفِ. وَلَنَا قَوْلُهُ تَعَالَى: {فَاجْلِدُوا} جَعَلَ الْجَلْدَ كُلَّ الْوَاجِبِ رُجُوعًا إِلَى حَرْفِ الْفَاءِ أَوْ إِلَى كَوْنِهِ كُلَّ الْمَذْكُورِ. وَلَا فِي التَّغْرِيبِ فَتْحُ بَابِ الزِّنَا لِانْعِدَامِ الِاسْتِحْيَاءِ مِنَ الْعَشِيرَةِ، ثُمَّ فِيهِ قَطْعُ مَوَادِّ الْبَقَاءِ فَرُبَّمَا تَتَّخِذُ الزِّنَا مَكْسِبَةً وَهُوَ مِنْ أَقْبَحِ وُجُودِ الزِّنَا. وَهَذِهِ الْجِهَةُ مُرَجِّحَةٌ لِقَوْلِ عَلِيٍّ: «كَفَى بِالنَّفْيِ فِتْنَةً». وَالْحَدِيثُ مَنْسُوخٌ كَشَطْرِهِ، وَهُوَ قَوْلُهُ عَلَيْهِ السَّلَامُ: «الثَّيِّبُ بِالثَّيِّبِ جَلْدُ مِائَةٍ وَرَجْمٌ بِالْحِجَارَةِ» وَقَدْ عُرِفَ طَرِيقُهُ فِي مَوْضِعِهِ. إِلَّا أَنْ يَرَى الْإِمَامُ فِي ذَلِكَ مَصْلَحَةً فَيُغَرِّبَهُ عَلَى قَدْرِ مَا يَرَى، وَذَلِكَ تَعْزِيرٌ وَسِيَاسَةٌ لِأَنَّهُ قَدْ يُفِيدُ فِي بَعْضِ الْأَحْوَالِ، فَيَكُونُ الرَّأْيُ فِيهِ إِلَى الْإِمَامِ. وَعَلَيْهِ يُحْمَلُ النَّفْيُ الْمَرْوِيُّ عَنْ بَعْضِ الصَّحَابَةِ رَضِيَ اللَّهُ عَنْهُمْ أَجْمَعِينَ.
He said: And one does not combine for the unmarried (bikr) between flogging and exile, and al-Shafi'i combines both as a hadd, due to his saying (peace be upon him): 'The unmarried with the unmarried: flogging of one hundred and exile for a year.' And because in it is blocking the door of fornication due to the scarcity of acquaintances. And our proof is the saying of the Most High: 'So flog them.' He made flogging the entire obligation, with reference to the letter fa, or to it being the entirety of what is mentioned. And in exile is opening the door of fornication due to the absence of shame from the tribe; then in it is cutting off the means of livelihood, so perhaps she will take fornication as a profession, and that is among the most repulsive forms of fornication. And this aspect is preponderant over the saying of 'Ali: 'Exile is sufficient as a trial.' And the hadith is abrogated like a portion of it, namely his saying (peace be upon him): 'The married with the married: flogging of one hundred and stoning with stones.' And its method is known in its place. Unless the imam perceives a maslaha in that, so he may exile him to the extent that he sees, and that is tazir and siyasah, because it may be beneficial in some situations, so the opinion in it rests with the imam. And upon that is carried the exile reported from some of the Companions, may Allah be pleased with them all.
Source pages p. 150

Commentary
This passage addresses a dispute between the Hanafi school and Imam al-Shafi'i regarding whether flogging and exile (jald and nafy) are combined as a hadd punishment for the unmarried (bikr) fornicator. The Hanafi position, stated by the author, is that they are not combined. The Shafi'i argument relies on the hadith 'al-bikru bil-bikri jaldu mi'atin wa taghribu 'am' and the reasoning that exile blocks fornication by reducing acquaintances. The Hanafi response first cites the Quranic verse 'fajlidu' from Surah al-Nur, arguing that the particle 'fa' (indicating cause and effect) and the context make flogging the exclusive punishment. Then they argue that exile actually opens the door to fornication by removing familial shame and potentially forcing the woman into prostitution, citing the statement of 'Ali: 'Exile is sufficient as a trial.' Additionally, the hadith used by al-Shafi'i is claimed to be abrogated (mansukh) just like the part of another hadith that combined flogging and stoning for the married, which is known to be abrogated by the practice of the Prophet. However, the text allows the ruler (imam) to order exile as a discretionary punishment (tazir) if he sees a maslaha (public interest), and this is considered siyasah (policy). The narrations of some Companions practicing exile are interpreted as tazir, not as hadd.

Key Terms
بِكْر (bikr): A person (male or female) who has never been married or has not consummated marriage.
جَلْد (jald): Flogging with a whip as a hadd punishment, specifically 100 lashes for the unmarried fornicator.
نَفْي (nafy): Exile or banishment from one's hometown as a punishment; in this context, as a potential hadd or tazir.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, why is exile not combined with flogging as a hadd for the unmarried fornicator?
A) Because the Quran only mentions flogging, and exile is considered a form of tazir.
B) Because the hadith that mentions exile has been abrogated by the verse of flogging.
C) Because the ruler never has the authority to order exile.
D) Because the bikr is not subject to hadd at all.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school distinguishes between fixed hadd (flogging) and discretionary tazir (exile), using Quranic priority and abrogation to reject mandatory exile for the bikr.

Delay of Hadd for a Sick Person
Differentiating between rajm and jald for a sick fornicator

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state why a sick fornicator is stoned immediately but flogged only after recovery, and apply the same rationale to amputation during extreme temperatures.

Core Idea
Rajm is carried out despite illness because death is intended; flogging is delayed to avoid unintended death.

The application of hadd punishments must consider the physical state of the offender to avoid contradictory outcomes. This lesson examines how the Hanafi school distinguishes between punishments that aim to extinguish life and those that aim to deter, using the example of a sick person guilty of zina or theft.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the two possible hadds
The act is zina committed by a sick person. The hadd depends on marital status: rajm for a muhsan (married) and jald for a ghayr muhsan (unmarried).
2
State the ruling for each hadd
For rajm: the person is stoned despite illness. For jald: flogging is delayed until recovery.
3
Analyze the rationale
Rajm aims to destroy the person (al-itlaf mustahaqq), so illness does not prevent its immediate application. Jald aims to deter (zajr), and flogging a sick person could lead to unintended death, defeating the purpose.
The underlying principle is that the execution of hadd must align with its intended objective.
4
Apply the analogy to qat'
The text extends the reasoning to amputation for theft: it is also delayed during extreme heat or cold to avoid causing death, as it is a deterrent punishment like jald.
This shows the Hanafi use of qiyas in hudud.
5
Note the consensus
This ruling is from the zahir al-riwayah and is agreed upon among the Hanafi imams, with no intra-madhhab ikhtilaf.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَإِذَا زَنَى الْمَرِيضُ وَحَدُّهُ الرَّجْمُ رُجِمَ لِأَنَّ الْإِتْلَافَ مُسْتَحِقٌّ فَلَا يَمْتَنِعُ بِسَبَبِ الْمَرَضِ، وَإِنْ كَانَ حَدُّهُ الْجَلْدَ لَمْ يُجْلَدْ حَتَّى يَبْرَأَ كَيْلَا يُفْضِيَ إِلَى الْهَلَاكِ، وَلِهَذَا لَا يُقَامُ الْقَطْعُ عِنْدَ شِدَّةِ الْحَرِّ وَالْبَرْدِ.
And if a sick person fornicates, and his prescribed punishment is stoning, he is stoned because destruction is due, so it is not prevented due to illness. And if his prescribed punishment is flogging, he is not flogged until he recovers, lest it lead to death. And for this reason, amputation is not carried out during extreme heat or cold.
Source pages p. 151

Commentary
This passage rules on the application of hadd to a sick person who commits zina. The ruling differentiates based on the nature of the punishment: if the hadd is rajm (stoning to death), the punishment is carried out despite illness because the purpose of rajm is to destroy the person (it is a capital punishment). Since death is intended, illness does not prevent it. However, if the hadd is jald (flogging), the punishment is delayed until the person recovers from illness because the purpose of flogging is deterrence (zajr) and not destruction. Flogging a sick person could lead to death, which would defeat the purpose. The text draws an analogy with the amputation of a thief's hand, which is also delayed during extreme heat or cold to prevent death or severe harm. This is based on the principle of avoiding harm (darar). The ruling is consistent across the four schools in principle, though details may differ.

Key Terms
مَرِيض (mariid): A person suffering from an illness that may be exacerbated by flogging.
رَجْم (rajm): Stoning to death as a hadd punishment for a married fornicator.
قَطْع (qat'): Amputation of the hand as a hadd punishment for theft.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is a sick person stoned immediately if the hadd is rajm, but flogged only after recovery?
A) Because rajm is a lighter punishment than flogging.
B) Because rajm aims to destroy the person, so illness does not matter; flogging aims to deter, and may kill a sick person.
C) Because the Quran commands stoning without delay.
D) Because the imam has discretion to choose when to apply hadd.

Key Takeaway
The execution of hadd is governed by the purpose of the punishment: punishments that intend destruction (rajm) are not delayed, while deterrent punishments (jald, qat') are delayed if they risk death.

Delay of Hadd for a Pregnant Woman
Ruling on delaying hadd for a pregnant woman until delivery and weaning

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rules for delaying hadd for a pregnant woman, distinguishing between flogging and stoning, and between proof by bayyinah and iqrar.

Core Idea
A pregnant woman's hadd is delayed to protect the child; flogging waits until after nifas, and stoning may be further delayed until weaning.

When a pregnant woman commits zina, her hadd punishment is not executed immediately because the well-being of the unborn child takes precedence. The Hanafi school provides detailed rules on how long to delay, depending on whether the punishment is flogging or stoning, and whether the proof is by witnesses or confession. This lesson unpacks those rules, including an intra-madhhab difference from Imam Abu Hanifa.

Guided Path
1
Identify the General Principle
State the overarching rule: the hadd is delayed until after delivery to prevent harm to the child, which is a protected soul.
The child's life is paramount; the delay is wajib.
2
Distinguish Flogging vs Stoning
For flogging (jald), delay continues until the woman recovers from postpartum bleeding (nifas), as nifas is a type of illness. For stoning (rajm), the delay ends at delivery because the child is already born.
The rationale differs: for flogging, nifas is an impediment; for stoning, only the child's presence.
3
Note Abu Hanifa's Additional Delay
Imam Abu Hanifa holds that even stoning should be further delayed until the child is weaned and no longer needs the mother, if no one else can care for it. This is based on the hadith of al-Ghamidiyyah.
This is an intra-Hanafi difference; the general position is after delivery.
4
Procedural Distinction: Imprisonment
If the hadd is proven by bayyinah (two male witnesses), the woman is imprisoned until delivery to prevent flight. If proven by iqrar (confession), she is not imprisoned because she may retract her confession.
Retraction nullifies the hadd, so imprisonment would be futile.
5
Connect to the Dalil
The ruling for flogging delay is based on analogy (qiyas) to illness. The stoning delay after weaning is supported by the Prophetic command to al-Ghamidiyyah: 'Go back until your child becomes independent.'
The hadith is a direct proof for Abu Hanifa's position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَإِذَا زَنَتِ الْحَامِلُ لَمْ تُحَدَّ حَتَّى تَضَعَ حَمْلَهَا كَيْلَا يُؤَدِّيَ إِلَى هَلَاكِ الْوَلَدِ وَهُوَ نَفْسٌ مُحْتَرَمَةٌ. وَإِنْ كَانَ حَدُّهَا الْجَلْدَ لَمْ تُجْلَدْ حَتَّى تَتَعَالَى مِنْ نِفَاسِهَا أَيْ تَرْتَفِعَ يُرِيدُ بِهِ تَخْرُجَ مِنْهُ، لِأَنَّ النِّفَاسَ نَوْعُ مَرَضٍ فَيُؤَخَّرُ إِلَى زَمَانِ الْبُرْءِ، بِخِلَافِ الرَّجْمِ لِأَنَّ التَّأْخِيرَ لِأَجْلِ الْوَلَدِ وَقَدِ انْفَصَلَ. وَعَنْ أَبِي حَنِيفَةَ أَنَّهُ يُؤَخَّرُ إِلَى أَنْ يَسْتَغْنِيَ وَلَدُهَا عَنْهَا إِذَا لَمْ يَكُنْ أَحَدٌ يَقُومُ بِتَرْبِيَتِهِ، لِأَنَّ فِي التَّأْخِيرِ صِيَانَةَ الْوَلَدِ عَنِ الضَّيَاعِ. وَقَدْ رُوِيَ أَنَّهُ عَلَيْهِ السَّلَامُ قَالَ لِلْغَامِدِيَّةِ بَعْدَ مَا رَضَعَتْ: «ارْجِعِي حَتَّى يَسْتَغْنِيَ وَلَدُكِ». ثُمَّ الْحُبْلَى تُحْبَسُ إِلَى أَنْ تَلِدَ إِنْ كَانَ الْحَدُّ ثَابِتًا بِالْبَيِّنَةِ كَيْلَا تَهْرُبَ، بِخِلَافِ الْإِقْرَارِ لِأَنَّ الرُّجُوعَ عَنْهُ عَامِلٌ فَلَا يُفِيدُ الْحَبْسَ. وَاللَّهُ أَعْلَمُ.
And if a pregnant woman fornicates, she is not punished with hadd until she delivers her child, so that it does not lead to the destruction of the child, which is a protected soul. And if her prescribed punishment is flogging, she is not flogged until she recovers from her postpartum bleeding (nifas), i.e., she is purified, meaning she comes out of it, because postpartum bleeding is a type of illness, so it is delayed until the time of recovery. Unlike stoning, because the delay was for the sake of the child, and it has separated. And from Abu Hanifa: it is delayed until her child becomes independent of her, if there is no one to take care of his upbringing, because in the delay is preservation of the child from loss. And it has been narrated that he (peace be upon him) said to al-Ghamidiyyah after she had weaned: 'Go back until your child becomes independent.' Then the pregnant woman is imprisoned until she gives birth if the hadd is established by evidence (bayyinah), lest she flee; contrary to confession (iqrar), because retraction of it is operative, so imprisonment is not beneficial. And Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 151

Commentary
This passage deals with the application of hadd to a pregnant woman who commits zina. The general principle is that the hadd is delayed until after delivery to protect the child's life. If the hadd is flogging (jald), it is further delayed until the woman is purified from postpartum bleeding (nifas), because nifas is considered a type of illness and flogging during it could cause harm. In contrast, if the hadd is stoning (rajm), the ruling differs: since the child is already born, the delay for the child's safety is no longer necessary, so the punishment is carried out after delivery. However, Imam Abu Hanifa has a specific view that even stoning should be delayed until the child no longer needs the mother's care (i.e., is weaned or independent), provided there is no one else to care for the child. This is based on a hadith where the Prophet (peace be upon him) told the Ghamidiyyah woman to return after weaning her child. Additionally, the text addresses the issue of imprisonment before delivery: if the hadd is proven by two witnesses (bayyinah), the woman is imprisoned to prevent her from fleeing; but if it is proven by her own confession (iqrar), she is not imprisoned because she can retract her confession at any time before execution, and imprisonment would be futile. This demonstrates a nuanced application of procedural safeguards in hadd cases.

Key Terms
حَامِل (haamil): A pregnant woman who has committed zina and is subject to hadd.
نِفاس (nifas): Postpartum bleeding; considered a form of illness, so flogging is delayed until it ceases.
غَامِدِيَّة (Ghaamidiyyah): A woman from the tribe of Ghamid who confessed to zina and was told by the Prophet to return after weaning her child.
بَيِّنَة (bayyinah): Evidence, specifically testimony of two male witnesses or one male and two females.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if a pregnant woman confesses to zina (iqrar) and is subject to stoning, is she imprisoned before delivery?
A) Yes, she is imprisoned to prevent escape.
B) No, because she may retract her confession, making imprisonment pointless.
C) Yes, but only if the child is not harmed.
D) No, because confession is not a valid proof in hadd cases.

Key Takeaway
Hadd for a pregnant woman is delayed to protect the child; flogging waits until after nifas, stoning after delivery, and Abu Hanifa further delays stoning until weaning if the child has no caregiver.

Hanafi Ruling on Flogging and Exile for Unmarried Fornicator
States Hanafi position on not combining flogging and exile for unmarried fornicator

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on the prescribed hadd punishment for an unmarried fornicator, explain why exile is not included as part of the hadd, and contrast it with the Shafi'i position while identifying the practical difference (thamarah al-khilaf).

Core Idea
The Hanafi hadd for an unmarried fornicator is flogging only; exile is discretionary, not obligatory.

This passage from al-Hidayah addresses a key difference between the Hanafi and Shafi'i schools regarding the punishment for unmarried fornicators. While the Shafi'i school considers both flogging and exile as part of the fixed hadd, the Hanafi school limits the hadd to flogging and treats exile as a discretionary policy (siyasa) left to the imam's judgment. Understanding this distinction is crucial for correctly applying hudud punishments in the Hanafi tradition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling level
The act is zina (fornication) by an unmarried person (al-bikr). The Hanafi school classifies the hadd punishment as wajib (obligatory).
Hadd punishments are fixed by divine law; their application is mandatory upon proof.
2
Locate the Hanafi position and its basis
The Hanafi position is that the hadd consists only of flogging (100 lashes). Exile is not part of the hadd but may be applied as siyasa (discretionary policy). The basis is the interpretation of the hadith: 'al-bikr...' understood as flogging only.
The full hadith is commonly: 'The unmarried (fornicator) is flogged and exiled for one year.' Hanafis interpret the exile portion as either abrogated or as recommending discretionary punishment.
3
Identify the opposing school and its basis
Imam al-Shafi'i holds that both flogging and exile are part of the prescribed hadd, based on the same hadith but interpreting the imperative as including both.
The Shafi'i school considers the hadith to be explicit about both punishments.
4
Analyze the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf)
In the Hanafi school, a judge cannot order exile as a hadd punishment; if the imam deems it beneficial, he may order exile as siyasa. In the Shafi'i school, exile is mandatory as part of the hadd.
This means the judge's authority in the Hanafi school is more flexible regarding exile.
5
Determine the mufta bihi and its authority
The relied-upon (mufta bihi) position in the Hanafi school is flogging only, with exile discretionary. This is from the zahir al-riwayah and affirmed by al-Marghinani in al-Hidayah.
The authority is strong (zahir al-riwayah) from the core Hanafi texts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال ولا يجمع في البكر بين الجلد والنفي والشافعي يجمع بينهما حد القوله عليه السلام البكر .... الخ یعنی غیر شادی شدہ زانی کو ایک سو کوڑے مارے جائیں گے لیکن ایک سال جلا وطن نہ کریں گے لیکن اگر امام اس میں مصلحت سمجھے تو سیاستا اس کو جلا وطن کر سکتا ہے امام شافعی کے ہاں جلا وطن کرنا بھی سزا کا حصہ ہے۔
He said: It is not combined for the unmarried (fornicator) between flogging and exile, and al-Shafi'i combines them for him based on his (peace be upon him) statement: 'The unmarried...' etc. That is, an unmarried fornicator will be given one hundred lashes but will not be exiled for one year. However, if the leader deems it in the public interest, he may exile him as a policy. According to Imam al-Shafi'i, exile is part of the punishment.
Source pages p. 151

Commentary
This is an inter-madhhab dispute. The Hanafi position, as stated by the author, is that flogging and exile are not combined as hadd punishments for the unmarried fornicator. The punishment is exclusively one hundred lashes. Exile is not a prescribed hadd; rather, it is a matter of state policy (siyasa) left to the imam's discretion if he sees benefit. Imam al-Shafi'i, on the other hand, considers both flogging and one year of exile as part of the hadd punishment, citing the hadith 'The unmarried (fornicator) is flogged and exiled for one year.' The Hanafi school interprets this hadith as referring to a discretionary punishment (ta'zir) or as abrogated, but the text here simply states the position without the full rebuttal.

Key Terms
البكر (al-bikr): An unmarried person who has never engaged in valid sexual intercourse (i.e., virgin/unmarried).
الجلد (al-jald): Flogging, specifically one hundred lashes as the hadd punishment for unmarried fornicators.
النفي (al-nafy): Exile, banishment from one's residence for one year.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the Hanafi school's position on combining flogging and exile for an unmarried fornicator?
A) Both are required as hadd.
B) Flogging is hadd; exile is discretionary.
C) Exile is hadd; flogging is discretionary.
D) Neither is required; it is all ta'zir.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school prescribes flogging as the only fixed hadd for unmarried fornicators; exile, though not part of the hadd, may be imposed by the imam as a discretionary measure for public benefit.

Execution of Hadd on a Sick Person: Stoning vs. Flogging
Ruling on executing hadd on a sick person

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on executing hadd upon a sick person and distinguish between punishments aiming at destruction versus deterrence.

Core Idea
Stoning aims at death, so illness is no barrier; flogging aims at deterrence, so it is delayed to prevent accidental death.

The hadd penalties are obligatory, but their execution must align with the objective of the punishment. This passage from Al-Hidayah clarifies how a sick person's physical condition affects the timing of two major hudud: stoning (rajm) and flogging (jald). By understanding the underlying rationale, you will see why stoning is immediate while flogging is postponed—and how the same logic applies to amputation in extreme weather.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two hadd punishments and their objectives
Note that stoning (rajm) is for the married fornicator and its objective is destruction (itlaf) of the criminal. Flogging is for the unmarried fornicator and its objective is deterrence (zajr). The distinction is key.
2
Determine the ruling for each when the person is sick
The passage states: if the sick person's hadd is stoning, he is stoned immediately; if it is flogging, he is not flogged until he recovers. Extract these two rulings from the text.
3
Extract the legal principle (illa) that determines timing
The illa (effective cause) is the objective of the punishment. For stoning, destroying life is the intended outcome, so illness does not prevent it. For flogging, deterrence is intended, not death; thus flogging is delayed to avoid the unintended consequence of death.
This is a clear example of talil (reasoning based on maqasid).
4
Apply the principle to the analogous case of amputation
The same illa—fear of causing death due to the condition—applies to amputation of a thief's hand during extreme heat or cold. Amputation is delayed until moderate weather because the extreme condition could lead to death, which is not the objective.
This is a qiyas jaliy (clear analogy) based on the shared illa of preventing unintended death.
5
Conclude with the mufta bihi position
The relied-upon position in the Hanafi school is: stoning is carried out immediately even if the person is sick; flogging is delayed until recovery. Amputation is delayed during extreme weather. All four Imams agree on the stoning ruling, while the flogging delay is Hanafi.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَإِذَا زَنَى الْمَرِيضُ وَحَدَّهُ الرَّجمُ رُجِمَ لان الإتلاف مُسْتَحِقٌ فَلَا يَمْتَنِعُ بِسَبَبِ الْمَرَضِ وَإِنْ كَانَ حَدَّهُ لِاَنَّ الْجَلْدُلَمْ يُجْلَدَ حَتَّى يَبْرَأَ كَيْلَا يُفْضِي إِلَى الْهَلَاكِ وَلِهَذَا لَا يُقَامُ الْقَطْعُ عِنْدَ شِدَّةِ الْحَرِّ وَ الْبَرْدِ ترجمہ اور اگر کسی اپنے بیمار نے زنا کر لیا جس کی سزا سنگسار کر دینے کی ہو تو اسے بھی سنگسار کر دیا جائے۔ (اس مسئلہ میں چاروں ائمہ کا اتفاق ہے) کیونکہ اسے تو ہلاک کر دینا ہی مقصود ہے۔ اس لئے اس کی بیماری پر اسے روکا نہیں جائے گا اور اگر اس کی سزا کوڑے مارنے کی ہو تو اس کے تندرست ہو جانے تک اسے کوڑے نہیں مارے جائیں گے۔ تاکہ کوڑے مارے جانے سے وہ ہلاک نہ ہو جائے اسی وجہ سے سردی اور گرمی کی زیادتی کے دنوں میں چور کا ہاتھ نہیں کاٹا جاتا۔ تشریح.. واذا زنى المريض وحده الرجم رجم لان الاتلاف مستحق فلا يمتنع بسبب ... الخ حاصل یہ کہ سنگسار میں ہلاکت فلايمتنع کرنا مقصود ہوتا ہے اسلئے حالت مرض میں سنگسار کیا جائے گا البتہ کوڑے مارنے میں زجر مقصود ہوتا ہے اگر حالت مرض میں کوڑے ماریں گے تو یہ مرض ہلاک ہو جائے گا حالانکہ اس کی ہلاکت مقصود نہیں ہے بلکہ باز رکھنا مقصود ہے۔
And when a sick person commits zina and his punishment is stoning, he is stoned. Because destruction is deserved and is not prevented due to illness. And if his punishment is flogging, he is not flogged until he recovers, so that it does not lead to his destruction. And for this reason, amputation is not carried out during extreme heat or cold. Translation: And if a sick person commits zina and his punishment is stoning, he is stoned. (All four Imams agree on this issue.) Because the purpose is to put him to death. Therefore, it is not prevented due to his illness. And if his punishment is flogging, then he is not flogged until he becomes healthy, lest flogging cause his death. Similarly, a thief's hand is not amputated during extreme cold or heat. Explanation: The summary is that in stoning, destruction is intended, so it is carried out despite illness. In flogging, deterrence is intended; if flogged during illness, he would die, but his death is not intended; rather, prevention is intended.
Source pages p. 151

Commentary
This passage provides a ruling on executing hadd upon a sick person. It distinguishes between two types of hadd: those that aim at destruction (istilaf) and those that aim at deterrence (zajr). Stoning the married fornicator (rajm) is intended to end life, so it is carried out immediately even if the person is ill, because the objective is not delayed. Flogging, however, is meant to deter, not kill, so it is postponed until the person recovers, to avoid causing death inadvertently. The same principle applies to the amputation of a thief's hand: it is not performed during extreme weather conditions that could exacerbate the wound and lead to death. This ruling is agreed upon by all four Imams for stoning, but for flogging, the delay is Hanafi.

Key Terms
الرجم (al-rajm): Execution by stoning; the hadd punishment for a married fornicator (muhsan).
الإتلاف (al-itlaf): Destruction; the objective of stoning is to end the life of the criminal.
الزجر (az-zajr): Deterrence; the objective of flogging is to prevent future sin, not to kill.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is stoning carried out immediately on a sick person while flogging is delayed?
A) Stoning is a lighter punishment than flogging.
B) Stoning aims at destruction, which is not prevented by illness.
C) The sick person cannot be flogged because he is too weak.
D) The judge has discretion to delay any hadd.

Key Takeaway
The timing of hadd punishments depends on their objective: stoning (aiming at death) is immediate for a sick person; flogging (aiming at deterrence) is delayed until recovery.

Execution of Hadd on a Pregnant Woman: Timing and Custody
Ruling on delaying hadd for a pregnant woman until after delivery and weaning

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which the execution of hadd for a pregnant woman is delayed, distinguish between flogging and stoning timing, and apply the relied-upon Hanafi position regarding delaying stoning for the child's care.

Core Idea
Hadd for a pregnant woman is delayed to protect the child; flogging is also delayed until after nifas, and stoning may be further delayed if the child needs care.

This passage explores the delicate balance between executing a divine punishment (hadd) and protecting the life and welfare of an innocent child. The Hanafi school provides detailed rulings on when to administer flogging or stoning for a pregnant woman, considering both the unborn child and the newborn's dependency. We will examine the proofs, the intra-Hanafi difference regarding stoning, and the practical application.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Determine what legal act is being discussed (zina) and the default hukm (hadd is wajib). The passage explains that execution is delayed in certain circumstances.
The delay is not a cancellation—it is a temporary suspension for valid reasons.
2
Locate the dalil
Find the evidence cited: the hadith of al-Ghamidiyyah, where the Prophet told her to return after delivery until her child is independent.
This is a sunnah ahad, so its thubut is zanni.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
Analyze how the hadith proves the delay. The command 'Arji‘i hatta yastaghniya waladuki' implies permission or obligation to postpone the punishment for the child's benefit.
Hanafis interpret the command as indicating a required delay to protect the child.
4
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Recognize that Abu Hanifa holds that stoning should be further delayed until weaning if no other caretaker exists, while the Sahibayn may not require this extra delay.
The text endorses Abu Hanifa's view as the soundest and upon which fatwa is given.
5
Evaluate the tarjih
Understand why Abu Hanifa's view is preferred: it prioritizes the child's welfare and is supported by the hadith.
The ruling for flogging includes a separate delay for nifas, based on it being an illness.

نِفَاسِهَا أَى تَرْتَفِعُ يُرِيدُ بِهِ تَخْرُجُ مِنْهُ لِاَنَّ النِّفَاسِ نَوْعُ مَرَضِ فَيُوَخَّرُ إِلَى زَمَانِ الْمُرْءِ بِخِلَافِ الرَّجُمِ لِاَنَّ التَّاخِيرَ لَا جَلِ الْوَلَدِ وَقَدِ انْفَصَلَ بِالْبَيِّنَةِ كَيْلَا تَهْرُبَ بِخِلَافِ الْإِقْرَارِ لِاَنَّ الرُّجُوعَ عَنْهُ عَامِلٌ فَلَا يُفِيدُ الْحَبْسَ وَاللَّهُ أَعْلَمُ ترجمہ اور جب کسی حاملہ نے زنا کیا ہو تو اس کے بچہ پیدا ہو جانے تک اس پر حد جاری نہیں کی جائے گی۔ تا کہ اس کی وجہ سے اس کا بچہ ہلاک نہ ہو جائے ۔ کیونکہ یہ بچہ تو ایک قابل احترام جان ہے اور اگر اس حالہ زانیہ کی سزا کوڑے مارے جانے کی ہو تو عورت کے نفاس سے پاک ہو جانے تک And when a pregnant woman commits zina, she is not punished until she delivers her child, so as not to lead to the destruction of the child, and it is a respected soul. And if her punishment is flogging, she is not flogged until she recovers from her postpartum bleeding, i.e., she emerges from it, because postpartum bleeding is a type of illness, so it is delayed until the time of well-being. Unlike stoning, because the delay was only for the sake of the child, and it has separated. And from Abu Hanifa that it is delayed until her child becomes independent of her, if there is no one to undertake his upbringing, because in the delay is protection of the child from being lost. And it has been narrated that he (peace be upon him) said to al-Ghamidiyyah after she gave birth: 'Go back until your child becomes independent.' Then the pregnant woman is imprisoned until she gives birth, if the hadd is established by evidence, so that she does not flee, unlike confession, because retraction from it is operative, so imprisonment is not useful. And Allah knows best. Translation: And when a pregnant woman committed zina, the hadd is not executed until she gives birth, so that the child is not killed. Because the child is a respected soul. If her punishment is flogging, she is not flogged until she becomes pure from postpartum bleeding, because postpartum bleeding is a type of illness, so it is delayed until she recovers. Unlike stoning, because the delay was only for the child, and it has already been born. And it is narrated from Abu Hanifa that stoning is also delayed until the child no longer needs her breastfeeding/care, provided there is no one else to care for the child, because delaying protects the child from being lost. The hadith mentions that the Prophet said to Ghamidiyyah after delivery: 'Go back until your child becomes independent.' And this is the soundest view and fatwa should be upon it. Then a pregnant woman whose zina is proven by witnesses is imprisoned until she gives birth, so she does not flee, unlike confession, because if she retracts her confession, imprisonment is useless.
Source pages p. 151

Commentary
This passage deals with the timing of hadd for a pregnant woman. The ruling distinguishes between stoning and flogging. For flogging, the woman must wait until after delivery and also until she recovers from postpartum bleeding (nifas), because that is considered a type of illness. For stoning, the ruling is more nuanced: the immediate basis for delay is the protection of the unborn child; after delivery, stoning could theoretically be carried out, but Imam Abu Hanifa narrates a view (and it is considered the relied-upon position) that if the woman is the only caretaker of the child, stoning should be delayed until the child is weaned or independent. This is based on the hadith of al-Ghamidiyyah, where the Prophet told her to return after giving birth until the child no longer needed breastfeeding. Additionally, if the hadd is proven by witnesses (bayyinah), the woman is held in custody until delivery to prevent flight. If proven by confession, no custody is needed because she can retract.

Key Terms
الحامل (al-hamil): A pregnant woman who has committed zina.
النفاس (al-nifas): Postpartum bleeding; considered a type of illness delaying flogging.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) She might retract her confession, making imprisonment pointless
C) Imprisonment is part of the hadd
D) She needs to be punished earlier with confession

Key Takeaway
Hadd for a pregnant woman is delayed to protect the child and the woman's health, and the highest Hanafi authority delays stoning further if the child needs care.

Definition of Zina and the Principle of Averting Hadd by Doubt
Defines zina and introduces the principle of averting hadd by shubha.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of zina and explain how shubha (doubt) prevents the hadd punishment.

Core Idea
Zina is defined as vaginal intercourse without ownership or its semblance; hadd is averted by any plausible doubt.

This passage begins by defining the act that incurs the hadd punishment: zina. It then establishes the foundational legal principle from the hadith 'Avert the legal punishments by doubts' that any plausible doubt waives the hadd. Two distinct categories of doubt are introduced: subjective doubt in the act (shubhat al-fi'l) and objective doubt in the subject (shubhat al-mahall). This lesson focuses on the definition and the general principle, setting the stage for the specific cases.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition of zina
Locate the sentence that defines zina. It specifies 'intercourse in the front (vagina) without ownership or a semblance of ownership.' Note that the definition requires actual penile-vaginal penetration and the absence of any valid ownership claim or legal doubt about ownership.
The Hanafi school defines zina narrowly; other schools may include anal intercourse.
2
Extract the hadith principle
Find the hadith 'Avert the legal punishments by doubts.' This is the core legal maxim that hadd is waived in the presence of any plausible doubt. Understand that this principle applies to all hudud, not just zina.
The hadith is known as 'Idra'u al-hudud bi al-shubuhat.'
3
Distinguish the two types of shubha
The passage divides shubha into: (1) shubhat al-fi'l (doubt in the act) – a subjective confusion where the person mistakenly believes the act is permissible; (2) shubhat al-mahall (doubt in the subject) – an objective legal indicator that suggests the woman was lawful, regardless of the person's belief.
Shubhat al-fi'l depends on the individual's state of mind; shubhat al-mahall does not.
4
Understand the consequence on lineage (nasab)
Note the key difference: In shubhat al-mahall, if the man claims the child, lineage is established because the act is not pure zina. In shubhat al-fi'l, lineage is not established even if claimed, because the act is considered pure zina.
This has practical implications for inheritance and parentage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب الوطى الذي يوجب الحد والذي لا يوجبه ترجمہ باب ایسی ویلی کا بیان جو حد کو واجب کرتی ہے اور جو واجب نہیں کرتی ہے۔ زنا کی وطی موجب حد ہے قَالَ الْوَى الْمُوجِبُ لِلْحَدَهُوَ الزَّنَاءُ وَإِنَّهُ فِي عُرْفِ الشَّرْعِ وَاللَّسَانِ وَطُئُ الرَّجُلِ الْمَرْأَةَ فِي الْقُبُلِ فِي غَيْرِ الْمِلْكِ وَشُبْهَةُ الْمِلْكِ لِأَنَّهُ فِعْلَ مَحْظُورٌ وَ الْحُرْمَةُ عَلَى الْإِطْلَاقِ عِنْدَ التَّعِرِى عَنِ الْمِلْكِ وَشُبْهَتِهِ يَوْيَدُ ذَالِكَ قَوْلُهُ عَلَيْهِ السَّلَامُ اِدْرَ والْحُدُودَ بِالشُّبُهَاتِ ثُمَّ الشَّبْهَةُ نَوْعَانِ شُبُهَةٌ فِي الْفِعْلِ وَتُسَمَّى شُبْهَةَ اشْتِبَاهِ وَ شُبُهَةٌ فِي الْمَحَلَّ وَتُسَمَّى شُبْهَةٌ حُكْمِيَّةً فَالْأوْلى تَتَحَقَّقُ فِي حَقِّ مَنِ اشْتَبَهَ عَلَيْهِ لِأَنَّ مَعْنَاهُ أَنْ يَظُنَّ غَيْرَ الدَّلِيلِ دَلِيْلًا وَلَا بُدَّ مِنَ الظَّنِّ لِتَحَقُّقِ الْإِشْتِبَاهِ وَالثَّانِيَةُ تَتَحَقَّقُ لِقِيَامِ الدَّلِيلِ النَّافِى لِلْحُرْمَةِ فِي ذَاتِهِ وَلَا تَتَوَقَّفُ عَلَى ظَةٍ الْجَانِي وَاعْتِقَادِهِ وَالْحَدُّيَسْقُطُ بِالنَّوْعَيْنِ لِإِطْلَاقِ الْحَدِيثِ وَالنَّسْبُ يَثْبُتُ فِي الثَّانِيَةِ إِذَا ادَّعَى الْوَلَدَ وَلَا يَتُتُ فِي الْأوْلى وَإِنِ ادَّعَاهُ لَاتَ الْفِعْلَ تَمَحُضُ زِنَاءٍ فِى الأولى وَإِنَّمَا يَسْقُطُ الْحَدُّلَا مُرِرَاجِعِ إِلَيْهِ وَهُوَ اشْتِبَاهُ الْأَمْرِ عَلَيْهِ وَلَمْ يَتَمَحْضُ فِى الثَّانِيَةِ فَشُبْهَةُ الْفِعْلِ فِى ثَمَانِيَةِ مَوَاضِعَ جَارِيَةُ اَبِيْهِ وَأُمِّهِ وَزَوْجَتُهُ وَالْمُطَلَّقَةُ تَلِفًا وَهِيَ فِي الْعِدَّةِ وَبَائِنَا بِالطَّلَاقِ عَلَى مَالٍ وَهِيَ فِى الْعِدَّةِ وَاُمُّ وَلَدِاَعْتَقَهَا مَوْلَا هَا وَهِيَ فِي الْعِدَّةِ وَجَارِيَةُ الْمَوْلَى فِي حَقِّ الْعَبْدِ وَالْجَارِيَةُ الْمَرْهُوْنَهُ فِي حَقِّ الْمُرْتَهِنِ فِي رَوَايَةِ كِتَابِ الْحُدُودِ فَفِي هَذِهِ الْمَوَاضِعِ لَا حَدَّاذْ قَالَ ظَنَنتُ أَنَّهَا تَحِلُّ لِي وَلَوْ قَالَ عَلِمْتُ أَنَّهَا عَلَى حَرَامَ وَجَبَ الْحَدُّ وَ الشَّبْهَةُ فِي الْمَحَلَّ فِي سِتَّةِ مَوَاضِعَ جَارِيَةً ابْنِهِ وَالْمُطَلَّقَةُ طَلَاقَابَاتِنَا بِالْكِتَايَاتِ وَالْجَارِيَةُ الْمَسِيعَةُ فِى حَقِّ الْبَائِعِ قَبْلَ التَّسْلِيمِ وَالْمَمْهُوْرَةُ فِي حَقِّ الزَّوْجِ
Chapter of intercourse that necessitates hadd and that which does not. Translation: Chapter on the statement of intercourse that makes hadd obligatory and that which does not. Zina's intercourse is the cause of hadd. He said: The intercourse that necessitates hadd is zina. And it is, in the custom of the Law and the tongue, a man's intercourse with a woman in the front (vagina) without ownership or a semblance of ownership. Because it is a prohibited act, and the prohibition is absolute when devoid of ownership or its semblance. This is supported by his (peace be upon him) statement: 'Avert the legal punishments by doubts.' Then doubt is of two types: doubt in the act (shubhat al-fi'l), called doubt of confusion, and doubt in the subject (shubhat al-mahall), called legal doubt. The first type is realized concerning one who is confused, because its meaning is that he considers something other than evidence as evidence, and confusion requires actual mistaken belief. The second type is realized due to the existence of evidence negating the prohibition in its essence, and it does not depend on the criminal's belief or understanding. The hadd falls due to both types, due to the general application of the hadith. Lineage (nasab) is established in the second type if he claims the child, but not in the first type even if he claims it, because the act in the first type is pure zina. Hadd falls only due to consideration of his confusion, whereas in the second type it is not pure zina. The doubt in the act occurs in eight places: his father's slave-girl, his mother's slave-girl, his wife, the divorced woman who has been divorced three times and is in her waiting period, the woman divorced by khul' on payment and is in her waiting period, the umm walad when her master frees her and she is in her waiting period, the master's slave-girl with regard to the slave, and the mortgaged slave-girl with regard to the mortgagee in the narration of the book of Hudud. In these places there is no hadd, because he said, 'I thought that she was lawful for me.' If he said, 'I knew she was unlawful,' the hadd becomes obligatory. The doubt in the subject is in six places: his son's slave-girl, the woman divorced irrevocably by three divorces, and the sold slave-girl with regard to the seller before delivery, and the dowered slave-girl with regard to the husband.
Source pages p. 152

Commentary
This passage defines the intercourse that constitutes zina and incurs the hadd punishment, and then explains the two categories of shubha (doubt) that can prevent the hadd. First, zina is defined specifically as vaginal intercourse (wat' in al-qurb) between a man and a woman without any valid ownership (milk) or semblance of ownership (shubhat milk). The hadith 'Avert legal punishments by doubts' supports the principle that hadd is waived when there is any plausible doubt. Two types of doubt are distinguished: (1) Shubhat al-fi'l (doubt in the act): This arises from the person's own confusion, where he mistakenly believes the act is permissible due to misinterpretation of the law. This doubt is subjective and depends on the individual's state of mind. (2) Shubhat al-mahall (doubt in the subject): This arises from an objective legal indicator (dalil nafi li al-hurma) that suggests the permissibility of the specific woman, regardless of the person's own belief. This doubt is objective. Both types waive the hadd punishment, but they differ in the effect on lineage (nasab): for shubhat al-mahall, if the man claims the child, lineage is established; for shubhat al-fi'l, lineage is not established because the act is considered pure zina. The passage then enumerates eight cases of shubhat al-fi'l (e.g., intercourse with one's father's or mother's slave-girl, etc.) and six cases of shubhat al-mahall (e.g., son's slave-girl, etc.). In all these, hadd is waived if the person claims doubt, unless they admit knowledge of unlawfulness.

Key Terms
الوطء (al-wat'): Intercourse/sexual penetration. In this passage, it specifically means penile-vaginal intercourse.
شبهة (shubhah): A plausible legal doubt that prevents the execution of hadd.
شبهة في الفعل (shubhat al-fi'l): Doubt in the act: a subjective confusion where the person mistakenly believes the act is permissible.
شبهة في المحل (shubhat al-mahall): Doubt in the subject: an objective legal indicator that suggests the woman is lawful, regardless of the person's belief.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the basis for the principle that hadd is waived by doubts?
A) Ijma' of the Companions
B) Qiyas on the law of theft
C) A hadith of the Prophet (peace be upon him)
D) The practice of the Caliphs

Key Takeaway
Zina is defined as vaginal intercourse without ownership or its semblance, and hadd is averted by any plausible doubt, whether subjective (shubhat al-fi'l) or objective (shubhat al-mahall).

Cases of Shubhat al-Fi'l and Shubhat al-Mahall and Their Legal Effects
Enumerates eight cases of shubhat al-fi'l and six of shubhat al-mahall, with legal effects on hadd and lineage.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the classifications of shubha to specific cases and determine the legal effects on hadd and lineage (nasab).

Core Idea
Eight cases of shubhat al-fi'l and six of shubhat al-mahall; lineage is established only in the latter.

After defining the two types of shubha, the passage enumerates concrete cases where these doubts apply. For shubhat al-fi'l, there are eight scenarios—such as intercourse with one's father's slave-girl or with a wife during iddah—where the hadd is waived if the person claims ignorance. For shubhat al-mahall, there are six scenarios—such as with a son's slave-girl or a sold slave-girl before delivery—where the hadd is waived and additionally, lineage is established if claimed. This lesson examines these cases in detail and their practical implications.

Guided Path
1
List the eight cases of shubhat al-fi'l
Identify each of the eight cases mentioned: father's slave-girl, mother's slave-girl, wife (during iddah?), etc. Note that these involve subjective confusion about the permissibility of the act.
The common thread is that the person might mistakenly think the act is lawful due to a personal misunderstanding.
2
List the six cases of shubhat al-mahall
Identify each of the six cases: son's slave-girl, woman divorced by three talags, sold slave-girl before delivery, etc. These involve objective legal indicators that suggest permissibility.
These cases are based on legal relationships that create a semblance of ownership or permission.
3
Apply the rule for lineage (nasab)
For each case, determine whether lineage is established if a child is claimed. For shubhat al-fi'l, lineage is not established; for shubhat al-mahall, it is established.
This has significant implications for inheritance and familial obligations.
4
Understand the effect of admitting knowledge
The passage notes that if the person says 'I knew it was unlawful,' the hadd is applied. This shows that the doubt must be genuinely held.
This reinforces the subjective nature of shubhat al-fi'l.

النسب (al-nasab): Lineage/paternity.
جارية (jariyah): Slave-girl / female slave.

In the case of a man having intercourse with his son's slave-girl, which type of shubha applies and what is the effect on lineage?
A) Shubhat al-fi'l; lineage not established.

Definition of Zina and the Two Types of Shubha
Explains zina definition and two types of shubha

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define zina in Hanafi fiqh and distinguish between shubha fi al-fi'l and shubha fi al-hukm, explaining how each prevents hadd.

Core Idea
Zina is intercourse without any ownership or semblance of ownership; shubha of either action or ruling drops the hadd.

This passage opens the chapter on intercourse that necessitates hadd and that which does not. The author defines zina as intercourse free from any milk (ownership) or shubhat milk. He then cites the famous hadith 'Avert hadd punishments by shubuhat' and explains that shubha is of two types: one based on personal doubt in the act and the other based on a legal semblance in the ruling. Understanding these categories is essential for determining when hadd applies.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act that triggers hadd
Recognize that the text defines zina as the intercourse that makes hadd obligatory. The definition includes three negatives: absence of milk al-nikah, milk al-yamin, and shubha of either.
Hadd is only for zina without any ownership or semblance.
2
Understand the hadith proof
The hadith 'Idra'u al-hudud bi al-shubuhat' (Avert hadd punishments by doubts/semblances) is cited. The imperative 'idra'u' indicates command, and 'bi al-shubuhat' is the cause – hadd is dropped when shubha exists.
This is a zanni dalil (ahad hadith) but accepted by the school.
3
Distinguish the two types of shubha
The text divides shubha into shubha fi al-fi'l (ambiguity in the act, e.g., mistaking a woman for one's wife) and shubha fi al-hukm (ambiguity in the ruling, e.g., relying on a general evidence that negates absolute prohibition).
Shubha fi al-hukm does not require personal doubt; it is objective.
4
Note the consequence of shubha
Both types drop the hadd punishment, but only shubha fi al-fi'l can establish lineage if claimed. Shubha fi al-hukm does not establish lineage.
This difference is practical: lineage claims are accepted only in the first type.
5
Apply to a simple scenario
If a man has intercourse with a woman in darkness thinking she is his wife, that is shubha fi al-fi'l; no hadd. If a man has intercourse with his son's slave-girl citing the hadith 'You and your wealth belong to your father', that is shubha fi al-hukm; no hadd, but no lineage.
Practice applying the two types to distinguish them.

باب الوطى الذي يوجب الحد والذي لا يوجبه

اشرف الہدایہ شرح اردو ہدایہ جلد ششم قَبْلَ الْقَبْضِ وَالْمُشْتَرَكَةُ بَيْنَهُ وَبَيْنَ غَيْرِهِ وَالْمَرْهُونَةُ فِى حَقِ الْمُرْتَهِنِ فِي رَوَايَةِ كِتَابِ الرَّهْنِ فَفِي هَذِهِ الْمَوَاضِع لَا يَجِبُ الْحَدُّوَإِنْ قَالَ عَلِمْتُ أَنَّهَا عَلَى حَرَامٌ ثُمَّ الشَّبْهَةُ عِنْدَ ابِي حَنِيفَةَ تَثْبُتُ بِالْعَقْدِ وَ إِنْ كَانَ مُتَّفَقًا عَلَى تَحْرِيمِهِ وَهُوَ عَالِمٌ بِهِ وَعِنْدَ الْبَاقِينَ لَا تَثْبُتُ إِذَا عَلِمَ بِتَحْرِيمِهِ وَيَظْهَرُ ذَالِكَ فِي نِكَاحِ الْمَحَارِمِ عَلَى مَا يَأْيَتُكَ إِنْشَاءَ اللَّهُ تَعَالَى إِذَا عَرَفْنَا هَذَا

وَمَنْ طَلَّقَ امْرَأَتَهُ ثَلنَا ثُمَّ وَطِيَهَا فِى الْعِدَّةِ وَقَالَ عَلِمْتُ أَنَّهَا عَلَى حَرَامٌ حُدَّ لِزَوَالِ الْمِلْكِ الْمُحَلَّلِ مِنْ كُلِّ وَجْهِ فَتَكُونُ الشَّبْهَةُ مُنْتَفِيَةٌ وَقَدْ نَطَقَ الْكِتَابُ بِانْتِفَاءِ الْحِلَّ وَعَلَى ذَالِكَ الْإِجْمَاعُ وَلَا يُعْتَبَرُ قَولُ الْمُخَالِفِ فِيهِ لِأَنَّهُ خِلَاتٌ لَا إِخْتِلاتٌ وَلَوْ قَالَ ظَنَنْتُ أَنَّهَا تَحِلُّ لِى لَا يُحَدُّ لأَنَّ الظَّنَّ فِي مَوْضِعِهِ لِاَنَّ اَتَرَ الْمِلْكِ قَائِمٌ فِي حَقِّ النَّسَبِ وَالْحَبْسِ وَالنَّفْقَةِ فَاعْتُبِرَظَنَّهُ فِي إِسْقَاطِ الْحَدِ وَأَمُ الْوَلَدِإِذَا اعْتَقَهَا مَوْلَاهَا وَالْمُخْتَلَعَةُ وَالْمُطَلَّقَةُ عَلَى مَالٍ بِمَنْزِلَةِ الْمُطَلَّقَةِ الثَّالِثِ لِقُبُوتِ الحُرْمَةِ بِالاجماع وَ قِيَامِ بَعْضِ الْآثَارِ فِي الْعِدَّةِ ترجمہ اگر ایک شخص نے اپنی بیوی کو تین طلاقیں دیں پھر عدت ہی میں اس سے وطی کرلی کہ اور یہ کہا کہ میں یہ جانتا تھا کہ یہ مجھ پر حرام ہے تو اس پر حد جاری کی جائے گی (یعنی اگر وہ محصن ہے تو رجم کیا جائے گاور نہ سو کوڑے مارے جائیں گے ) کیونکہ اس مرد کو اسے حلال کرنے کی ملکیت جو نکاح کی وجہ سے تھی وہ مکمل ختم ہو چکی ہے۔ اس لئے کسی طرح کا شبہ باقی نہیں رہا۔ اور قرآن پاک نے اس کے حلال ہونے کی صراحت کے ساتھ انفی
Chapter of intercourse that makes hadd obligatory and that which does not. ... (Urdu commentary follows) ... The author said: The intercourse that makes hadd obligatory is zina, and the shari and linguistic meaning of zina is that a man has intercourse with an unrelated woman in her vagina while she is free from milk (ownership) and shubhat milk. This is supported by the hadith of the Prophet (peace be upon him): 'Avert the hadd punishments by shubuhat (semblances).' Then shubuhat are of two types: shubha in action (shubhat ishtibah) and shubha in ruling (shubha hukmiyyah). ... Then the eight instances of shubha fi al-fi'l: ... Then the six instances of shubha fi al-hukm: ... Then the ruling on the triple-divorced woman: If a man divorces his wife three times then has intercourse with her during the iddah and says 'I knew it was haram for me', he is punished because the ownership that made her halal is completely removed, so no shubha remains. The Quran has declared the negation of halal and there is consensus on that. If he says 'I thought it was halal for me', he is not punished because the traces of ownership remain with respect to lineage, seclusion, and maintenance, so his assumption is considered for dropping the hadd.
Source pages p. 153

Commentary
This passage is a commentary (sharh) that explains the chapter on intercourse that necessitates hadd and that which does not. It begins by defining zina: only intercourse that is free from any ownership (milk) or semblance of ownership (shubhat milk) incurs the hadd punishment. The commentary emphasizes that zina is a prohibited act, and its complete prohibition occurs only when there is no true ownership (through marriage or slave ownership) and no semblance of either. The author then cites the well-known hadith: 'Avert the hadd punishments by shubuhat,' which is the primary proof that shubha prevents hadd. Two types of shubha are distinguished: shubha fi al-fi'l (ambiguity in the act itself) and shubha fi al-hukm (ambiguity in the legal ruling). Shubha fi al-fi'l depends on the actor's personal doubt (e.g., mistaking a woman for his wife in darkness). Shubha fi al-hukm does not depend on personal doubt; it arises when a general legal evidence exists that negates the absolute prohibition of the act (e.g., the hadith 'You and your wealth belong to your father,' which creates an ambiguity about the prohibition of intercourse with a son's slave-girl). Both types drop the hadd, but only shubha fi al-fi'l establishes lineage if claimed. The commentary then lists eight specific situations where shubha fi al-fi'l applies: (1) intercourse with one's father's slave-girl, (2) mother's slave-girl, (3) wife's slave-girl, (4) triple-divorced wife during iddah, (5) wife divorced for compensation, (6) umm walad after emancipation, (7) slave's intercourse with master's slave-girl, (8) intercourse with a pawned slave-girl (according to Kitab al-Hudud riwaya, which is the relied-upon one; in Kitab al-Rahn riwaya, hadd is not applied absolutely). In these eight, if the person claims he thought it halal, hadd is not applied; if he admits knowing it was haram, hadd is applied. Then six situations are given for shubha fi al-hukm: (1) son's slave-girl, (2) wife divorced with kinayah and during iddah, (3) selling one's slave-girl before delivery to buyer, (4) giving slave-girl as mahr before wife takes possession, (5) jointly owned slave-girl, (6) pawned slave-girl (according to Kitab al-Rahn riwaya). In these, hadd is not applied even if the person knew it was haram. The commentary notes that Imam Abu Hanifa holds that a contract (e.g., a defective marriage contract) establishes shubha even if all scholars agree it is haram and the person knows it, whereas the two Sahibayn hold that shubha is not established when the person knows the prohibition. This difference appears in cases of permanently prohibited women. Finally, the specific case of a triple-divorced woman during iddah is explained: if the man knew the prohibition, hadd is applied because all ownership is gone; if he thought it was halal due to remaining effects (lineage, seclusion, maintenance), then hadd is dropped.

Key Terms
زنا (zina): Intercourse between a man and a woman that is free from milk (ownership through marriage or slave ownership) and shubhat milk (semblance of such ownership).
شبہ (shubha): An ambiguity or semblance that prevents the hadd punishment; divided into shubha fi al-fi'l (personal doubt) and shubha fi al-hukm (legal ambiguity).
حد (hadd): Fixed punishment prescribed by Shari'ah for certain crimes, specifically here the punishment for zina (stoning or flogging).
ملک (milk): Ownership that makes intercourse halal: either milk al-nikah (marriage) or milk al-yamin (slave ownership).
عدت (iddah): Waiting period after divorce or death during which the woman cannot remarry; the text discusses intercourse during iddah after a triple divorce.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is an example of shubha fi al-hukm?
A) Mistaking a woman for one's wife in the dark
B) Having intercourse with a son's slave-girl citing the hadith 'You and your wealth belong to your father'
C) Having intercourse with a woman during her menses
D) Marrying a woman with a defective contract and knowing it is invalid

Key Takeaway
The definition of zina excludes any ownership or shubha; the two types of shubha provide the framework for when hadd is applied.

Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Contractual Shubha and the Triple Divorce Case
Intra-Hanafi dispute on contractual shubha and application to triple divorce

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the difference between Imam Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn regarding shubha established by a known haram contract, and apply the ruling on triple divorce during iddah as an example.

Core Idea
Imam Abu Hanifa holds that a contract creates shubha even if the prohibition is known; the Sahibayn require actual doubt.

After listing the instances of shubha, the passage turns to a fundamental disagreement within the Hanafi school: does a contract (such as a marriage to a mahram) create shubha even when the parties know it is haram? Imam Abu Hanifa says yes; Abu Yusuf and Muhammad say no. This difference has practical consequences in cases like marriage to permanently prohibited women. The passage then examines the specific case of triple divorce during iddah to illustrate the application of these principles.

Guided Path
1
Identify the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Recognize that the passage presents a difference between Imam Abu Hanifa and the two Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) regarding shubha established by a contract.
The issue: does a contract (e.g., a marriage with a mahram) create shubha even if the person knows the prohibition?
2
Understand Abu Hanifa's position
Imam Abu Hanifa holds that the contract itself is a legal cause (sabab) that creates shubha, regardless of the person's knowledge of its prohibition.
His dalil is the general application of 'Idra'u al-hudud bi al-shubuhat'; the contract provides a semblance of legality.
3
Understand the Sahibayn's position
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad hold that shubha is not established when the person knows the prohibition; knowledge negates the ambiguity.
They require genuine doubt based on the evidence.
4
Analyze the triple divorce case
The passage then gives a specific case: a man divorces his wife three times and then has intercourse during iddah. If he knew it was haram, hadd applies; if he thought it was halal (due to remaining traces of marriage like lineage, seclusion, maintenance), then shubha is present and hadd is dropped.
This illustrates Abu Hanifa's principle: the remaining effects create a semblance that can excuse the person.
5
Evaluate the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference appears in cases like marriage to a mahram: under Abu Hanifa, if the person claims he thought it was permissible due to the contract, hadd is dropped; under the Sahibayn, hadd is applied if he knew the prohibition.
The triple divorce case shows that the mufta bihi follows Abu Hanifa's broader view of shubha.

وَمَنْ طَلَّقَ امْرَأَتَهُ ثَلنَا ثُمَّ وَطِيَهَا فِى الْعِدَّةِ وَقَالَ عَلِمْتُ أَنَّهَا عَلَى حَرَامٌ حُدَّ لِزَوَالِ الْمِلْكِ الْمُحَلَّلِ مِنْ كُلِّ وَجْهِ فَتَكُونُ الشَّبْهَةُ مُنْتَفِيَةٌ وَقَدْ نَطَقَ الْكِتَابُ بِانْتِفَاءِ الْحِلَّ وَعَلَى ذَالِكَ الْإِجْمَاعُ وَلَا يُعْتَبَرُ قَولُ الْمُخَالِفِ فِيهِ لِأَنَّهُ خِلَاتٌ لَا إِخْتِلاتٌ وَلَوْ قَالَ ظَنَنْتُ أَنَّهَا تَحِلُّ لِى لَا يُحَدُّ لأَنَّ الظَّنَّ فِي مَوْضِعِهِ لِاَنَّ اَتَرَ الْمِلْكِ قَائِمٌ فِي حَقِّ النَّسَبِ وَالْحَبْسِ وَالنَّفْقَةِ فَاعْتُبِرَظَنَّهُ فِي إِسْقَاطِ الْحَدِ وَأَمُ الْوَلَدِإِذَا اعْتَقَهَا مَوْلَاهَا وَالْمُخْتَلَعَةُ وَالْمُطَلَّقَةُ عَلَى مَالٍ بِمَنْزِلَةِ الْمُطَلَّقَةِ الثَّالِثِ لِقُبُوتِ الحُرْمَةِ بِالاجماع وَ قِيَامِ بَعْضِ الْآثَارِ فِي الْعِدَّةِ ترجمہ اگر ایک شخص نے اپنی بیوی کو تین طلاقیں دیں پھر عدت ہی میں اس سے وطی کرلی کہ اور یہ کہا کہ میں یہ جانتا تھا کہ یہ مجھ پر حرام ہے تو اس پر حد جاری کی جائے گی (یعنی اگر وہ محصن ہے تو رجم کیا جائے گاور نہ سو کوڑے مارے جائیں گے ) کیونکہ اس مرد کو اسے حلال کرنے کی ملکیت جو نکاح کی وجہ سے تھی وہ مکمل ختم ہو چکی ہے۔ اس لئے کسی طرح کا شبہ باقی نہیں رہا۔ اور قرآن پاک نے اس کے حلال ہونے کی صراحت کے ساتھ انفی
Chapter of intercourse that makes hadd obligatory and that which does not. ... (Urdu commentary follows) ... The author said: The intercourse that makes hadd obligatory is zina, and the shari and linguistic meaning of zina is that a man has intercourse with an unrelated woman in her vagina while she is free from milk (ownership) and shubhat milk. This is supported by the hadith of the Prophet (peace be upon him): 'Avert the hadd punishments by shubuhat (semblances).' Then shubuhat are of two types: shubha in action (shubhat ishtibah) and shubha in ruling (shubha hukmiyyah). ... Then the eight instances of shubha fi al-fi'l: ... Then the six instances of shubha fi al-hukm: ... Then the ruling on the triple-divorced woman: If a man divorces his wife three times then has intercourse with her during the iddah and says 'I knew it was haram for me', he is punished because the ownership that made her halal is completely removed, so no shubha remains. The Quran has declared the negation of halal and there is consensus on that. If he says 'I thought it was halal for me', he is not punished because the traces of ownership remain with respect to lineage, seclusion, and maintenance, so his assumption is considered for dropping the hadd.
Source pages p. 153

Commentary
This passage is a commentary (sharh) that explains the chapter on intercourse that necessitates hadd and that which does not. It begins by defining zina: only intercourse that is free from any ownership (milk) or semblance of ownership (shubhat milk) incurs the hadd punishment. The commentary emphasizes that zina is a prohibited act, and its complete prohibition occurs only when there is no true ownership (through marriage or slave ownership) and no semblance of either. The author then cites the well-known hadith: 'Avert the hadd punishments by shubuhat,' which is the primary proof that shubha prevents hadd. Two types of shubha are distinguished: shubha fi al-fi'l (ambiguity in the act itself) and shubha fi al-hukm (ambiguity in the legal ruling). Shubha fi al-fi'l depends on the actor's personal doubt (e.g., mistaking a woman for his wife in darkness). Shubha fi al-hukm does not depend on personal doubt; it arises when a general legal evidence exists that negates the absolute prohibition of the act (e.g., the hadith 'You and your wealth belong to your father,' which creates an ambiguity about the prohibition of intercourse with a son's slave-girl). Both types drop the hadd, but only shubha fi al-fi'l establishes lineage if claimed. The commentary then lists eight specific situations where shubha fi al-fi'l applies: (1) intercourse with one's father's slave-girl, (2) mother's slave-girl, (3) wife's slave-girl, (4) triple-divorced wife during iddah, (5) wife divorced for compensation, (6) umm walad after emancipation, (7) slave's intercourse with master's slave-girl, (8) intercourse with a pawned slave-girl (according to Kitab al-Hudud riwaya, which is the relied-upon one; in Kitab al-Rahn riwaya, hadd is not applied absolutely). In these eight, if the person claims he thought it halal, hadd is not applied; if he admits knowing it was haram, hadd is applied. Then six situations are given for shubha fi al-hukm: (1) son's slave-girl, (2) wife divorced with kinayah and during iddah, (3) selling one's slave-girl before delivery to buyer, (4) giving slave-girl as mahr before wife takes possession, (5) jointly owned slave-girl, (6) pawned slave-girl (according to Kitab al-Rahn riwaya). In these, hadd is not applied even if the person knew it was haram. The commentary notes that Imam Abu Hanifa holds that a contract (e.g., a defective marriage contract) establishes shubha even if all scholars agree it is haram and the person knows it, whereas the two Sahibayn hold that shubha is not established when the person knows the prohibition. This difference appears in cases of permanently prohibited women. Finally, the specific case of a triple-divorced woman during iddah is explained: if the man knew the prohibition, hadd is applied because all ownership is gone; if he thought it was halal due to remaining effects (lineage, seclusion, maintenance), then hadd is dropped.

Key Terms
شبہ (shubha): An ambiguity or semblance that prevents the hadd punishment; here specifically contractual shubha.
حد (hadd): Fixed punishment for zina (stoning or flogging).
عدت (iddah): Waiting period after divorce; the passage discusses triple divorce iddah.
عقد (aqd): Contract; in this context, a marriage contract.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the triple divorce case in the passage, when is hadd applied?
A) Always, because triple divorce completely severs marriage.
B) Only if the man admits he knew it was haram.
C) Never, because shubha is always present.
D) It depends on whether he claims he thought it was halal.

Key Takeaway
Intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on contractual shubha directly impacts hadd application; the triple divorce case illustrates how remaining traces of marriage can create shubha.

Hudud — Fixed Penalties
intermediate
6 min
Definition of Zinā and the Two Types of Shubha
Defining zinā and explaining shubha types that avert ḥadd

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of zinā and distinguish between shubha fi al-fi'l and shubha fi al-kull, applying them to the six listed scenarios.

Core Idea
Zinā is intercourse that incurs ḥadd; shubha in the act or the essence removes that punishment.

This passage opens by defining zinā strictly as intercourse that triggers the legal fixed punishment (ḥadd). It then introduces the critical concept of shubha (legal doubt) which, when present, averts ḥadd. The author lists six specific positions where shubha applies and explains that they fall into two categories: shubha in the act itself (shubha fi al-fi'l) and shubha in the essence of ownership or marriage (shubha fi al-kull). Understanding this distinction is essential for correctly applying ḥudūd in borderline cases.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal definition
Find the sentence that defines zinā. It says: every intercourse that necessitates the legal hadd is called zinā. Write that definition in your own words.
The definition is operational: it ties the label to the legal consequence.
2
Recognize the role of shubha
Notice that if any shubha is present (ownership or marriage doubt), the intercourse is not zinā and ḥadd is not applied. Shubha acts as a legal barrier.
Shubha is not subjective doubt; it is a legally recognized semblance of right.
3
Categorize the shubha types
The text mentions two types: shubha fi al-fi'l (doubt in the act itself) and shubha fi al-kull (doubt in the essence). For each of the six positions, decide which type applies.
Example: intercourse with a son's slave-girl involves shubha fi al-kull (ownership doubt), while intercourse after a kinayah divorce involves shubha fi al-fi'l (act might still be permissible).
4
Note the additional shubha type
The commentary points out that Imam Abu Hanifah adds a third type: shubha fi al-i'tiqad (doubt arising from mistaken belief). The author of Hidayah did not treat it separately here.
This will be relevant when we study the dispute about contract shubha.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

اگر ولی کرنے والے نے یہ گوئی کیا کہ میں نے اس باندی کو اپنے لئے حلال سمجھا تھاتو اس پر د جاری نہیں ہوگی اور اگر یہ کہا کہ میں یہ جانتا تھا کہ یہ مجھ پر حرام ہے تو حد واجب ہو جائے گی اور محل میں شبہ کے مواقع چھ ہی ہیں، اول یہ کہ اپنے بیٹے کی باندی سے دلی ۔ دوم یہ کہ کنایہ سے طلاق بائن دینے کے بعد ہی بیوی سے ولی کی۔ سوم یہ کہ بالغ نے اپنی باندی کو فروخت کرنے کے بعد خریدار کے حوالہ کرنے سے پہلے اس سے وطی کرلی۔ چہارم یہ کہ شوہرنے اپنی باندی کو اپنی بیوی کے مہر میں دیا لیکن بیوی کے قبضہ کرنے سے پہلے اس سے ولی کر لی۔ پنجم جو باندی دو آدمیوں کے درمیان مشترک تھی ۔ اس سے ایک شخص نے وطی کرنی۔ ششم اس مرتین یعنی جس کے پاس ایک باندی رہن رکھی ہوئی تھی اس نے اس باندی سے ولی کر لی۔ لیکن یہ کتاب الرہن کی روایت کے مطابق ہے کہ ان مواقع میں حد واجب نہیں ہوگی۔ اگر چہ اس نے یہ کہا ہو کہ میں تو یہ جانتا تھا کہ یہ مجھ پر حرام ہے۔ پھر یہ بھی معلوم ہونا چاہیئے کہ امام ابو حنیفہ کے نزدیک عقد کی وجہ سے شبہ ثابت ہو جاتا ہے۔ اگر چہ علماء کے نزدیک وہ بالاتفاق حرام ہو اور وطی کرنے والا خود بھی یہ جانتا ہو۔ لیکن باقی فقہاء کے نزدیک عقد کر لینے سے شبہ ثابت نہیں ہوتا ہے اس صورت میں جب کہ وطی کرنے والے کو یہ بات معلوم ہو کہ یہ عقد نکاح حرام ہے۔ ان فقہاء کے مذکورہ اختلاف کا نتیجہ ایسی عورتوں کے بارے میں ظاہر ہوگا۔ جن سے ہمیشہ کے لئے نکاح کرنا حرام ہے۔ انشاء اللہ اس کی تفصیل آئندہ آئینگی۔ اب جب کہ یہ بات معلوم ہو چکی ہے کہ شبہ کی دو قسمیں ہیں۔ تو ہم آئندہ ان مسائل کی تشریح کریں گے۔ تشریح حاصل یہ کہ اس عبارت میں زنا کی تعریف کی کہ ہر وہ ولی جس سے شرعی حد واجب ہو وہ زنا کہلاتی ہے اور اگر اس دلی میں کسی قسم کا شبہ پایا گیا خواہ وہ ملکیت کا ہو یا نکاح ک تو وہ زنانہ ہو گا یعنی اس پر دز نا واجب نہ ہوگی اور آگے شبہ کی اقسام بیان کی ہیں اور وہ مقامات بھی بتادیئے کہ جن میں شبہ فی الفعل ہے اور جن میں شبہ فی الکل ہے کہ ان دونوں قسم کے شبہ سے حد زنا ساقط ہو جاتی ہے امام ابو حنیفہ کے ہاں شبہ کی ایک مزید تقسم شبہ فی اعتقد ہے۔ صاحب ہدایہ نے اس قسم کو مستقل ذکر نہ فرمایا۔
Ashraf al-Hidayah Urdu commentary of Hidayah Volume 6 ... Chapter of intercourse that necessitates the hadd and that which does not. (And this is the chosen narration. Al-Zayla'i. And in the narration of Kitab al-Rahn, absolutely no hadd.) So in these eight positions, if the one who did intercourse said 'I considered this slave-girl halal for myself', then hadd will not be imposed upon him; and if he said 'I knew that she was haram for me', then hadd becomes obligatory. And the positions of shubha are six: first, with one's son's slave-girl; second, intercourse with his wife after giving a kināyah divorce; third, an adult having intercourse with his slave-girl after selling her before handing over to buyer; fourth, a husband giving his slave-girl as his wife's mahr but having intercourse before the wife takes possession; fifth, a slave-girl shared between two men, one having intercourse; sixth, a mortgagee having intercourse with a mortgaged slave-girl. But according to the narration of Kitab al-Rahn, in these positions hadd will not be obligatory even if he said 'I knew it was haram for me.' Then it should also be known that according to Imam Abu Hanifah, shubha is established due to a contract even if the scholars unanimously consider it haram and the one doing intercourse himself knows it. But according to the other jurists, shubha is not established by a contract when the one doing intercourse knows that this marriage contract is haram. The difference of these jurists will become apparent regarding women whom it is permanently haram to marry. God willing, its detail will come later. Now that it is known that shubha is of two types, we will explain these issues further. Explanation: In this passage, the definition of zinā is that every intercourse that necessitates the legal hadd is called zinā, and if some kind of shubha is found in that intercourse, whether of ownership or of marriage, then it will not be zinā, meaning hadd will not be obligatory. And further the types of shubha have been mentioned: places where shubha fi al-fi'l and shubha fi al-kull. Both types remove the hadd of zinā. According to Imam Abu Hanifah, there is an additional division: shubha fi al-i'tiqad. The author of Hidayah did not mention this type independently.
Source pages p. 154

Commentary
This passage is a definition and introductory ruling. It defines zinā as intercourse that incurs the legal hadd punishment. If any shubha (doubt) is present, whether regarding ownership or the validity of marriage, the hadd is averted. The text lists six specific scenarios where shubha applies (later mentioned as eight in some narrations). The discussion includes a difference within the Hanafi school: according to Imam Abu Hanifah, a contract (even if invalid) creates shubha, whereas other jurists hold that if the person knows the contract is haram, no shubha exists. This distinction is crucial for cases involving women permanently prohibited in marriage. The author further categorizes shubha into shubha fi al-fi'l (doubt in the act itself) and shubha fi al-kull (doubt in the essence), both of which negate hadd. Imam Abu Hanifah adds a third category: shubha fi al-i'tiqad (doubt arising from a mistaken belief), though the author of Hidayah did not treat it separately.

Key Terms
زنا (zinā): Unlawful intercourse that necessitates the legal fixed punishment (ḥadd).
شبهة (shubha): A legal doubt or semblance of right that averts the ḥadd punishment.
شبهة في الفعل (shubha fi al-fi'l): Doubt concerning the nature of the act itself, e.g., whether the sexual act is lawful or not.
شبهة في الكل (shubha fi al-kull): Doubt concerning ownership or marriage tie, i.e., whether the woman was permissible to have intercourse with at all.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, how many positions of shubha are listed that prevent ḥadd?
A) Six
B) Eight
C) Four
D) Ten

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi definition of zinā is tied to the application of ḥadd; any legally recognized shubha (in act, essence, or belief) removes the ḥadd.

The Contract Shubha Dispute: Does Knowledge of Prohibition Remove Shubha?
Dispute over whether a known invalid contract creates shubha

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi and opposing positions on whether a known invalid contract creates shubha, and identify the practical consequence for permanently prohibited marriage partners.

Core Idea
For Abu Hanifah, a contract creates shubha even if one knows it's haram; other jurists require ignorance for shubha to avert ḥadd.

This passage delves into a nuanced ikhtilaf within the Hanafi school and between Hanafis and other fuqaha. Imam Abu Hanifah holds that the mere existence of a contract (even if invalid) generates shubha, and ḥadd is averted regardless of the person's knowledge. The majority of other jurists (including the Sahibayn in some narrations) disagree: if the person knows the contract is haram, no shubha is present and ḥadd applies. The text notes that the chosen narration (al-mukhtar) from al-Zayla'i supports Abu Hanifah's view.

Guided Path
1
State the issue
The central dispute: Does a known invalid contract create shubha that averts ḥadd? Formulate the question precisely.
The context is contracts that are unanimously considered ḥarām, e.g., marriage to a woman permanently prohibited.
2
Identify Imam Abu Hanifah's position
According to the text, Abu Hanifah says shubha is established due to the contract itself, even if the person knows it is ḥarām. The outward form creates the doubt.
The text says: 'عقد کی وجہ سے شبہ ثابت ہو جاتا ہے' (shubha is established due to the contract).
3
Identify the opposing position
The other jurists hold that shubha is not established by a contract when the person knows it is ḥarām. Their reasoning: knowledge of prohibition removes any semblance of right.
This opposes Abu Hanifah's view; they require ignorance or a valid reason to believe the contract is lawful.
4
Trace the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference appears in cases of women permanently prohibited in marriage (e.g., mahrams). Abu Hanifah would not impose ḥadd if a (invalid) contract was made, even if the person knew; others would impose ḥadd.
This is a significant difference affecting the application of ḥudūd.
5
Note the tarjih
The commentary says 'اور یہی مختار روایت ہے الزیلعی' (and this is the chosen narration from al-Zayla'i), indicating preference for the view that shubha exists in the eight positions and ḥadd is averted.
Al-Zayla'i's Tabyīn al-Ḥaqāʾiq is a major Hanafi reference.

Source Text

Commentary
This passage is a definition and introductory ruling. It defines zinā as intercourse that incurs the legal hadd punishment. If any shubha (doubt) is present, whether regarding ownership or the validity of marriage, the hadd is averted. The text lists six specific scenarios where shubha applies (later mentioned as eight in some narrations). The discussion includes a difference within the Hanafi school: according to Imam Abu Hanifah, a contract (even if invalid) creates shubha, whereas other jurists hold that if the person knows the contract is haram, no shubha exists. Key Terms
شبهة العقد (shubhat al-ʿaqd): The legal doubt arising from the existence of a contract, even if invalid.
مختار (mukhtār): The chosen or preferred opinion in the madhhab.

Key Terms from Source Passage

According to the text, what is the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence) of the dispute over contract shubha?
A) It affects rulings on divorce validity.

D) It only applies to mortgaged slave-girls.

Key Takeaway
The dispute over contract shubha hinges on whether the outward form of the contract or the inner knowledge of the individual determines the presence of legal doubt.
