No Ḥadd for Intercourse with Son's Slave-Girl: Shubha Ḥukmiyyah from Ḥadīth
Ruling on ḥadd for intercourse with relatives' slave-girls, with shubha analysis.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why Hanafis negate ḥadd in cases of intercourse with specific relatives' slave-girls, applying the concept of shubha ḥukmiyyah, and distinguish cases where knowledge of prohibition does or does not trigger ḥadd.

Core Idea
A legal doubt (shubha) arising from a prophetic text can negate the ḥadd penalty even when the person knew the act was ḥarām.

This passage from Al-Hidāyah addresses a set of interconnected rulings on intercourse with slave-girls belonging to close relatives or one's own master. The central principle is the role of shubha (legal doubt) in averting the ḥadd punishment. Understanding these cases requires careful attention to how the Ḥanafī school evaluates the strength of the shubha and whether the person's knowledge of prohibition is decisive.

Guided Path
1
Identify the central ruling
The passage states that intercourse with a son's or grandson's slave-girl does not incur ḥadd, even with knowledge. This is the core ruling.
2
Locate the dalīl
The proof is the ḥadīth 'anta wa māluka li-abīka' (you and your wealth belong to your father). This creates a shubha ḥukmiyyah.
3
Apply the concept of shubha
The shubha arises from a textual proof, so it is a legal doubt that prevents ḥadd. Note that the father–grandfather relationship extends the shubha.
4
Contrast with other cases
For father's/mother's/wife's slave-girl, if the person assumed it was ḥalāl, no ḥadd; but if he knew it was ḥarām, ḥadd applies. Why? Because there is no shubha from a text; the assumption is merely personal.
5
Handle the slave case
A slave has a connection (bisāṭ) with his master's property, so his assumption of permissibility is plausible, creating shubha. However, the act is essentially zinā, so the accuser is not flogged.

Source Text

وَلَا حَدَّ عَلَى مَنْ وَطِيَ جَارِيَةَ وَلَدِهِ وَوَلَدِ وَلَدِهِ وَإِنْ قَالَ عَلِمْتُ أَنَّهَا عَلَيَّ حَرَامٌ لِأَنَّ الشَّبْهَةَ حُكْمِيَّةٌ لِأَنَّهَا نَشَأَتْ عَنْ دَلِيلٍ وَهُوَ قَوْلُهُ عَلَيْهِ السَّلَامُ أَنْتَ وَمَالُكَ لِأَبِيكَ وَالْأُبُوَّةُ قَائِمَةٌ فِي حَقِّ الْجِدِّ وَيَثْبُتُ النَّسَبُ مِنْهُ وَعَلَيْهِ قِيمَةُ الْجَارِيَةِ وَقَدْ ذَكَرْنَاهُ وَإِذَا وَطِيَ جَارِيَةَ أَبِيهِ أَوْ أُمِّهِ أَوْ زَوْجَتِهِ وَقَالَ ظَنَنْتُ أَنَّهَا تَحِلُّ لِي فَلَا حَدَّ عَلَيْهِ وَلَا عَلَى قَاذِفِهِ وَإِنْ قَالَ عَلِمْتُ أَنَّهَا عَلَيَّ حَرَامٌ حُدَّ وَكَذَا الْعَبْدُ إِذَا وَطِيَ جَارِيَةَ مَوْلَاهُ لِأَنَّ بَيْنَهُ وَبَيْنَهُ بِسَاطاً فِي الِانْتِفَاعِ فَظَنُّهُ فِي الِاسْتِمْتَاعِ مُحْتَمَلٌ فَكَانَتْ شُبْهَةً إِلَّا أَنَّهُ زِنًاءٌ حَقِيقَةً فَلَا يُحَدُّ قَاذِفُهُ وَكَذَا إِذَا قَالَتِ الْجَارِيَةُ ظَنَنْتُ أَنَّهُ يَحِلُّ لِي وَالْفِعْلُ لَمْ يُدَّعَ فِي الظَّاهِرِ لِأَنَّ الْفِعْلَ وَاحِدٌ ترجمہ: اور اس شخص پر حد لازم نہیں ہوگی جس نے اپنے بیٹے یا پوتے کی باندی سے وطی کر لی ہو۔ اگر چہ اس نے یہ کہا ہو کہ میں جانتا تھا کہ وہ مجھ پر
And there is no ḥadd upon one who has intercourse with the slave-girl of his son or the slave-girl of his son's son, even if he said 'I knew that she was haram for me', because the shubha is ḥukmiyyah (legal) as it arises from a proof, which is his (peace be upon him) statement: 'You and your wealth belong to your father.' And fatherhood exists in the case of the grandfather, and lineage is established from him, and he must pay the value of the slave-girl, as we have mentioned. And if he has intercourse with the slave-girl of his father or his mother or his wife, and he said 'I assumed she was halal for me', then there is no ḥadd upon him and no ḥadd upon the one who accuses him (of zinā). But if he said 'I knew she was haram for me', he is flogged. And likewise the slave if he has intercourse with his master's slave-girl, because there is a connection between them in terms of benefit, so his assumption in seeking pleasure is plausible, thus it is shubha. However, it is essentially zinā, so the one who accuses him (of zinā) is not flogged. And likewise if the slave-girl said 'I assumed it was halal for me' and the act was not claimed (as force?) outwardly, because the act is one. Translation: And there will be no ḥadd upon the person who has intercourse with the slave-girl of his son or grandson, even if he said 'I knew that she was haram for me'. Because the shubha is ḥukmiyyah, arising from a proof, which is the Prophet's saying: 'You and your wealth belong to your father.' And paternity exists regarding the grandfather, and lineage is established from him, and he must pay the value of the slave-girl, as we have mentioned. And if he has intercourse with his father's or mother's or wife's slave-girl, and he said 'I assumed she was halal for me', then there is no ḥadd upon him and no ḥadd upon his accuser. But if he said 'I knew she was haram for me', he is flogged. Similarly, a slave who has intercourse with his master's slave-girl (with assumption) since there is a connection in benefit, his assumption for enjoyment is plausible, so it is shubha. However, it is essentially zinā, so his accuser is not flogged. Similarly, if the slave-girl said 'I assumed it was halal for me' and the act was not outwardly alleged? because the act is one.
Source pages p. 155

Commentary
This passage provides rulings on intercourse with various slave-girls. The first case: a man's intercourse with his son's or grandson's slave-girl does not incur ḥadd, even if he knew the prohibition, due to shubha ḥukmiyyah from the hadith 'anta wa māluka li-abīka' (you and your wealth belong to your father). This legal principle suggests a form of implied ownership. Intercourse with one's own father's, mother's, or wife's slave-girl: if he assumed it was halal, no ḥadd; if he knew it was haram, ḥadd applies. For a slave with his master's slave-girl, if he assumed it permissible due to the closeness of service, it is shubha and no ḥadd, but the act is essentially zinā so the accuser is not flogged. The same applies if the slave-girl assumed it was halal. The key distinction is whether the assumption of permissibility has a legal basis.

Key Terms
شبهة حكمية (shubha ḥukmiyyah): A legal doubt arising from a textual proof (dalil), such as a hadith.
جارية (jāriyah): Female slave, concubine.

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) Be stoned

Key Takeaway
Shubha ḥukmiyyah from a textual proof can negate the ḥadd penalty even when the person knows the act is prohibited, but this is limited to cases where the shubha arises from a recognized dalīl.

No Hadd for Kinayah Divorce Intercourse Due to Sahabi Ikhtilaf
Ruling with proof from sahaba ikhtilaf

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why shubha (doubt) from sahaba ikhtilaf overrides a party's admission of knowledge in hadd cases, and apply the principle 'dara' al-hudud bi al-shubuhat'.

Core Idea
Hadd is averted by doubt – even admission of knowledge cannot cure shubha from valid ikhtilaf.

This lesson examines a nuanced ruling where a man uses a kinayah (allusive) expression of divorce, his wife chooses herself (effecting a divorce), and then he has intercourse with her during the idda period. Even if he admits he knew it was unlawful, no hadd punishment is imposed. The reason lies in the ikhtilaf among the Companions regarding the effect of such kinayah divorces, which creates a shubha (doubt) that blocks the hadd. This illustrates the powerful Hanafi principle that hadd punishments are averted by doubts, regardless of personal admission.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and the admission
The husband uses kinayah words like 'You are free' or 'You are released', and the wife chooses herself. Then he has intercourse with her during the idda. He states he knew she was haram for him. This admission of knowledge is key to understanding why hadd would normally apply.
Kinayah requires intention; here the husband's intention is assumed to be divorce, otherwise the ruling might differ.
2
Recognize the shubha from sahaba ikhtilaf
The Companions differed on the effect of kinayah divorce: Umar (RA) held it is one revocable divorce; others considered it three divorces or an irrevocable divorce. This ikhtilaf creates uncertainty about the legal status of the woman during idda.
Umar's view is adopted by the Hanafi school.
3
Apply the hadd-dropping principle
The Hanafi maxim 'al-hudud tudra'u bi al-shubuhat' (hadd punishments are averted by doubts) applies. Even though the man admitted knowing it was haram, the underlying shubha about the validity of the divorce remains, so hadd is not imposed.
Admission of knowledge does not remove the objective shubha; it only confirms subjective awareness.
4
Extend to all kinayah and intended three
The same ruling applies to all kinayah expressions. Even if the husband intended three divorces by the kinayah words, the ikhtilaf persists, so no hadd. The shubha is based on the nature of the expression, not the intention.
This keeps the ruling consistent across similar expressions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَلَوْ قَالَ لَهَا أَنْتِ خَلِيَّةٌ أَوْ بَرِيَّةٌ أَوْ أَمْرُكِ بِيَدِكِ فَاخْتَارَتْ نَفْسَهَا ثُمَّ وَطِيَهَا فِي الْعِدَّةِ وَقَالَ عَلِمْتُ أَنَّهَا عَلَيَّ حَرَامٌ لَمْ يُحَدَّ لِاخْتِلَافِ الصَّحَابَةِ فِيهِ فَمِنْ مَذْهَبِ عُمَرَ أَنَّهَا تَطْلِيقَةٌ رَجْعِيَّةٌ وَكَذَا الْجَوَابُ فِي سَائِرِ الْكِنَايَاتِ وَكَذَا إِذَا نَوَى ثَلَاثًا لِقِيَامِ الْإِخْتِلَافِ مَعَ ذَلِكَ
And if he said to her 'You are free' or 'You are released' or 'Your affair is in your hand,' and she chose herself, then he had intercourse with her during the idda and said 'I knew that she was unlawful for me,' he is not hadded due to the ikhtilaf of the Companions about it. For from the madhhab of Umar is that it is one revocable divorce. And likewise the answer in all kinayah (words of divorce). And likewise if he intended three, because the ikhtilaf remains with that.
Source pages p. 155

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof from the ikhtilaf of the Companions. It addresses the case of kinayah (allusive) divorces. If a man uses a kinayah expression such as 'You are free' (khaliyya), 'You are released' (bariyya), or 'Your affair is in your hand' (amruki biyadik), and his wife chooses herself (resulting in divorce), then he has intercourse with her during the idda period. Even if he admits that he knew she was unlawful for him, no hadd punishment is imposed. The reason is that the Companions differed on the legal effect of such kinayah divorces: Umar (RA) held that it is a single revocable divorce, while others considered it a final divorce or three divorces. This disagreement creates a shubha (doubt), and hadd punishments are averted by doubts. The same ruling applies to all kinayah expressions, and even if the man intended three divorces (by the kinayah), because the ikhtilaf remains. The Hanafi position, following Umar, considers this shubha sufficient to drop the hadd, prioritizing the principle of 'dara' al-hudud bi al-shubuhat' (avert hadd punishments by doubts).

Key Terms
خلية (khaliyya): Literally 'free'; a kinayah expression for divorce meaning 'you are free from the marriage bond.'
برية (bariyya): Literally 'released'; a kinayah expression for divorce. Similar to khaliyya.
أمرك بيدك (amruki biyadik): An expression meaning 'your affair is in your hand'; when the wife chooses herself, talaq occurs.
كناية (kināyah): An allusive or indirect expression that may imply divorce but requires intention or context to be effective.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because he thought it was halal
B) Because the Sahaba differed on whether such a divorce is revocable or final, creating shubha
C) Because kinayah divorce does not make her haram at all
D) Because the man did not know the hukum

Key Takeaway
Shubha from valid sahaba ikhtilaf overrides personal admission of knowledge, and the hadd is dropped under the principle 'al-hudud tudra'u bi al-shubuhat'.

Shubha Hukmiyya and Hadd Aversion in Intercourse with Relatives' Slave-Girls
This passage lists cases where hadd is averted due to shubha hukmiyya, especially from father to son's slave-girl.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the specific cases where hadd is averted due to shubha hukmiyya and shubha ishtibah, and apply the hadith 'Anta wa māluka li-abīk' to explain the father's exemption.

Core Idea
A legal doubt from a hadith can avert hadd even if the perpetrator admits knowledge.

This passage from al-Hidayah addresses the delicate interface between textual proof and criminal punishment. The Hanafi school recognizes that certain legal doubts (shubha) can block the imposition of the hadd penalty for zina, even when the act is known to be prohibited. The most prominent example is the father's intercourse with his son's slave-girl, grounded in the Prophet's statement 'You and your wealth belong to your father.' We will walk through the various scenarios and the reasoning behind each.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and default ruling
Start by recognizing that intercourse with a slave-girl not owned by the perpetrator is zina, which normally incurs hadd. The passage lists exceptions where hadd is averted due to shubha.
The default for zina is hadd, but shubha can block it.
2
Locate the primary dalil
The key proof is the hadith 'Anta wa māluka li-abīk' (You and your wealth belong to your father). This textual basis creates a shubha hukmiyya that the father might believe the son's property is permissible for him.
This hadith is considered hasan sahih by many scholars.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The phrase 'your wealth belongs to your father' indicates a form of ownership or right over the son's property. This creates a plausible doubt about the prohibition of the son's slave-girl for the father, sufficient to avert hadd even if the father admits knowing it is haram.
The doubt is objective, not based on the individual's claim.
4
Distinguish shubha types
The passage distinguishes shubha hukmiyya (arising from a textual proof) from shubha ishtibah (arising from customary expansion, as with a slave and his master's slave-girl). Identify which type applies in each scenario.
Shubha hukmiyya is stronger and overrides even admitted knowledge.
5
Apply to all cases
Extend the principle to other cases: father/mother/wife's slave-girl (shubha ishtibah based on inbisat), slave with master's slave-girl (shubha ishtibah), and the slanderer's exemption. Note the conditions and exemptions.
Admission of knowledge can remove shubha in some cases (e.g., with father's/mother's/wife's slave-girl), triggering hadd.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَلَا حَدَّ عَلَى مَنْ وَطِيَ جَارِيَةَ وَلَدِهِ وَوَلَدِ وَلَدِهِ وَإِنْ قَالَ عَلِمْتُ أَنَّهَا عَلَيَّ حَرَامٌ لِأَنَّ الشَّبْهَةَ حُكْمِيَّةٌ لِأَنَّهَا نَشَأَتْ عَنْ دَلِيلٍ وَهُوَ قَوْلُهُ عَلَيْهِ السَّلَامُ أَنْتَ وَمَالُكَ لِأَبِيكَ وَالْأُبُوَّةُ قَائِمَةٌ فِي حَقِّ الْجَدِّ وَيَثْبُتُ النَّسَبُ مِنْهُ وَعَلَيْهِ قِيمَةُ الْجَارِيَةِ وَقَدْ ذَكَرْنَاهُ وَإِذَا وَطِيَ جَارِيَةَ أَبِيهِ أَوْ أُمِّهِ أَوْ زَوْجَتِهِ وَقَالَ ظَنَنْتُ أَنَّهَا تَحِلُّ لِي فَلَا حَدَّ عَلَيْهِ وَلَا عَلَى قَاذِفِهِ وَإِنْ قَالَ عَلِمْتُ أَنَّهَا عَلَيَّ حَرَامٌ حُدَّ وَكَذَا الْعَبْدُ إِذَا وَطِيَ جَارِيَةَ مَوْلَاهُ لِأَنَّ بَيْنَهُمَا الْإِنْبِسَاطَ فِي الِانْتِفَاعِ فَظَنُّهُ فِي الِاسْتِمْتَاعِ مُحْتَمَلٌ فَكَانَتْ شُبْهَةَ اشْتِبَاهٍ إِلَّا أَنَّهُ زِنَاءٌ حَقِيقَةً فَلَا يُحَدُّ قَاذِفَهُ وَكَذَا إِذَا قَالَتِ الْجَارِيَةُ ظَنَنْتُ أَنَّهُ يَحِلُّ لِي وَالْفِعْلُ لَمْ يَدَّعِ فِي الظَّاهِرِ لِأَنَّ الْفِعْلَ وَاحِدٌ
And there is no hadd upon whoever had intercourse with his son's slave-girl or his grandson's slave-girl, even if he said 'I knew that she was unlawful for me,' because the shubha is hukmiyya (legal), because it arises from a proof, and that is his statement (peace be upon him): 'You and your wealth belong to your father.' And fatherhood exists in the right of the grandfather. And lineage is established from him, and upon him is the value of the slave-girl, and we have mentioned it. And if he had intercourse with his father's or mother's or wife's slave-girl and said 'I thought it was permissible for me,' then there is no hadd upon him nor upon his slanderer. And if he said 'I knew that she was unlawful for me,' he is hadded. And likewise the slave if he had intercourse with his master's slave-girl, because there is expansion (inbisat) between them in benefit, so his assumption in enjoyment is probable, so it is a shubha of doubt. Except that it is in reality zina, so his slanderer is not hadded. And likewise if the slave-girl said 'I thought it was permissible for me' and the man did not claim that outwardly, because the act is one.
Source pages p. 155

Commentary
This passage presents multiple rulings regarding intercourse with slave-girls of close relatives, with proofs from hadith and legal reasoning. The first case: a father (or grandfather) who has intercourse with his son's (or grandson's) slave-girl. Even if he says he knew it was haram, no hadd because of a shubha hukmiyya (legal doubt) arising from the Prophet's hadith 'You and your wealth belong to your father.' This hadith indicates a form of ownership the father has over the son's property, creating a doubt about the prohibition of the son's slave-girl for the father. The same applies to the grandfather. However, the father must pay the value of the slave-girl to the son. The second case: if a man has intercourse with his own father's, mother's, or wife's slave-girl, his claim of ignorance ('I thought it was halal') gives him no hadd, and likewise no hadd for slander against him. But if he admits knowledge of prohibition, he is hadded. The third case: a slave who has intercourse with his master's slave-girl. Because there is customary expansion (inbisat) between them in benefit (use of property), his assumption that it might be permissible is plausible, creating a shubha ishtibah (doubt of confusion). Therefore, no hadd on him if he claims he thought it halal, and no hadd on his slanderer. However, if the man does not claim ignorance, the default is that it is zina, but his slanderer is still not hadded because the act itself is ambiguous. Finally, if the slave-girl also claims she thought it was halal, and the man does not make that claim, but the outward act is the same for both, so no hadd on the man either, because the act is one and the doubt applies to both.

Key Terms
شبهة حكمية (shubha hukmiyya): A legal doubt arising from a legitimate Shariah proof, such as a hadith or general principle, that makes an act appear permissible despite being originally prohibited.
انبساط (inbisāṭ): Literally 'expansion' or 'spread'; in this context, a customary degree of mutual ease and sharing in property between two parties, leading to a plausible assumption of permissibility.
شبهة اشتباه (shubha ishtibāh): A doubt of confusion arising from a resemblance or ambiguity in the situation, without a precise textual proof.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is no hadd imposed on a father who has intercourse with his son's slave-girl, even if he says he knew it was haram?
A) Because the son's property is considered the father's property
B) Because the hadith 'You and your wealth belong to your father' creates a shubha hukmiyya
C) Because the father can own his son's property absolutely
D) Because the father did not actually know

Key Takeaway
The hadith 'Anta wa māluka li-abīk' creates a legal doubt that completely averts the hadd punishment for a father's intercourse with his son's slave-girl, even if he knowingly transgressed.

Hadd for Intercourse with Brother's Slave-Girl
This passage states that for brother's or uncle's slave-girl, hadd applies because no inbisat exists.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling for intercourse with a brother's or uncle's slave-girl and explain why inbisat (customary expansion in property) determines the presence of shubha.

Core Idea
No inbisat between brothers means no shubha; hadd is not averted.

This passage continues the discussion on shubha in hudud. The previous case involved a father and son, where inbisat created doubt. Here, the same logic is tested on other relatives: brothers and uncles. You will see that the key factor is not simply kinship, but the customary financial interdependence that creates a plausible assumption of permission.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
The passage rules on a man who has intercourse with his brother's or uncle's slave-girl and claims he thought it was permissible. The ruling is that hadd (the prescribed punishment) applies.
The claim of ignorance does not create shubha here.
2
Recognize the asl (original case) used for comparison
The ruling is based on qiyas. The original case is the father with his son's slave-girl, where no hadd applies due to shubha created by inbisat. Recall that the father and son customarily share property (inbisat), so the father might genuinely assume permission.
The asl is the father-son relationship.
3
Identify the illa (effective cause)
The illa is the presence of customary inbisat (expansion) in property between the parties. In the father-son case, inbisat exists and creates shubha. In the brother-brother or uncle-nephew case, inbisat does not customarily exist to the same degree.
Inbisat is a jaliy (clear) illa, extracted by tahrir al-manat.
4
Apply the illa to the far' (new case)
For brothers and uncles, there is no customary inbisat in property. Therefore, no shubha arises, and hadd is applied. The passage extends this to all maharim (blood relatives) except direct lineage (wilad), which includes parents and children.
The exception: wilad (direct ascendants/descendants) may have inbisat.
5
Confirm the ruling and note the exception
Thus, for any mahram other than ancestors or descendants, hadd applies. The exception underscores that inbisat is the determining factor, not merely the state of being mahram.
This is a clear example of qiyas jaliy in Hanafi fiqh.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَإِنْ وَطِيَ جَارِيَةَ أَخِيهِ أَوْ عَمِّهِ وَقَالَ ظَنَنْتُ أَنَّهَا تَحِلُّ لِي حُدَّ لِأَنَّهُ لَا انْبِسَاطَ فِي الْمَالِ فِيمَا بَيْنَهُمَا وَكَذَا سَائِرُ الْمَحَارِمِ سِوَى الْوِلَادِ لِمَا بَيَّنَّا
And if he had intercourse with his brother's or his uncle's slave-girl and said 'I thought it was permissible for me,' he is hadded because there is no expansion (inbisat) in property between them. And likewise all other maharim (blood relatives) except those by birth (wilad) as we have explained.
Source pages p. 156

Commentary
This passage is a ruling contrasting with the previous one. The case is a man who has intercourse with his brother's or uncle's slave-girl. If he claims he thought it was halal, he is still subject to hadd punishment. The reason is that there is no customary inbisat (expansion, mutual ease) in property between these relatives. Unlike the father-son or slave-master relationships, brothers and uncles do not typically share property in a way that would create a plausible assumption of permissibility for using each other's slave-girls. The ruling extends to all other maharim (blood relatives who are permanently prohibited in marriage) except those connected by direct lineage (wilad: parents, children, grandchildren), where the father-child relationship may have some shubha. Thus, for uncles, aunts, etc., the default is that hadd applies because there is no basis for shubha.

Key Terms
انبساط في المال (inbisāṭ fī al-māl): Customary ease and mutual benefit in property between two parties, typically between parent and child or master and slave, which can create a plausible assumption of permissibility.
محارم (maḥārim): Blood relatives who are permanently prohibited in marriage (e.g., mother, sister, daughter, aunt).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does a man who has intercourse with his brother's slave-girl receive hadd punishment, even if he claims he thought it was halal?
A) Because brothers cannot own slave-girls
B) Because there is no customary inbisat in property between brothers
C) Because the Shafi'i school disagrees
D) Because the man is lying

Key Takeaway
For mahram relatives other than direct lineage, absence of customary inbisat means no shubha, so hadd applies.

No Hadd for Intercourse Based on Credible Report in Doubt
Ruling with proof from the judgment of Ali (RA)

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a man who has intercourse with a woman brought by women claiming she is his wife, and explain why no hadd is imposed but mahr and idda are required.

Core Idea
Reliance on a credible report in a context of doubt averts hadd, but financial obligations remain.

Imagine a man in a dark room, unable to see clearly, when several women bring him a woman and assure him this is his new wife. Trusting them, he consummates the marriage. Later, he discovers she was a complete stranger. Is he liable for the hudud punishment? This passage from al-Hidayah answers with a clear ruling from the judgment of Ali ibn Abi Talib (RA): no hadd, but he must pay the mahr and she must observe idda. Let us examine the reasoning behind this compassionate yet just ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and the ruling
Understand the exact situation: a man is brought a woman who is not his wife, women testify that they have married her to him, and he relies on their statement to have intercourse. The ruling consists of three parts: no hadd punishment, mandatory mahr payment, and obligatory idda for the woman.
The hadd is for zina (unlawful intercourse); it is averted due to the semblance of marriage (shubha).
2
Locate the proof and its source
The text explicitly cites 'Qada bi dhalika Ali' – the judgment of Ali ibn Abi Talib (RA). This is a qawl sahabi (companion's saying), considered a valid proof in Hanafi usul. Identify the type of evidence: it is an authoritative precedent used for analogy.
Ali's judgment is preserved in the Hanafi corpus and treated as a binding precedent.
3
Understand the legal reasoning (ta'lil)
The text explains: 'Because he relied on a proof, and that is the report in a place of doubt, because a man cannot distinguish between his wife and other than her in the beginning.' The key concepts are: i'timad al-dalil (reliance on evidence), and the doubt (ishtibah) arising from darkness or similar circumstances. This doubt creates a shubha that prevents hadd.
Hanafi law requires certainty (yaqin) for hudud; doubt averts them. Here, the report constitutes a reasonable doubt.
4
Contrast with possible opposing views
Some other schools may argue that the man should have verified the marriage before intercourse, and thus hadd is not averted. This difference stems from different weights given to external indicators versus strict verification. Recognize that this is a legitimate ikhtilaf within the broader fiqh tradition.
The mufta bihi in the Hanafi school is as stated: no hadd, but mahr and idda.
5
Apply the ruling to contemporary contexts
Think of modern equivalents: a mistaken identity in a dimly lit ceremony, or reliance on a written document that turns out to be forged. The principle applies when the man has a reasonable excuse. However, if he knew or could easily have verified, the ruling might differ (e.g., if he was negligent). This step builds extrapolation skills.
The underlying illa is the combination of a credible report and a context of genuine uncertainty.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَمَنْ رُفِعَتْ إِلَيْهِ غَيْرُ امْرَأَتِهِ وَقَالَتِ النِّسَاءُ إِنَّهَا زَوَّجْتُكَ فَوَطِيَهَا لَا حَدَّ عَلَيْهِ وَعَلَيْهَا الْمَهْرُ قَضَى بِذَلِكَ عَلِيٌّ وَبِالْعِدَّةِ وَلِأَنَّهُ اعْتَمَدَ دَلِيلًا وَهُوَ الْإِخْبَارُ فِي مَوْضِعِ الِاشْتِبَاهِ إِذِ الْإِنْسَانُ لَا يُمَيِّزُ بَيْنَ امْرَأَتِهِ وَبَيْنَ غَيْرِهَا فِي أَوَّلِ
And whoever someone is brought to him who is not his wife, and the women say 'She is married to you,' and he had intercourse with her: no hadd upon him, and upon her is the mahr. Ali (RA) judged this, and the idda. Because he relied on a proof, and that is the report in a place of doubt, because a man cannot distinguish between his wife and other than her in the beginning...
Source pages p. 156

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof from the judgment of Ali (RA). The scenario: a man is brought a woman in the dark or under confusion, and women claim that they have married her to him. The man relies on their word and has intercourse with her. No hadd is imposed on him because he relied on a proof (the women's report) in a context of legitimate doubt. The man cannot distinguish his wife from another in the dark, so his action is based on a reasonable assumption. However, he must pay the mahr (dower) to the woman, and she must observe the idda (waiting period). This ruling is attributed to Ali (RA). The text is incomplete but the core ruling and reasoning are clear.

Key Terms
مهر (mahr): The obligatory dower paid by a husband to his wife upon marriage; in this context, the man must pay mahr for the intercourse due to the semblance of marriage.
عدة (idda): The waiting period a woman must observe after divorce or death of husband to ensure no pregnancy; here the woman must observe idda after the intercourse.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
When a man relies on a credible report in a situation of genuine doubt, the hadd is averted, but financial and waiting period obligations remain.

Shubha and Hadd: Father's Intercourse with Son's Slave-Girl
Ruling with proof on shubha dropping hadd

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school drops the hadd punishment when a father has intercourse with his son's slave-girl, and identify the role of shubha in hudud.

Core Idea
A doubt of ownership (shubhat al-milk) based on a hadith suffices to drop hadd, even though the act itself is haram.

Hudud punishments are severe and fixed by sacred law, but the Hanafi school recognizes that certain doubts (shubha) can prevent their application. This passage examines a key case: a father's intercourse with his son's slave-girl. The ruling hinges on a hadith that creates a semblance of ownership, thereby generating a shubha that averts the hadd. We will trace the proof, understand the concept of shubha hukmiyya, and see how it extends to other relationships like grandfather, mother, and master's slave-girl.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its default ruling
The act in question is intercourse between a father and his son's slave-girl. Determine the inherent hukm of this act (haram) and the prescribed punishment (hadd for zina).
The act is zina, which is haram and normally requires hadd if conditions are met.
2
Understand the concept of shubha in hudud
Shubha is a doubt or uncertainty that can prevent the application of hadd. Hanafi fiqh recognizes several types; here, shubhat al-milk (doubt of ownership) is invoked.
Shubha is not a license to commit the act; it only blocks punishment.
3
Examine the proof (dalil)
The hadith 'أنت ومالك لأبيك' (You and your property belong to your father) is cited. Analyze how this text creates a shubha: it suggests the father has a right over his son's property, including slave-girls.
The hadith is ahad and considered hasan sahih by some scholars; it provides a zanni (probable) basis.
4
Trace the extension to other cases
The passage extends the ruling to grandfather, father's or mother's or wife's slave-girl, and slave with master's slave-girl. The underlying rationale is inbisat (familiarity) creating a probability of assumption of permissibility.
These extensions illustrate the Hanafi method of qiyas and the broad application of shubha.
5
Identify the mufta bihi and the limits
The ruling that hadd is not applied is from zahir al-riwayah and is mufta bihi. However, if the person knew the act was haram, hadd is imposed. Note that the hadith does not make the act halal.
The common misunderstanding is that the hadith permits the act; it only prevents punishment.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

تشريح ولا حد على من وطى جارية ولده وولد ولده وان قال علمت انها على ... الخ چونکہ حدود شبہ سے ساقط ہو جاتی ہیں مذکورہ صورت میں باپ یا دادا کیلئے اپنے بیٹے یا پوتے کی باندی سے وطی کرنے کی صورت میں شبہ ملکیت ہے حدیث میں ہے انت و مالك لا بيك اس لئے شبہ واقع ہوا لہذا حد جاری نہ ہوگی۔
Chapter on intercourse that necessitates the hadd punishment and which does not. It is forbidden. Because it is a shubha hukmiyya. Because one evidence gives rise to this doubt. That evidence is the saying of the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him): 'You and your property belong to your father' (narrated by Ibn Majah, Tabarani, Bazzar, and Ibn Hibban; this hadith is hasan sahih). And the ruling of the father applies to the grandfather as well. And the lineage of the child will be established and the father must only pay the value of his son's slave-girl. We have explained this issue in the chapter on marriage of slaves. And if someone has intercourse with his father's or mother's or wife's slave-girl and said, 'I thought it was permissible for me,' then hadd is not imposed on him, and whoever accuses him of adultery, the hadd of qadhf will not be imposed on him because in reality this act is haram. However, due to the occurrence of shubha, the hadd of qadhf is not imposed. And if the father said, 'I knew it was haram for me,' then hadd will be imposed. Similarly, if a slave has intercourse with his master's slave-girl, the same detailed ruling applies because there is familiarity (inbisat) in deriving benefit among them, so there is probability that he assumed it was permissible. Therefore this shubha is called shubha ishtibah. But in reality it is adultery, so if someone accuses him, he will not receive hadd of qadhf. Similarly, if the slave-girl said, 'I thought it was permissible for me,' even if the slave did not claim that, according to zahir al-riwayah, hadd is not imposed on the slave because the act is one. Commentary: 'No hadd on one who has intercourse with his child's or grandchild's slave-girl, even if he said I knew it was...' Because hudud are dropped by shubha. In the mentioned case, the father or grandfather has shubhat al-milk due to the hadith, so shubha occurs and thus hadd is not applied.

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof discussing scenarios where hadd is not applied due to shubha (doubt) arising from blood relationships. The author begins by stating the general rule that shubha can prevent the application of hudud. The specific case is a father having intercourse with his son's slave-girl. The shubha here is shubhat al-milk (doubt of ownership) based on the hadith 'You and your property belong to your father' (أنت ومالك لأبيك). This hadith creates the impression that the father has some right over his son's property, including slave-girls. Therefore, the father is not subject to hadd, and anyone accusing him of adultery also does not receive hadd of qadhf. However, if the father knew it was haram, then hadd applies. The same applies to a slave and his master's slave-girl due to inbisat (familiarity) in deriving benefit. The commentary reiterates that hudud are dropped by shubha, and the hadith is the basis for shubhat al-milk.

Key Terms
حد (hadd): Corporal punishment prescribed for specific crimes like adultery (zina) and false accusation (qadhf).
شبہ (shubha): A doubt or uncertainty that prevents the application of hadd punishment.
شبہ اشتباہ (shubha ishtibah): A specific type of shubha arising from a situation where the person genuinely mistakes the act as permissible due to some evidence.

Hadd for Intercourse with a Brother's or Uncle's Slave-Girl
Ruling with qiyas proof on hadd application

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish when hadd applies for intercourse with a relative's slave-girl based on the concept of inbisat, and apply the qiyas reasoning to other mahram relatives.

Core Idea
Hadd applies for intercourse with a brother's or uncle's slave-girl because inbisat is absent, unlike between father and son.

This passage extends the well-known ruling that a father is not subject to hadd for intercourse with his son's slave-girl due to shubha. Here, the text clarifies that this concession does not extend to brothers, uncles, and other mahram relatives except direct ascendants and descendants. The key legal factor is the presence or absence of inbisat—the customary ease of benefiting from each other's property. Understanding this distinction is essential for correctly applying hudud rulings in inheritance and property contexts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Ruling and the Exception
Note that the passage states hadd is imposed for intercourse with a brother's or uncle's slave-girl. The exception is for direct blood relatives (wilad) where inbisat exists, giving rise to shubha that drops the hadd.
The exception is only for direct ascendants/descendants, not all mahram.
2
Extract the Underlying Principle
The principle is the presence or absence of inbisat (familiarity in property dealings). Where inbisat exists, a shubha is created that prevents hadd; where absent, no shubha exists and hadd applies.
Inbisat is a customary concept, not a textual one.
3
Trace the Qiyas Reasoning
The asl (original case) is the father's intercourse with his son's slave-girl (no hadd). The far' (new case) is the brother/uncle's intercourse. The illa (effective cause) is the presence/absence of inbisat. Since no inbisat between brother/uncle, hadd applies.
The qiyas is jali (clear) because the illa is explicitly derived.
4
Apply to Other Mahram Relatives
The text extends the ruling to all mahram relatives except those related by blood descent (wilad). This includes maternal uncles, aunts, etc., where inbisat is not customary.
Mahram by marriage or fosterage does not create inbisat in property.
5
Determine the Consequence
The ruling means that a claim of ignorance (e.g., 'I thought it was permissible') does not drop the hadd, because the absence of shubha is clear.
Hadd is applied unless a valid shubha exists; here it does not.

Source Text

ترجمہ اور اگر کسی نے اپنے بھائی یا چا کی باندی سے ولی کی۔ اور یہ کہا کہ میرا گمان تو یہ تھا کہ وہ باندی میرے لئے حلال ہے۔ تو اس پر حد جاری کی جائے گی کیونکہ بھتیجے اور چا کے درمیان مال کے بارے میں کوئی انبساط نہیں ہے اور یہی حکم دوسرے محارم مثلا ماموں اور خالہ وغیرہ کا بھی ہے۔ سوائے ان لوگوں کے جن سے ولادت کی رشتہ داری کی وجہ سے مال سے نفع اٹھانے کا انبساط تو ہوتا ہے۔ یعنی ان کے درمیان آپس میں ایسی گنجائش ہوتی ہے کہ ایک دوسرے ک مال سے نفع اٹھا ئیں اس طرح ان میں شبہ کی بھی گنجائش ہوتی ہے۔ اور ماموں والہ و چاوغیرہ میں ایسا انسا نہیں ہوتا ہے۔ اس لئے شبہ کی بھی گنجائش ہوتی ہے۔ تشریح... و ان وطى جارية اخيه او عمه و قال ظننت الخ مطلب ترجمہ سے واضح ہے۔
Intercourse with brother's or uncle's slave-girl necessitates hadd. And if someone has intercourse with his brother's or uncle's slave-girl and said, 'I thought it was permissible for me,' then hadd will be imposed on him because there is no inbisat (familiarity) in property between them. Likewise, all other mahram relatives except those related by blood descent (wilad), as we have explained. Translation: And if someone has intercourse with his brother's or uncle's slave-girl, and said, 'I thought that slave-girl was permissible for me,' then hadd will be imposed on him. The same ruling applies to other mahram relatives like maternal uncle and aunt, except for those with whom there is inbisat in deriving benefit from property due to blood descent relationship. That is, among them there is such ease that they benefit from each other's property, thus there is scope for shubha. But among maternal uncle, aunt, and uncle, there is no such inbisat, so there is no scope for shubha. Commentary: This is clear from the translation.

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof explaining that hadd is imposed for intercourse with a brother's or uncle's slave-girl, even if the person claims he thought it was permissible. The reason is that there is no inbisat (familiarity) in property dealings between these relatives, unlike the case of a father and son. The exception is for direct ascendants and descendants (wilad) where inbisat exists due to the natural flow of property within the family. Therefore, the shubha that existed in the father's case does not exist here, so hadd is not dropped.

Key Terms
انبساط (inbisat): Familiarity and ease in dealing with each other's property, which creates a possibility of shubha.
محرم (mahram): A person with whom marriage is permanently prohibited due to blood, marriage, or fosterage.

Shubha in Intercourse: No Hadd on the Deceived Man
Ruling on a man deceived with shubha on wedding night

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling when a man is deceived into intercourse on his wedding night and explain why the hadd penalty is dropped but mahr and 'iddah remain obligatory.

Core Idea
Reliance on a plausible report in uncertainty creates shubha that drops hadd but not financial duties.

This lesson examines a specific case of shubha (ambiguity) in the law of hudud. A man on his wedding night is sent a woman other than his wife, told that she is his wife, and he has intercourse with her. The Hanafi school rules that he is not liable for hadd because he acted on a deceptive report that constituted a valid shubha. However, the act is still haram, and he must pay the mahr to the woman; she must also observe 'iddah.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is intercourse with a woman who is not his wife, under the belief she is his wife. The ruling: no hadd (hadd penalty) applies to him.
2
Locate the dalil (proof)
The proof is the judgment of Ali (ra) and consensus of the Companions. The text cites: 'Ali judged accordingly' and adds that there is consensus.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah (how the proof yields the ruling)
Ali’s judgment that mahr is due implies that the intercourse is not considered zina, thus no hadd. The consensus supports dropping hadd in such shubha.
4
Understand the reasoning (ta'lil) for the shubha
The man relied on a report (the women's statement) in a context of genuine uncertainty – he cannot distinguish his new wife from another in the dark. This makes him like a deceived person (maghrur), and shubha blocks hadd.
5
Note the remaining obligations
Even though hadd is dropped, the man must pay mahr (full bridal gift) and the woman must observe 'iddah. This shows that the act is still considered haram and creates legal effects.
Mahr is wajib in any valid contract or doubtful intercourse; 'iddah is for ascertaining paternity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وطی بالشبہ موجب حد نہیں
وَمَنْ رُقَتْ إِلَيْهِ غَيْرُ امْرَأتِهِ وَقَالَتِ النِّسَاءُ إِنَّهَا زَوَّجْتُكَ فَوَطِيَهَا لَا حَدَّ عَلَيْهِ وَعَلَيْهَا الْمَهْرُ قَضَى بِذَلِكَ عَلِيٌّ وَبِالْعِدَّةِ وَلِأَنَّهُ اعْتَمَدَ دَلِيْلًا وَهُوَالْا خْبَارُ فِي مَوْضِعِ الْإِشْتِبَاءِ إِذِ الْإِنْسَانُ لَا يُمَيِّزُبَيْنَ امْرَاتِهِ وَبَيْنَ غَيْرِ هَا فِي أَوَّلِ الْوَهْلَةِ فَصَارَ كَالْمَغْرُورِ وَلَا يُحَدُّ قَاذِفَهُ إِلَّا فِي رَوَايَةٍ عَنْ أَبِي يُوْسُفَ لِأَنَّ الْمِلْكَ مُنْعَدِمٌ حَقِيقَةٌ
ترجمہ اگر نکاح کے بعد ملاپ کی رات کو شوہر کے پاس اس کی بیوی کے علاوہ دوسری عورت بھیج دی گئی۔ یہ کہتے ہوئے کہ یہ تمہاری بیوی ہے۔ اس بناء پر اس نے اس عورت سے وطی کر لی تو اس پر حد جاری نہیں کی جائے گی۔ البتہ اس مرد پر عورت کا مہر لازم ہو جائے گا۔ یعنی عورت کا جو مہر ہوتا ہے وہ دینا ہوگا۔ چنانچہ حضرت علی کرم اللہ وجہ نے بھی مہر کے واجب ہونے کا حکم دیا تھا ۔ لیکن شارحین کو اگر چہ یہ روایت نہیں ملی ہے۔ پھر بھی اسی پر اجماع ہے ۔ (ع)۔ اور اس وجہ سے بھی حد جاری نہیں ہوگی کہ مرد نے جو اپنی بیوی کی نئی ہونے کی وجہ سے اس کی شناخت ویقین میں مشتبہ تھا۔ اس نے ان عورتوں کی اس طرح کی رہبری کہ اپنی بیوی کے پاس جاؤ کو اپنے لئے دلیل بنالیا اور اس پر اعتماد کر لیا۔ کیونکہ ایک مرد پہلی نظر میں اپنی بیوی اور غیر کے درمیان تمیز نہیں کر سکتا ہے۔ اس لئے یہ شخص بھی دھو کہ کھائے ہوئے انسان کے مثل ہو گیا ( یعنی جسے کسی عورت نے خود کو غیر شادی شدہ ظاہر کرتے ہوئے اپنے نکاح پر آمادہ کرلیا۔ یا غیرشخص کی بھاگی ہوئی باندی تھی اور اسے حقیقت معلوم نہ تھی ۔ چنانچہ اس سے نکاح کے بعد ولی بھی کرلی اور بعد میں معلوم ہو گیا۔ تو اس مرد پر حد جاری نہیں ہوتی ہے اس طرح موجودہ صورت میں بھی مرد پر حد جاری نہیں ہوگی ۔ اور ایسے شخص کو اگر کوئی زنا کی تہمت لگائے تو اسے بھی حد قذف نہیں لگائی جائے گی۔ اس وطی کے خرام ثابت ہونے کی وجہ سے۔ کیونکہ حقیقت میں اس مرد کو اس عورت پر ملکیت نہیں ہے۔ یہی ظاہر الروایۃ ہے۔ لیکن امام ابو یوسف سے ایک روایت یہ بھی ہے۔ کہ اس کی تہمت دینے والے کو حد ماری جائے۔ تشريح . ومن زفت اليه غير امرأته وقالت النساء انها زوجتك فوطيها ... الخ مطلب ترجمہ سے واضح ہے۔
Intercourse with shubha does not necessitate hadd. And if a woman other than his wife is sent to him (at night) and the women say, 'We have married her to you,' and he has intercourse with her, there is no hadd on him, and he must pay the mahr. Ali (may Allah honor his face) judged accordingly, and she must observe the 'iddah. And because he relied on a proof, which is the report in a place of uncertainty, since a man cannot distinguish between his wife and another at the first moment (of the wedding night), so he became like one deceived. And his accuser is not punished with hadd except in a narration from Abu Yusuf, because ownership is non-existent in reality. Translation: If after the marriage, on the night of consummation, another woman is sent to the husband saying, 'This is your wife,' and on that basis he has intercourse with her, then hadd is not imposed on him. However, he must pay the mahr for the woman. Indeed, Ali (may Allah honor his face) also gave judgment that the mahr is obligatory. Although some commentators have not found this narration, still there is consensus upon it. And also because hadd is not imposed due to the fact that the man, because his wife was new, was uncertain in recognizing her. He took the guidance of those women that 'go to your wife' as a proof for himself and relied upon it. Because a man cannot distinguish between his wife and another on the first sight. Therefore, this person became like a deceived person. And if someone accuses him of adultery, the accuser will also not receive the hadd of qadhf because of the confirmed haram nature of this intercourse (due to lack of ownership). This is the evident position (zahir al-riwayah). However, there is a narration from Abu Yusuf that the accuser should be punished. Commentary: This is clear from the translation.
Source pages p. 156

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof discussing the case where a man is deceived on his wedding night. Another woman is sent to him claiming to be his wife. He has intercourse with her. The ruling is that no hadd is imposed on him because he relied on the report of the women, which constitutes a shubha. He cannot distinguish his new wife from another woman in the dark, so he is like a deceived person (maghrur). He must pay the mahr to the woman, and she must observe 'iddah. The accuser (qadif) also does not receive hadd of qadhf according to zahir al-riwayah, except in a narration from Abu Yusuf. This shows that even though the act is haram, the shubha prevents hadd.

Key Terms
مهر (mahr): The bridal gift owed by the husband to the wife; here owed to the woman for intercourse under a valid contract.
مغرور (maghrur): A person who is deceived; here, the man is likened to one deceived due to reliance on the women's statement.

Key Terms from Source Passage

In the case of a man who is sent a woman on his wedding night and told she is his wife, what is the ruling according to the Hanafi school?
A) He is subject to hadd because zina is proven.
B) No hadd, and no mahr or 'iddah.
C) No hadd, but mahr and 'iddah are obligatory.
D) He is subject to tazir and must pay mahr.

Key Takeaway
When a man relies on a plausible report in a situation of genuine uncertainty, hadd is dropped but the act remains legally effective for mahr and 'iddah.

Accusing the Deceived: No Hadd of Qadhf on the Accuser
Ruling on the accuser of a man who committed intercourse under shubha

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on a person who accuses a man of zina after the man was deceived into intercourse, and explain the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf regarding the accuser's punishment.

Core Idea
If the act is not zina due to shubha, accusing the doer of zina is false; thus no hadd of qadhf applies, except in a narration from Abu Yusuf.

This lesson continues from the previous case: now the focus shifts to the person who accuses the man of zina. Since the man's act is not considered zina (due to shubha), the accusation is false. The Hanafi school rules that the accuser does not receive the hadd punishment for qadhf (false accusation of zina) because the act, though haram, is not zina and therefore the accusation does not meet the conditions for qadhf. However, there is a narration from Abu Yusuf that the accuser should be punished. This lesson examines the basis of this dispute and the mufta bihi position.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is accusing (qadhf) the man of zina. The ruling: the accuser is not subjected to the hadd of qadhf.
2
Understand the basis for dropping hadd
The text gives the reason: 'because ownership is non-existent in reality.' This means the intercourse is not real zina because there is no ownership (no marriage or valid contract). However, it is still haram. The absence of the essence of zina (i.e., no real ownership) makes the accusation not truly qadhf.
The legal maxim: hadd of qadhf requires that the accused be truly guilty of zina; here the act is haram but not zina.
3
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
The text mentions an exception: 'except in a narration from Abu Yusuf.' Abu Yusuf held that the accuser should be punished with hadd. This is a minority view within the Hanafi school.
4
Evaluate the tarjih (preferred view)
The majority position (zahir al-riwayah) is that no hadd on the accuser. The text implies this is the relied-upon view (mufta bihi).
Tarjih authority: the text itself and later Hanafi jurisprudence typically follow the majority.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence)
Under the majority view, the accuser is not punished with hadd; under Abu Yusuf's narration, he would be. The difference affects whether the accuser faces fixed penalty or not.

Finding a Woman on His Bed: Hadd Applies Except for Blind Man
Ruling that hadd applies for intercourse with a woman found on his bed unless genuine shubha exists.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on hadd for a man who finds a woman on his bed and explain the exception for a blind man based on shubha.

Core Idea
Long companionship eliminates shubha, so hadd applies; a blind man's reliance on a woman's claim creates valid shubha.

This passage presents a key hadd ruling from the Hanafi school: if a man finds a woman on his bed and has intercourse with her, assuming she is his wife, hadd is imposed because after long companionship he should have recognized her. The only exception is a blind man who, upon calling, receives a woman's claim that she is his wife—this report creates a genuine shubha that drops the hadd. Understanding this ruling requires analysing the role of shubha and the evidentiary weight of a woman's statement.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What is the act being judged? (Finding a woman on his bed and having intercourse). What is the ruling? (Hadd is obligatory).
The act is considered zina unless a valid shubha (uncertainty) exists.
2
Locate the dalil
What proof justifies the ruling? The text states: 'because there is no uncertainty after a long period of companionship' (lā ishtibāh ba‘da ṭūl al-ṣuḥbah). This is a rational proof based on the man's ability to distinguish his wife.
The dalil is aqli (rational), not naqli (textual).
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
How does the proof yield the ruling? Long companionship (suhbah) makes recognition certain, so any assumption that the woman is his wife is baseless—hence no shubha, and hadd applies.
The man's failure to verify, despite his ability, means he is culpable.
4
Note the exception for the blind man
What is the exception? If the man is blind, he can distinguish by asking. But if he calls and a woman responds claiming to be his wife, that report (khabar) constitutes evidence, creating shubha and dropping hadd.
The exception hinges on the woman's explicit claim, not mere silence or acquiescence.
5
Evaluate the role of shubha
Shubha is a key element in hadd: it negates the penalty. Here, the sighted man has no shubha because he can see; the blind man may have shubha if he receives a credible report.
Other mahram relatives on the bed do not create shubha because the man should verify.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اپنے بستر پر کسی عورت کو پایا اور اس سے وطی کر لی تو حد جاری ہوگی
وَ مَنْ وَجَدَ اِمْرَأَةٌ عَلَى فِرَاشِهِ فَوَطِيَهَا فَعَلَيْهِ الْحَدُّ لِأَنَّهُ لَا إِشْتِبَاهَ بَعْدَ طُولِ الصُّحْبَةِ فَلَمْ يَكُنِ الظَّنُّ مُسْتَتِدًا إِلَى دَلِيلِ وَهذَا لَا نَّهُ قَدْيَنَامُ عَلَى فِرَاشِهَا غَيْرُهَا مِنَ الْمَحَارِمِ الَّتِي فِي بَيْتِهَا وَكَذَا إِذَا كَانَ أَعْمَى لِأَنَّهُ يُمْكِنُهُ السَّمِيزُ بِالسُّوَالِ وَغَيْرِهِ إِلَّا إِذَا كَانَ دَعَا هَا فَاجَابَتْهُ أَجْنَبِيَّةٌ وَقَالَتْ اَنَا زَوْجَتُكَ فَوَاقَعَهَا لِأَنَّ الْإِخْبَارَ دَلِيلٌ
ترجمہ اور جس نے اپنے بستر پر کسی عورت کو پا کر اس سے ولی کر لی ۔ حالانکہ وہ اس کی بیوی نہیں ہے ) تو اس پر حد واجب ہوگی۔ کیونکہ ایک زمانہ تک ساتھ رہنے سے اپنی بیوی کی شناخت میں اشتباہ نہیں ہو سکتا ہے۔ اس لئے اس کے گمان کے ساتھ کوئی قابل قبول دلیل نہیں پائی گئی۔ اسی لئے اشتباہ نہیں ہوا۔ کیونکہ کبھی کبھی ایسا بھی ہوتا رہتا ہے کہ انسان کے بستر پر بیوی کے علاوہ ماں بہن بیٹی وغیرہ اور گھر کے افراد میں سے بھی کوئی آکر سویا کرتی ہے۔ اسی طرح اگر کوئی اندھا آدمی ہو تو وہ بھی اس سے کچھ پوچھ کر باتیں وغیرہ کر کے بیوی اور غیر کے درمیان امتیاز کر سکتا ہے۔ البتہ اگر اس اندھے کے آواز دینے پر بیوی ہونے کا اقرار کر لیا۔ تو اس نے اس عورت سے وھی کر لی تب اس پر حد واجب نہیں ہوگی۔ کیونکہ خبر دینا اقرار کرنا ایک قابل قبول دلیل ہے (اور اگر وہ صرف ولی کرانے پر آمادہ ہوئی یا اس نے زبان سے صرف اچھا کہہ دیا۔ اس پر اندھے نے ولی کر لی تب اسے حد ماری جائے گی )۔ تشريح .... و من وجد امرأة على فراشه فوطيها فعليه الحد لانه لا اشتباه ... الخ مطلب ترجمہ سے واضح ہے۔
And whoever finds a woman on his bed and has intercourse with her, then hadd is upon him, because there is no uncertainty (ishtibah) after a long period of companionship, so the assumption was not based on any evidence. And this is because sometimes other mahram women in the house sleep on her bed. Likewise, even if he is blind, he can distinguish by asking and other means, except if he called her and a female stranger responded and said, 'I am your wife,' and he had intercourse with her, because the report is evidence. Translation: And whoever finds a woman on his bed and has intercourse with her (while she is not his wife), then hadd is obligatory upon him because after a long time of living together, he cannot have uncertainty in recognizing his wife. Therefore, his assumption had no acceptable evidence. Hence, no shubha occurs. Because sometimes other than his wife, such as mother, sister, daughter, etc., from the household may come and sleep on his bed. Similarly, if he is blind, he can distinguish by asking and talking. However, if the blind man called out and she responded claiming to be his wife, then he has intercourse with her, then hadd is not obligatory because the report (khabar) is evidence. (But if she only agreed to intercourse or just said 'okay', then the blind man will be subject to hadd.) Commentary: This is clear from the translation.
Source pages p. 157

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof explaining that if a man finds a woman on his bed and has intercourse with her, assuming she is his wife, hadd is imposed because after long companionship he should be able to recognize his wife. There is no shubha here. The exception is a blind man who, if he calls and a woman responds claiming to be his wife, may have a valid shubha. Also, the rule applies even though other mahram women might sleep on his bed; he should verify before intercourse. So the default is hadd unless there is a genuine reason for uncertainty.

Key Terms
صُحْبَة (suhbah): Companionship; long cohabitation makes recognition certain.
إخْبَار (ikhbar): A report or information; here, a woman's claim to be the wife creates shubha for a blind man.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) If he simply finds her on the bed.
B) If he calls out and she claims to be his wife.
C) If she is a mahram relative.
D) If he is blind, hadd is never applied.

Key Takeaway
Long companionship removes shubha for a sighted man, making him liable for hadd; a blind man can escape hadd only if he receives a credible claim from the woman.

Hadd for Marrying a Mahram: Abu Hanifa vs. Sahibayn
Intra-Hanafi dispute on hadd for marrying a mahram

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn on hadd for marrying a mahram and explain the role of shubha in the dispute.

Core Idea
An invalid contract can still create shubha to avert hadd, but only if it has some semblance of legal form.

This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute on a sensitive issue: a man who marries a woman he is permanently prohibited from marrying (a mahram) and then has intercourse. The key question is whether the hadd punishment for zina applies. Abu Hanifa distinguishes between the mere existence of a contract (even invalid) and a complete nullity. The Sahibayn and Imam al-Shafi'i disagree, focusing on the fundamental prohibition. Understanding this requires grasping the concept of shubha and how it interacts with the conditions for hadd.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the disputed ruling
The act is marrying a mahram woman and having intercourse. The dispute is whether the person is liable to hadd (the fixed punishment for zina).
The woman is a mahram, e.g., sister, mother, daughter, etc.
2
State Abu Hanifa's position and its basis
Abu Hanifa says no hadd. His reasoning is that even an invalid marriage contract (nikah fasid) creates a shubha (semblance of legality) that averts the hadd. However, the marriage is invalid and must be annulled, and a discretionary punishment (ta'zir) may be applied if the person knew the prohibition.
Shubha in Hanafi fiqh is a doubt or semblance that negates the prerequisite of definite knowledge for hadd.
3
State the Sahibayn and Shafi'i position and its basis
Abu Yusuf, Muhammad al-Shaybani, and al-Shafi'i say hadd is obligatory if the person knew the woman was prohibited. Their reasoning: the contract is not valid (the text cuts off, but the logical completion is that an invalid contract cannot create a shubha because it is essentially null).
They deem the contract as batil (void) rather than fasid, so no shubha.
4
Identify the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence)
Under Abu Hanifa, the person avoids the fixed punishment (hadd) but may face a discretionary punishment and the marriage must be cancelled. Under the Sahibayn, the person receives the full hadd for zina (e.g., 100 lashes or stoning), and the marriage is void from the start.
The difference affects not only the punishment but also the legal consequences of the marriage (e.g., lineage, mahr).
5
Evaluate the mufta bihi position
The passage does not specify tarjih (which view is relied upon). In the Hanafi school, the later preferred view (mufta bihi) often follows Abu Hanifa but with qualification: if the person truly believed the contract could be valid (i.e., was ignorant), no hadd; if they knew the prohibition, some later scholars lean toward the Sahibayn's view. However, the dominant position in the madhhab is that the invalid marriage contract does not create a shubha that blocks hadd when the prohibition is known.
See Al-Hidayah and Radd al-Muhtar for detailed tarjih.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
محرمہ سے نکاح کرنے کے بعد وطی کر لی تو حد جاری ہوگی یا نہیں، اقوال فقہاء
وَ مَنْ تَزَوَّجَ امْرَأَةٌ لَا يَحِلُّ لَهُ نِكَاحُهَا فَوَطِيَهَا لَا يَجِبُ عَلَيْهِ الْحَدُّ عِنْدَ أَبِي حَنِيفَةَ لَكِنَّهُ يُرْجِعُ عُقُوبَةٌ إِذَا كَانَ عَلِيمٌ بِذَالِكَ وَقَالَ أَبُويُوسُفَ وَمُحَمَّدٌ وَالشَّافِعِيُّ عَلَيْهِ الْجَدَاذَا كَانَ عَالِمًا بِذَالِكَ لِأَنَّهُ عَقْدَلَمْ
And whoever marries a woman whom it is not permissible for him to marry (a mahram) and then has intercourse with her, the hadd is not obligatory upon him according to Abu Hanifa, but he (the marriage) is invalid, and he is to be punished (if he was knowledgeable). Abu Yusuf, Muhammad, and al-Shafi'i said: Hadd is upon him if he was knowledgeable of that, because the contract (is invalid)...
Source pages p. 157

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute about the application of hadd when someone marries a mahram woman (e.g., mother, sister) and then has intercourse. Abu Hanifa holds that hadd is not imposed because there is a contract, even though it is invalid, creating a shubha. However, he says the marriage is invalid and there is some punishment (ta'zir). Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the Sahibayn) along with al-Shafi'i say that hadd applies if the person knew the prohibition. The text is cut off but gives the beginning of their reasoning: because the contract is not valid.

Key Terms
محرمہ (mahrama): A woman who is permanently prohibited in marriage due to blood, marriage, or fosterage.
عقوبہ (uqubah): Punishment; here, probably ta'zir (discretionary punishment) as opposed to hadd.
حد (hadd): Fixed punishment ordained by Allah, such as for zina.
شبهہ (shubha): Legal doubt or semblance that averts the fixed punishment.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the position of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the Sahibayn) on the hadd for a man who marries a mahram and has intercourse?
A) No hadd applies regardless of knowledge.
B) Hadd applies only if he was ignorant.
C) Hadd applies if he was knowledgeable of the prohibition.
D) Hadd applies only if the woman also consented.

Key Takeaway
An invalid marriage contract with a mahram creates a shubha for Abu Hanifa that blocks hadd, but the Sahibayn require substantial validity for such a shubha.

No Hadd for Mistaken Intercourse: The Wedding Night Scenario
Explains the hadd ruling for mistaken wedding night intercourse and its effect on the accuser.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on hadd for a man who mistakenly had intercourse due to shubha, and explain the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the accuser's liability for qadhf.

Core Idea
A credible mistake (shubha) waives the hadd penalty, and the accuser is not flogged except in one narration.

This lesson explores a nuanced scenario where a man is sent a woman on his wedding night and, believing her to be his wife, has intercourse. The Hanafi school reasons that the man's credible mistake creates a shubha, exempting him from the hadd punishment. The text further examines whether someone who accuses this man of zina is liable for the hadd of qadhf, revealing an intra-Hanafi difference between Zahir al-Riwayah and a narration from Abu Yusuf.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its default ruling
Intercourse with a woman not one's wife is normally zina, incurring hadd. Here, the man's belief that she is his wife creates a shubha that waives the hadd.
The default ruling is altered by the presence of a credible mistake.
2
Understand the reason for shubha
The women accompanying the bride gave credible information that the woman was his wife. This is considered shubha based on credible testimony, not mere suspicion.
Shubha based on credible information is recognized in Hanafi law.
3
Compare with the case of al-maghruur
The man is analogous to one deceived into a marriage (al-maghruur), where no hadd applies. The text explicitly states 'so he became like the deceived one'.
Qiyas: the illa is the presence of a credible mistake that negates criminal intent.
4
Determine the ruling on the accuser
According to Zahir al-Riwayah (Imam Abu Hanifa and Muhammad), the accuser is not liable for hadd al-qadhf because the man's act is not proven to be zina due to shubha.
The accuser is not a qadhif if the act itself is not legally zina.
5
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Abu Yusuf narrates that the accuser should be flogged, because in reality the man had no ownership over the woman, so the act is zina. The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is the Zahir al-Riwayah view.
This is a legitimate ikhtilaf within the school; the practical difference is whether the accuser receives 80 lashes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب الوطى الذي يوجب الحد والذي لا يوجبه [الْوَهْلَةِ؟] فَصَارَ كَالْمَغْرُورِ وَلَا يُحَدُّ قَاذِفَهُ إِلَّا فِي رَوَايَةٍ عَنْ أَبِي يُوْسُفَ لِأَنَّ الْمِلْكَ مُنْعَدِمٌ حَقِيقَةٌ
Chapter on intercourse that necessitates the hadd and that which does not. ... the panic? so he became like the deceived one, and his accuser is not subjected to hadd except in a narration from Abu Yusuf, because the ownership is non-existent in reality.
Source pages p. 157

Commentary
This passage is a ruling from the chapter on intercourse that incurs or does not incur the hadd penalty, with an intra-Hanafi dispute. It addresses a scenario where, on the wedding night, a woman other than the man's wife is sent to him, and the women accompanying her say, 'This is your wife.' The man then has intercourse with her believing her to be his wife. The ruling states that no hadd is imposed on him because his mistake is based on credible information (the women's statement), which creates a shubha (doubt). This is analogous to the case of al-maghruur (one who is deceived into a marriage), where no hadd applies. Additionally, if someone accuses this man of zina, that accuser is not liable for the hadd of qadhf (false accusation) according to the apparent riwaya (Zahir al-Riwayah). There is, however, a narration from Abu Yusuf stating that the accuser should be subjected to hadd. The reason for no hadd on the accuser is that, in reality, the man had no valid ownership (milk) over the woman, so the act technically resembles zina, but the shubha prevents the hadd on the man. The text also implies consensus on the obligation of mahr.

Key Terms
شبهة (shubha): A doubt or resemblance that prevents the application of the hadd penalty.
المغرور (al-maghruur): One who is deceived into a marriage or contract, and thus not liable for hadd.
حد القذف (hadd al-qadhf): The prescribed punishment for falsely accusing a married person of zina.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Zahir al-Riwayah, what is the ruling on the accuser of a man who mistakenly had intercourse with a woman sent as his bride?
A) He is liable for hadd al-qadhf.
B) He is not liable.
C) He is liable for ta'zir.
D) He is liable only if the man knew the truth.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school waives hadd for both the man and his accuser due to shubha, with Abu Yusuf dissenting on the accuser.

Hadd for Intercourse with a Woman Found on One's Bed
States that hadd is required if a man finds a woman on his bed and has intercourse, with exceptions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi ruling on hadd when a man finds a woman on his bed, distinguishing when shubha negates the hadd.

Core Idea
After long companionship, doubt does not excuse hadd; only a credible claim from the woman can create shubha.

In this lesson, we examine a specific scenario from the chapter on hudud: a man finds a woman on his bed and has intercourse. The Hanafi school clarifies when the hadd of zina applies and when a shubha (doubt) can prevent it. The key factor is 'tul al-suhbah' (long companionship) and the presence of a credible verbal report (dalil).

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the general ruling
The act is intercourse with a woman found on his bed who is not his wife. The general ruling is that the hadd (punishment for zina) is wajib upon the man.
This is the default position: hadd applies.
2
Analyze the basis (dalil) for the ruling
The ruling is based on the lack of doubt after long companionship (tul al-suhbah). The assumption that it might be another woman is not supported by evidence (dalil), because the man has lived long enough with his wife to know her.
The absence of shubha is the justifying cause (illah).
3
Consider the exception: verbal report as shubha
If the man called out and a woman answered claiming to be his wife, and he relied on that report, then no hadd. The report is considered a dalil (evidence) that creates shubha, negating the hadd.
This exception is narrow: the man must have initiated the call.
4
Apply to special cases: blind man
A blind man is also liable because he can verify by asking or other means. Blindness does not automatically create shubha.
The blind man must take active steps to confirm identity.
5
Extract the legal principle
Certainty (tul al-suhbah) removes shubha; only a credible verbal report can reintroduce it. This balances the need for punishment with fairness in cases of genuine doubt.
This principle underpins the entire ruling.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَ مَنْ وَجَدَ اِمْرَأَةٌ عَلَى فِرَاشِهِ فَوَطِيَهَا فَعَلَيْهِ الْحَدُّ لِأَنَّهُ لَا إِشْتِبَاهَ بَعْدَ طُولِ الصُّحْبَةِ فَلَمْ يَكُنِ الظَّنُّ مُسْتَتِدًا إِلَى دَلِيلِ وَهذَا لَا نَّهُ قَدْيَنَامُ عَلَى فِرَاشِهَا غَيْرُهَا مِنَ الْمَحَارِمِ الَّتِي فِي بَيْتِهَا وَكَذَا إِذَا كَانَ أَعْمَى لِأَنَّهُ يُمْكِنُهُ السَّمِيزُ بِالسُّوَالِ وَغَيْرِهِ إِلَّا إِذَا كَانَ دَعَا هَا فَاجَابَتْهُ أَجْنَبِيَّةٌ وَقَالَتْ اَنَا زَوْجَتُكَ فَوَاقَعَهَا لِأَنَّ الْإِخْبَارَ دَلِيلٌ
And whoever finds a woman on his bed and has intercourse with her, then upon him is the hadd, because there is no doubt after long companionship, so the assumption is not based on evidence. And this is because sometimes other mahram women in the house sleep on her bed. Likewise if he is blind, because he can distinguish by asking and other means, except if he called and a non-mahram woman answered and said 'I am your wife,' and he had intercourse with her, because the report is evidence.
Source pages p. 157

Commentary
This is a ruling with proof from the chapter on intercourse that necessitates hadd. It states that if a man finds a woman on his bed (not his wife) and has intercourse with her, he is liable for the hadd of zina. The reason is that after a period of living together (tul al-suhbah), there is no reasonable doubt about the identity of one's wife. The mere possibility that another woman (like a mahram relative) might be on his bed is not a sufficient excuse; he must take precautions. Even a blind man is expected to verify by asking. However, an exception is made if the man called out and a woman responded claiming to be his wife, and he relied on that report—then no hadd, because the report is a valid evidence (dalil) creating shubha.

Key Terms
طول الصحبة (tul al-suhbah): Long companionship or living together with one's wife, which makes identification certain.
دليل (dalil): Evidence or authoritative report that can create a shubha preventing hadd.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the Hanafi school, if a man finds a woman on his bed and has intercourse, but she claims to be his wife, when is hadd not applied?
A) Never; hadd always applies.
B) If she looks like his wife.
C) If he called first and she answered claiming to be his wife.
D) If he is blind.

Key Takeaway
After long companionship (tul al-suhbah), the hadd of zina is wajib on a man who has intercourse with a woman on his bed unless she verbally claimed to be his wife upon his call.

Hadd or Ta'zir for Marrying a Permanently Forbidden Woman
Intra-Hanafi dispute on hadd for intercourse with a permanently prohibited woman

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the intra-Hanafi dispute on hadd for intercourse with a permanently prohibited woman and identify the role of shubha in preventing hadd.

Core Idea
Shubha from a valid-purpose contract prevents hadd, even if the marriage is prohibited.

When a man knowingly marries a woman who is permanently forbidden to him (e.g., his mother or sister), the Hanafi school is divided on the punishment. Abu Hanifa holds that no hadd is required because the marriage contract, though invalid, still has a valid purpose—procreation—which creates a shubha (legal doubt). The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), joined by al-Shafi'i, argue that the contract is completely void, so no shubha exists, and the man must receive the full hadd penalty. This lesson unpacks the reasoning behind each position and its practical consequence.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the ruling of Abu Hanifa: no hadd, only ta'zir (discretionary punishment) if the man knew of the prohibition.
Abu Hanifa's view is based on the contract having a valid purpose.
2
Identify the opponent's view
State the position of Abu Yusuf, Muhammad, and al-Shafi'i: hadd (execution or flogging) is wajib if the man knew.
They analogize the case to marrying a male, which is completely void.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
Understand their dalil: the contract did not fulfill its 'mahall' (place/object) because the woman is not a valid object for the contract's ruling (permissibility). Thus the contract is void, no shubha, hadd applies.
Their definition of mahall: that which accepts the ruling of the contract (tahlil/permissibility).
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
Understand Abu Hanifa's rebuttal: the mahall is that which accepts the purpose of the contract (procreation). Since women are capable of procreation, the contract is valid in purpose, creating a shubha even though it fails to produce permissibility.
Shubha: 'what resembles the established, not the very established'—the contract resembles a valid marriage in that it aims at procreation.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: under Abu Hanifa, the man receives a discretionary punishment (ta'zir); under the Sahibayn, he receives the fixed hadd (which could be death by stoning if he is muhsan, or 100 lashes if not).
This difference affects actual court rulings in Hanafi jurisdictions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَ مَنْ تَزَوَّجَ امْرَأَةٌ لَا يَحِلُّ لَهُ نِكَاحُهَا فَوَطِيَهَا لَا يَجِبُ عَلَيْهِ الْحَدُّ عِنْدَ أَبِي حَنِيفَةَ لَكِنَّهُ يُرْجِعُ عُقُوبَةٌ إِذَا كَانَ عَلِيمٌ بِذَالِكَ وَقَالَ أَبُو يُوسُفَ وَمُحَمَّدٌ وَالشَّافِعِيُّ عَلَيْهِ الْحَدُّ إِذَا كَانَ عَالِمًا بِذَالِكَ لِأَنَّهُ عَقْدٌ لَمْ يُصَادِقْ مَحَلَّةً فَيَدْعُو كَمَا إِذَا أُضِيفَ إِلَى الذُّكُورِ وَهذَا لِاَنَّ مَحَلَّ التَّصَرُّفِ مَا يَكُونُ مَحَلَّاً لِحُكْمِهِ وَحُكْمُهُ الْحِلُّ وَهِيَ مِنَ الْمُحَرَّمَاتِ وَعِنْدَ أَبِي حَنِيفَةَ أَنَّ الْعَقْدَ صَادَقَ مَحَلَّهُ لِأَنَّ مَحَلَّ التَّصَرُّفِ مَا يَقْبَلُ مَقْصُودَهُ وَالأنْثَى مِنْ بَنَاتِ بَنِي آدَمَ قَابَلَةً لِلتَّوَالُدِ وَهُوَ الْمَقْصُودُ فَكَانَ يَنْبَغِي أَنْ يُنْعَقِدَ فِي حَقِّ جَمِيع الْأَحْكَامِ إِلَّا إِنَّهُ تَقَاعَدَ عَنْ إِفَادَةِ حَقِيقَةِ الْحِلِّ فَيُوْرِثُ الشَّبْهَةَ لِاَنَّ الشُّبْهَةَ مَا يَشْبَهُ الثَّابِتَ لَانَفْسَ الثَّابِتِ إِلَّا أَنَّهُ ارْتَكَبَ جَرِيْمَةً وَلَيْسَ فِيهَا حَدٌ مُقَدَّرٌ فَيُعَزَّرُ
And whoever marries a woman whose marriage is not permissible for him and then has intercourse with her, the hadd is not obligatory upon him according to Abu Hanifa, but he is subjected to a punishment if he knew that. And Abu Yusuf, Muhammad, and al-Shafi'i said: upon him is the hadd if he knew that, because it is a contract that did not fulfill its place (mahall), so it is null like when added to males. This is because the place of a transaction is that which is a place for its ruling, and its ruling is permissibility, and she is from the forbidden ones. According to Abu Hanifa, the contract did fulfill its place because the place of a transaction is that which accepts its purpose, and the female from the daughters of Adam is capable of procreation, which is the purpose, so it should be effective in all rulings except that it fell short in conferring true permissibility, so it creates a shubha because shubha is what resembles the established, not the very established. But he committed a crime, and there is no prescribed hadd for it, so he is ta'zir.
Source pages p. 157

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute (with al-Shafi'i also mentioned) regarding intercourse with a woman who is permanently prohibited in marriage (e.g., mother, sister). Abu Hanifa holds that no hadd is wajib, but the man is given a severe ta'zir (discretionary punishment) if he knowingly married her. The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) and al-Shafi'i say hadd is wajib because the marriage contract is invalid (mahall is not suitable), similar to marrying a male. Abu Hanifa argues that the contract is valid in form because the woman is capable of childbirth, which is the purpose of marriage, but it fails to achieve the effect of tahrim (prohibition) due to the legal impediment, thus creating a shubha. Since shubha prevents hadd, he is only subject to ta'zir. The text notes that according to some later sources, the fatwa is on the Sahibayn's view, but other commentarial works prefer Abu Hanifa's view.

Key Terms
محل التصرف (mahall al-tasarruf): The subject of a contract; here, the suitability of the woman for marriage.
تعزير (ta'zir): A discretionary punishment for crimes without hadd.
شبهة (shubha): A legal doubt that prevents the application of hadd.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, why is hadd not wajib for intercourse with a permanently prohibited woman?
A) Because she is not his wife.
B) Because the contract is valid but fails to make her halal, creating shubha.
C) Because he was ignorant of the prohibition.
D) Because the crime is not proven by two witnesses.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, shubha (legal doubt) can prevent hadd even if the act is a major sin; the definition of mahall determines whether shubha exists.
