Woman's Hadd When Enabling Minor or Insane Man
Ruling on hadd for woman enabling minor or insane man

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on hadd for a woman who willingly enables a minor or insane man to commit zina, and explain the reasoning based on the requirement of taklif for the active partner.

Core Idea
Hadd requires the active partner to be legally responsible; a minor or insane man's act does not trigger hadd for either party.

This lesson explores a key disagreement between the Hanafi school and other scholars regarding the application of the hadd punishment for zina when the active partner lacks legal capacity. The text from al-Hidayah presents the Hanafi position and its rationale, contrasted with the opposing view. Understanding this dispute clarifies a fundamental principle: who must be the actual perpetrator for the hadd to apply.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and the Hanafi ruling
Read the first part of the source: when a minor or insane man commits zina with a woman who willingly enabled him. State the Hanafi ruling: no hadd on either.
This is the surface-level outcome.
2
Recognize the opposing view
Note that Zufar, al-Shafi‘i, and a narration of Abu Yusuf hold that the woman is punished. Understand that they view each person as accountable for their own act.
The opposition focuses on the woman's agency.
3
Understand the Hanafi proof: act attributed to man as origin
The text explains that the act of zina is actualized by the man (he is wati' and zani); she is the locus (mawtu'a). The term 'zaniya' for her is metaphorical or because she is a cause.
This linguistic argument shows the man is the primary agent.
4
Analyze the condition: the doer must be mukallaf
Hadd attaches only when the act is done by someone who is legally addressed (mukhatab) with the prohibition. The minor/insane is not, so their act is not a punishable zina.
This is the key principle: taklif is a prerequisite for hadd.
5
Note the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf)
In a case where a sane woman enables a minor boy, Hanafi school applies no hadd to her, while the opposing schools would punish her.
This difference has real-world implications in judicial settings.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قَالَ وَإِذَا زَنَى الصَّبِيُّ أَوِ الْمَجْنُونُ بِامْرَأَةٍ طَاوَعَتْهُ فَلَا حَدَّ عَلَيْهِ وَلَا عَلَيْهَا وَقَالَ زُفَرُ وَالشَّافِعِيُّ يَجِبُ الْحَدُّ عَلَيْهَا وَهُوَ رِوَايَةٌ عَنْ أَبِي يُوسُفَ وَإِنْ زَنَى صَحِيحٌ بِمَجْنُونَةٍ أَوْ صَغِيرَةٍ تُجَامَعُ مِثْلُهَا حُدَّ الرَّجُلُ خَاصَّةً وَهَذَا بِالْإِجْمَاعِ لَهُمَا أَنَّ الْعُذْرَ مِنْ جَانِبِهَا لَا يُوجِبُ سُقُوطَ الْحَدِّ مِنْ جَانِبِهِ فَكَذَا الْعُذْرُ مِنْ جَانِبِهِ وَهَذَا لِأَنَّ كُلًّا مِنْهُمَا مُؤَاخَذٌ بِفِعْلِهِ وَلَنَا أَنَّ فِعْلَ الزِّنَاءِ يَتَحَقَّقُ مِنْهُ وَإِنَّمَا هِيَ مَحَلُّ الْفِعْلِ وَلِهَذَا يُسَمَّى هُوَ وَاطِئًا وَزَانِيًا وَالْمَرْأَةُ مَوْطُوءَةٌ وَمَزْنِيٌّ بِهَا إِلَّا أَنَّهَا سُمِّيَتْ زَانِيَةً مَجَازًا تَسْمِيَةٌ لِلْمَفْعُولِ بِاسْمِ الْفَاعِلِ كَالرَّاضِيَةِ فِي مَعْنَى الْمَرْضِيَّةِ أَوْ لِكَوْنِهَا مُسَبِّبَةً بِالتَّمْكِينِ فَيَتَعَلَّقُ الْحَدُّ فِي حَقِّهَا بِالتَّمْكِينِ مِنْ فِعْلِ قَبِيحِ الزِّنَاءِ وَهُوَ فِعْلٌ مَنْ هُوَ مُخَاطَبٌ بِالْكَتِّ عَنْهُ وَمُؤْتَمٌ عَلَى مُبَاشَرَتِهِ وَفِعْلُ الصَّبِيِّ لَيْسَ بِهَذِهِ الصِّفَةِ فَلَا يُنَاطُ بِهِ الْحَدُّ
He said: And when a minor boy or an insane man commits zina with a woman who willingly enabled him, then there is no hadd upon him and no hadd upon her. And Zufar and al-Shafi‘i said: The hadd becomes obligatory upon her, and this is also a narration from Abu Yusuf. And if a sane man commits zina with an insane woman or a minor girl who is fit for intercourse, the man is punished specifically, and this is by consensus. For them two (Zufar and al-Shafi‘i), the excuse from her side does not necessitate the fall of the hadd from his side; likewise the excuse from his side (does not necessitate fall from her side). And this is because each of them is accountable for his own act. And for us (Hanafis), the act of zina is actualized from him (the man), and she is merely the locus of the act. For this reason, he is called ‘wati’ (coitus perpetrator) and ‘zani’ (adulterer), while the woman is called ‘mawtu’a’ (the one with whom intercourse is done) and ‘mazni biha’ (the one with whom zina is committed). However, she is called ‘zaniya’ (adulteress) metaphorically, naming the object with the name of the subject, like ‘al-radiya’ (the willing one) meaning ‘al-mardiyya’ (the one who is pleased with), or because she is a cause (musabbiba) through enabling. Thus the hadd pertains to her due to her enabling of the ugly act of zina, and (zina) is an act of one who is addressed with the prohibition of it and who is trusted (i.e., legally responsible) for committing it. The act of the minor is not of this description, so hadd is not attached to it.
Source pages p. 163

Commentary
This passage presents a dispute regarding the hadd of zina when the active partner is a minor or insane. According to the Hanafi school (as stated in the matn), if a minor or insane man commits zina with a woman who willingly enables him, neither the man nor the woman are subject to hadd. This is because the man is not legally responsible (not mukallaf), and the woman, though enabling, is considered a follower; the act of zina is attributed to the man as the origin. The woman is merely the locus of the act. Even though she is called a zaniya metaphorically or because she is a cause, the hadd is not attached to her because the act itself (from the man) is not a punishable act. Zufar and al-Shafi‘i, and in one narration Abu Yusuf, hold that the woman should be punished because she is a responsible agent who caused the act. The text then explains the Hanafi proof: the act of zina is actualized by the man; he is the one committing the act, while she is the subject of the act. Calling her zaniya is metaphorical. For the hadd to apply, the act must be from someone who is addressed with the prohibition and who is legally responsible for committing it. The minor/insane are not of this description, so their act does not trigger hadd for the woman. The passage also notes consensus that if a sane man commits zina with an insane woman or minor girl, only the man is punished, as the woman has an excuse. The Hanafi position is consistent: the excuse of the active partner (origin) prevents hadd for both.

Key Terms
مُخَاطَبٌ (mukhatab): A person who is legally addressed by the Shari‘ah (i.e., accountable, having legal capacity – taklif).
تَمْكِين (tamkin): Enabling, i.e., the woman giving the man access to commit zina with her.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi view stated in the passage, why is a woman who willingly enables a minor boy to commit zina not subject to hadd?
A) Because the woman is coerced
B) Because the minor boy's act is not a punishable zina since he is not legally responsible
C) Because the woman is a dhimmi
D) Because the minor boy is not her husband

Key Takeaway
The hadd of zina is only applicable when the active partner is legally responsible (mukallaf); the woman's enabling does not turn a non-punishable act into a punishable one.

Hadd for Minor/Insane Male with Willing Woman: Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Ruling and ikhtilaf on hadd for minor/insane zina with willing woman

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on hadd when a minor or insane male commits zina with a willing woman, explain the Hanafi proof based on attribution of the act, and distinguish it from the opposing positions of Zufar and al-Shafi'i.

Core Idea
No hadd on either party: the act is the male's, and a minor/insane person cannot be held liable.

This passage addresses a critical question in Hudud: if a minor or insane male engages in zina with a willing adult woman, does the woman face hadd? While some scholars hold her liable because she is a willing participant, the Hanafi school maintains that no hadd is imposed on either party. Understanding why reveals a key principle about legal capacity and the attribution of acts.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi ruling
Identify the ruling in the matn: no hadd on the minor/insane male and no hadd on the willing woman.
This is the mufta bihi position.
2
Identify the opposing position
Zufar, al-Shafi'i, and a narration from Abu Yusuf hold that the woman must be punished with hadd.
They argue that each person is accountable for their own act.
3
Trace the opponent's proof
The opponents invoke qiyas from the case of a healthy man with an insane woman: if her excuse does not remove his hadd, his excuse should not remove hers.
This is based on the principle of individual accountability.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
The Hanafis reject the qiyas: the act is primarily the male's; the woman is only the locus (mahall). The term 'zaniyah' is metaphorical. A minor's act is not legally prohibited for him, so no hadd attaches to his enabling.
The illa of individual accountability is not present because the primary act is not a sin.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
In practice, a Hanafi judge acquits both; a Shafi'i judge would punish the woman alone.
This difference has real-world consequences in judicial decisions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قَالَ وَإِذَا زَنَى الصَّبِيُّ أَوِ الْمَجْنُونُ بِامْرَأَةٍ طَاوَعَتْهُ فَلَا حَدَّ عَلَيْهِ وَلَا عَلَيْهَا وَقَالَ زُفَرُ وَالشَّافِعِيُّ يَجِبُ الْحَدُّ عَلَيْهَا وَهُوَ رِوَايَةٌ عَنْ أَبِي يُوسُفَ وَإِنْ زَنَى صَحِيحٌ بِمَجْنُونَةٍ أَوْ صَغِيرَةٍ تُجَامَعُ مِثْلُهَا حُدَّ الرَّجُلُ خَاصَّةً وَهَذَا بِالْإِجْمَاعِ لَهُمَا أَنَّ الْعُذْرَ مِنْ جَانِبِهَا لَا يُوجِبُ سُقُوطَ الْحَدِّ مِنْ جَانِبِهِ فَكَذَا الْعُذْرُ مِنْ جَانِبِهِ وَهَذَا لِأَنَّ كُلًّا مِنْهُمَا مُؤَاخَذٌ بِفِعْلِهِ وَلَنَا أَنَّ فِعْلَ الزِّنَا يَتَحَقَّقُ مِنْهُ وَإِنَّمَا هِيَ مَحَلُّ الْفِعْلِ وَلِهَذَا يُسَمَّى هُوَ وَاطِئًا وَزَانِيًا وَالْمَرْأَةُ مَوْطُوعَةٌ وَمَزْنِيٌّ بِهَا إِلَّا أَنَّهَا سُمِّيَتْ زَانِيَةً مَجَازًا تَسْمِيَةً لِلْمَفْعُولِ بِاسْمِ الْفَاعِلِ كَالرَّاضِيَةِ فِي مَعْنَى الْمَرْضِيَّةِ أَوْ لِكَوْنِهَا مُسَبِّبَةً بِالتَّمْكِينِ فَيَتَعَلَّقُ الْحَدُّ فِي حَقِّهَا بِالتَّمْكِينِ مِنْ قَبِيحِ الزِّنَا وَهُوَ فِعْلٌ مَنْ هُوَ مُخَاطَبٌ بِالْكِفِّ عَنْهُ وَمُؤْتَمَنٌ عَلَى مُبَاشَرَتِهِ وَفِعْلُ الصَّبِيِّ لَيْسَ بِهَذِهِ الصِّفَةِ فَلَا يُنَاطُ بِهِ الْحَدُّ
He said: When a minor or an insane person commits zina with a woman who is willing, there is no hadd upon him and no hadd upon her. Zufar and al-Shafi'i said: The hadd is obligatory upon her, and it is a narration from Abu Yusuf. And if a sound (adult) man commits zina with an insane woman or a young girl who is fit for intercourse, the man alone is punished with hadd, and this is by consensus. Their (the opponents') proof is that if an excuse on her side does not cause the hadd to fall from his side, then similarly an excuse on his side should not cause the hadd to fall from her side, because each of them is held accountable for his/her own act. Our proof is that the act of zina actually takes place from him; she is only the locus of the act. Therefore he is called wati' (one who has intercourse) and zani (fornicator), while the woman is called mawtu'ah (the one with whom intercourse is done) and mazniyyun biha (the one with whom fornication is committed). However, she is called zaniyah (fornicatress) metaphorically, by naming the object with the name of the subject, like al-radiyah (the accepting one) in the meaning of al-mardiyyah (the accepted one), or because she is the cause by granting access. So the hadd applies to her due to her granting access to the abominable act of zina, which is an act of one who is addressed with prohibition and entrusted with its commission. The act of a minor is not of this description, so the hadd is not attached to it.
Source pages p. 163

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute concerning the liability for hadd (fixed penalty for illicit sexual intercourse) when the male participant is a minor (صبي) or an insane person (مجنون) and the female participant is a legally competent woman who willingly (طاوعته) engages in the act. The ruling, attributed to Imam Muhammad (the author of the original matn), states that no hadd is imposed on either party: neither on the minor/insane male, because they lack legal capacity, nor on the willing woman, because the act is primarily attributed to the male. This is opposed by Imam Zufar and Imam al-Shafi'i, who hold that the woman should be punished with hadd because she is a willing participant. There is also a narration from Abu Yusuf supporting this view. The passage then presents an analogous case: if a healthy adult male commits zina with an insane woman or a minor girl who is fit for intercourse, there is unanimous consensus (إجماع) that only the man is punished, not the woman. The opponents use this as evidence for their position: if the woman's excuse (insanity or minority) does not remove the hadd from the man, then the man's excuse (minority/insanity) should not remove hadd from the woman. They argue that each person is responsible for their own action. The Hanafi response (ولنا) is that the act of zina is actually performed by the man—he is the active agent (واطئ and زانٍ), while the woman is merely the locus (محل الفعل) and is called mawtu'ah (the one with whom intercourse is done). The Quranic term 'zaniyah' (fornicatress) is applied to her metaphorically (مجازا), either by naming the object with the name of the subject (like calling the 'accepted one' 'accepting') or because she causes the act by granting access (تمكين). Thus, the hadd in her case is attached to her act of enabling the zina, which is an act of someone who is addressed by the prohibition and entrusted with avoidance. The minor's act is not of this nature—he is not addressed—so hadd is not attached to it. This reasoning illustrates the Hanafi principle that the primary responsibility for the act falls on the one who performs it, not on the one who merely facilitates.

Key Terms
طاوعته (tawā'athu): She willingly submitted to the act, without coercion.
محل الفعل (mahall al-fi'l): The locus or receptacle of the act; here the woman is described as the place where the act occurs, not the active agent.
مجازًا (majāzan): Metaphorically; the term 'zaniyah' (fornicatress) is applied to the woman metaphorically, not literally.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position in this passage, what is the ruling on hadd when a minor boy commits zina with a willing adult woman?
A) Both the minor and the woman are punished with hadd.
B) Only the minor is punished with hadd.
C) Neither the minor nor the woman is punished with hadd.
D) Only the woman is punished with hadd.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, the liability for hadd in zina is primarily attached to the active agent; when the active agent lacks legal capacity, no hadd falls on either party.

Coercion into Zina: Sultan vs. Non-Sultan – Intra-Hanafi Ikhtilaf
Intra-Hanafi dispute on hadd for coerced zina

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on hadd for a man coerced into zina by the Sultan and by a private individual, and explain the reasoning behind Abu Hanifa's position versus the Sahibayn.

Core Idea
Coercion by the Sultan creates a shubha that drops hadd; coercion by a non-Sultan does not, because escape is possible.

This passage from the Hidayah addresses a critical intra-Hanafi dispute: does the identity of the coercer affect the imposition of hadd for zina? Imam Muhammad states that coercion by the Sultan nullifies the penalty, but Abu Hanifa initially disagreed before retracting. The more significant dispute is with the Sahibayn over coercion by non-Sultan. Let us walk through the legal reasoning.

Guided Path
1
State the ruling for Sultan coercion
Identify the explicit ruling in the matn: no hadd is imposed on a man coerced by the Sultan into zina.
This is the final position of Imam Muhammad.
2
Trace Abu Hanifa's retraction on Sultan coercion
Abu Hanifa initially held that hadd is imposed because erection indicates willingness, but retracted due to the possibility of natural erection (like sleep) creating a shubha.
The retraction aligns the school on the Sultan case.
3
Contrast with non-Sultan coercion: Abu Hanifa's view
For coercion by a private individual, Abu Hanifa holds hadd is imposed. His proof: the victim can seek help from the Sultan, the community, or defend himself with weapons.
This relies on the principle 'al-nadir la hukma lahu' (rare occurrences have no legal weight).
4
Identify the Sahibayn's opposing view
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad argue that coercion drops hadd regardless of the source, because the essential element is fear of death (khawf al-halak), which can exist from any coercer.
They see the shubha as arising from the threat itself, not the identity.
5
Analyze the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: a judge following Abu Hanifa would impose hadd on a man coerced by a non-Sultan; following Sahibayn, he would not.
The mufta bihi in the madhhab is Abu Hanifa's view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قَالَ وَمَنْ أَكْرَهَهُ السُّلْطَانُ حَتَّى زَنَى فَلَا حَدَّ عَلَيْهِ وَكَانَ أَبُو حَنِيفَةَ يَقُولُ أَوَّلًا يُحَدُّ وَهُوَ قَوْلُ زُفَرَ لِأَنَّ الزِّنَا مِنَ الرَّجُلِ لَا يَكُونُ إِلَّا بَعْدَ انْتِشَارِ الْآلَةِ وَذَلِكَ دَلِيلُ الطَّوَاعِيَةِ ثُمَّ رَجَعَ عَنْهُ فَقَالَ لَا حَدَّ عَلَيْهِ لِأَنَّ سَبَبَهُ الْمُلْجِئَ قَائِمٌ ظَاهِرًا وَالِانْتِشَارُ دَلِيلٌ مُتَرَدِّدٌ لِأَنَّهُ قَدْ يَكُونُ مِنْ غَيْرِ قَصْدٍ لِأَنَّ الِانْتِشَارَ قَدْ يَكُونُ طَبْعًا لَا طَوْعًا كَمَا فِي النَّائِمِ فَأَوْرَثَ شُبْهَةً وَإِنْ أَكْرَهَهُ غَيْرُ السُّلْطَانِ حُدَّ عِنْدَ أَبِي حَنِيفَةَ وَقَالَا لَا يُحَدُّ لِأَنَّ الْإِكْرَاهَ عِنْدَهُمَا قَدْ يَتَحَقَّقُ مِنْ غَيْرِ السُّلْطَانِ لِأَنَّ الْمُوجِبَ خَوْفُ الْهَلَاكِ وَإِنَّهُ يَتَحَقَّقُ مِنْ غَيْرِهِ وَلَهُ أَنَّ الْإِكْرَاهَ مِنْ غَيْرِهِ لَا يَدُومُ إِلَّا نَادِرًا لِتَمَكُّنِهِ مِنَ الِاسْتِعَانَةِ بِالسُّلْطَانِ أَوْ بِجَمَاعَةِ الْمُسْلِمِينَ وَتَمَكُّنِهِ دَفْعُهُ بِنَفْسِهِ بِالسِّلَاحِ وَالنَّادِرُ لَا حُكْمَ لَهُ فَلَا يَسْقُطُ بِهِ الْحَدُّ بِخِلَافِ السُّلْطَانِ لِأَنَّهُ لَا يُمْكِنُهُ الِاسْتِعَانَةُ بِغَيْرِهِ وَلَا الْخُرُوجُ بِالسِّلَاحِ عَلَيْهِ فَافْتَرَقَا
He said: Whoever is coerced by the Sultan until he commits zina, there is no hadd upon him. Abu Hanifa initially used to say that he is punished with hadd, and this is the statement of Zufar, because zina from a man does not occur except after the erection of the organ, and that is evidence of willingness. Then he retracted from that and said: There is no hadd upon him, because his compelling cause (al-mulji') is outwardly present, and the erection is an ambiguous evidence, because it may occur without intent—for erection may occur naturally, not voluntarily, as in the case of a sleeping person—so this creates a legal ambiguity (shubha). And if someone other than the Sultan coerces him, then according to Abu Hanifa he is punished with hadd. They (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said: He is not punished with hadd, because according to them coercion can occur from other than the Sultan, for the cause is fear of destruction, and that can occur from others. His (Abu Hanifa's) proof is that coercion from other than the Sultan does not persist except rarely, because he is able to seek help from the Sultan or from a group of Muslims, and he is able to repel it himself with weapons. And a rare occurrence has no legal weight, so the hadd does not fall due to it. Unlike the Sultan, because he cannot seek help from anyone else, nor can he fight against the Sultan with weapons, so they differ.
Source pages p. 164

Commentary
This passage addresses the ruling on hadd (fixed penalty for zina) when a man is coerced into committing zina. It distinguishes between coercion by the Sultan (ruler) and coercion by someone else. Imam Muhammad states in the matn that if the Sultan coerces a man to commit zina, no hadd is imposed on that man. Abu Hanifa initially held that such a man should be punished with hadd (and Imam Zufar agreed), reasoning that the occurrence of an erection indicates willingness. However, Abu Hanifa later retracted this view and adopted the ruling of no hadd, because the presence of the Sultan's coercion as an external compelling cause (السبب الملجئ) is apparent and overrides the inference from erection. Erection can sometimes occur naturally (as during sleep) without voluntary consent, so it does not conclusively prove willingness; thus a legal ambiguity (شبهة) arises, which prevents the imposition of hadd. When the coercion comes from someone other than the Sultan, the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) hold that the coercion is sufficient to drop the hadd, because the essential element is fear of death (خوف الهلاك), which can exist regardless of the source. Abu Hanifa disagrees and maintains that the coerced person is liable for hadd. His reasoning is that coercion by a non-Sultan is rarely enduring—the victim can seek help from the Sultan, the Muslim community, or even resist with weapons. Since rare occurrences are not given legal consideration (النادر لا حكم له), the hadd is not dropped. The difference between the two cases (Sultan vs. non-Sultan) lies in the possibility of escape: against the Sultan, there is no recourse, so the coercion is deemed absolute; against others, the ability to resist exists, so the coercion is not considered a complete excuse.

Key Terms
إكراه (ikrāh): Coercion that forces a person to commit an act against his will; legally, it must involve a threat of death or severe harm.
شبهة (shubhah): Legal ambiguity or doubt that prevents the imposition of criminal penalties (hudud).
النادر (al-nādir): A rare or infrequent occurrence; it is not given legal consideration (لا حكم له) in establishing general rulings.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa's final position, what is the ruling on a man who is coerced into zina by a private individual (not the Sultan)?
A) He is not liable for hadd because coercion removes responsibility.
B) He is liable for hadd because he could have sought help or resisted.
C) He is liable only if he enjoyed the act.
D) The hadd is dropped if the threat was of death.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, coercion by the Sultan drops the hadd for zina due to a shubha, but coercion by a private individual does not, because the victim can realistically seek recourse.

Hadd for Zina by a Minor or Insane Person
States ruling on hadd for minor/insane and consenting woman, with ikhtilaf

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on hadd when a minor or insane person commits zina with a consenting adult woman, and explain the dispute with Zufar and al-Shafi'i.

Core Idea
No hadd for the incapacitated or his partner—the act lacks the legal quality of zina from the perpetrator's side.

This passage addresses a critical question in Hanafi criminal law: when does zina incur the hadd punishment? The text presents the Hanafi position that no hadd is imposed on either party if the male lacks legal capacity due to minority or insanity, even if the woman is an adult and consents. It then notes the disagreement of Zufar and al-Shafi'i, who would punish the woman. Understanding this ruling requires examining the role of legal capacity (taklif) in defining the crime of zina.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties and capacities
Note that the male is a minor or insane (lacking legal capacity), while the female is an adult and sane, and she consents.
Consent is relevant for distinguishing from rape, but does not itself trigger hadd in the Hanafi view.
2
State the Hanafi ruling
The Hanafi verdict: no hadd on the man (because he is not mukallaf) and no hadd on the woman (because the act is not legally zina from the man's side).
The act is still sinful but not subject to the fixed punishment.
3
Recognize the ikhtilaf
Zufar and al-Shafi'i hold the opposite: the woman should receive hadd because she is a responsible adult who willingly participated.
This disagreement stems from different conceptualizations of zina and the conditions for hadd.
4
Explain the Hanafi legal basis
The Hanafi school requires that the act be considered zina from both parties for hadd to apply. Since the man's act does not meet the definition due to his lack of capacity, the woman's role is not independent zina but merely a venue (mahall).
This principle is rooted in the maxim that hadd is ward off by doubts (shubuhat).
5
Discuss the practical consequence
The thamarah al-khilaf is clear: under Hanafi law the woman escapes hadd entirely; under Shafi'i law she would be liable to stoning or lashing.
This difference has real implications in jurisdictions applying Islamic criminal law.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قَالَ وَإِذَا زَنَى الصَّبِيُّ أَوِ الْمَجْنُونُ بِامْرَأَةٍ طَاوَعَتْهُ فَلَا حَدَّ عَلَيْهِ وَلَا عَلَيْهَا وَقَالَ زُفَرُ وَالشَّافِعِيُّ يَجِبُ الْحَدُّ
He said: When a minor or insane person commits zina with a woman who consents, there is no hadd on him nor on her. And Zufar and al-Shafi'i said: hadd is obligatory.
Source pages p. 164

Commentary
This passage states the Hanafi ruling on zina committed by a minor or an insane person with a consenting adult woman. The ruling is that no hadd punishment is imposed on either the man or the woman. This is because the minor and the insane person lack legal capacity (ahliyyah) for criminal liability—their actions are not considered intentional crimes that warrant hadd. The woman, though an adult and sane, is not punished because her act of consent does not constitute zina in the legal sense when the man lacks capacity. The passage then notes that Imam Zufar and Imam al-Shafi'i disagree, holding that hadd is obligatory on the woman because she is a responsible adult who willingly engaged in the act. The Hanafi response, though not detailed here, is based on the principle that the woman's act is not independent zina; the act is attributed to the man's action, and since the man's action does not constitute zina legally, the woman's consent does not result in hadd. This difference stems from how each school defines the nature of zina and the capacity required for the act.

Key Terms
الصَّبِيُّ (al-sabiyy): A male minor who has not reached puberty, considered lacking full legal capacity for criminal liability.
الْمَجْنُونُ (al-majnun): An insane person, completely lacking legal capacity, thus no hadd for actions during insanity.
طَاوَعَتْهُ (tawā'athu): She consented willingly; indicates the woman was not coerced and agreed to the act.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, why is no hadd imposed on the woman who consents to intercourse with a minor who has not reached puberty?
A) Because the woman's consent was not given freely
B) Because the act is not legally considered zina from the man's side, and the woman is not the active agent
C) Because the minor can be punished instead
D) Because the woman can claim marriage

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, the capacity of both parties is necessary for the act to be considered zina for hadd; the woman's consent does not alone create liability if the man lacks capacity.

Hadd for Coerced Zina: The Sultan vs. Private Coercion
Explains the Hanafi ruling on hadd for coerced zina with a sultan vs non-sultan distinction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on hadd for coerced zina, explain Abu Hanifa's final position and its evolution, and distinguish the ruling for sultan vs. non-sultan coercion.

Core Idea
Only irresistible coercion (sultan) creates a doubt that drops hadd; non-sultan coercion is resistible, so hadd remains.

This passage delves into a nuanced area of Hanafi criminal law: the effect of coercion (ikrah) on the hadd punishment for zina. It presents a dispute within the school, focusing on whether the coercer is the sultan (ruler) or a private individual. Understanding this distinction is crucial because it determines when the victim is considered legally complicit and when the hadd is averted by doubt (shubha).

Guided Path
1
Identify the core ruling
The matn states: whoever is coerced by the sultan until he commits zina, no hadd on him. This is the starting point.
This ruling is the consensus after Abu Hanifa's retraction.
2
Trace Abu Hanifa's opinion evolution
Initially, Abu Hanifa held that hadd is imposed because erection indicates willingness (Zufar agreed). He later retracted, reasoning that coercion is a compelling cause and erection is an equivocal proof (dalil mutaraddid).
The retraction is key: it shows the power of shubha in averting hadd.
3
Understand the reasoning for the change
The compelling cause (sabab mulji') is manifest externally, and erection can occur naturally (like in sleep), so it does not prove willingness. This creates a doubt that prevents hadd.
This reasoning applies only to sultan coercion because the sultan's coercion is irresistible.
4
Contrast the ruling for non-sultan coercion
Abu Hanifa holds that hadd is imposed when a non-sultan coerces, because the victim can seek help from the sultan, the community, or defend himself. The Sahibayn disagree, arguing that coercion from anyone can be life-threatening.
The practical difference: under Abu Hanifa, hadd is applied; under Sahibayn, it is not.
5
Evaluate the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: a man coerced by a robber into zina would be punished for hadd according to Abu Hanifa, but not according to Sahibayn. The mufta bihi is Abu Hanifa's view.
This illustrates how theoretical differences affect actual rulings.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قَالَ وَمَنْ أَكْرَهَهُ السُّلْطَانُ حَتَّى زَنَى فَلَا حَدَّ عَلَيْهِ وَكَانَ أَبُو حَنِيفَةَ يَقُولُ أَوَّلًا يُحَدُّ وَهُوَ قَوْلُ زُفَرَ لِأَنَّ الزِّنَاءَ مِنَ الرَّجُلِ لَا يَكُونُ إِلَّا بَعْدَ انْتِشَارِ الْآلَةِ وَذَلِكَ دَلِيلُ الطَّوَاعِيَةِ ثُمَّ رَجَعَ عَنْهُ فَقَالَ لَا حَدَّ عَلَيْهِ لِأَنَّ سَبَبَهُ الْمُلْجِئَ قَائِمٌ ظَاهِرًا وَالِانْتِشَارُ دَلِيلٌ مُتَرَدِّدٌ لِأَنَّهُ قَدْ يَكُونُ مِنْ غَيْرِ قَصْدٍ إِذِ الِانْتِشَارُ قَدْ يَكُونُ طَبْعًا لَا طَوْعًا كَمَا فِي النَّائِمِ فَأَوْرَثَ شُبْهَةً وَإِنْ أَكْرَهَهُ غَيْرُ السُّلْطَانِ حُدَّ عِنْدَ أَبِي حَنِيفَةَ وَقَالَا لَا يُحَدُّ لِأَنَّ الْإِكْرَاهَ عِنْدَهُمَا قَدْ يَتَحَقَّقُ مِنْ غَيْرِ السُّلْطَانِ لِأَنَّ الْمُؤْتَرَ خَوْفُ الْهَلَاكِ وَإِنَّهُ يَتَحَقَّقُ مِنْ غَيْرِهِ وَلَهُ أَنَّ الْإِكْرَاهَ مِنْ غَيْرِهِ لَا يَدُومُ إِلَّا نَادِرًا لِتَمَكُّنِهِ مِنَ الْإِسْتِعَانَةِ بِالسُّلْطَانِ أَوْ بِجَمَاعَةِ الْمُسْلِمِينَ وَتَمَكُّنِهِ دَفْعُهُ بِنَفْسِهِ بِالسِّلَاحِ وَالنَّادِرُ لَا حُكْمَ لَهُ فَلَا يَسْقُطُ بِهِ الْحَدُّ بِخِلَافِ السُّلْطَانِ لِأَنَّهُ لَا يُمْكِنُهُ الْإِسْتِعَانَةُ بِغَيْرِهِ وَلَا الْخُرُوجُ بِالسِّلَاحِ عَلَيْهِ فَافْتَرَقَا
He said: Whoever is coerced by the sultan until he commits zina, there is no hadd on him. Abu Hanifa initially used to say: he is to be punished, and that is the saying of Zufar, because zina from a man only occurs after erection of the organ, and that is evidence of willingness. Then he retracted from that and said: no hadd on him because the compelling cause is manifestly present, and the erection is an equivocal proof, because it may occur without intention since erection may be natural not voluntary, as in the case of a sleeper, so it creates a doubt. And if one other than the sultan coerces him, he is to be punished according to Abu Hanifa, and they (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said: he is not to be punished, because coercion according to them can be actualized from other than the sultan, since the threatened person fears destruction, and that is realized from other than him. And for him (Abu Hanifa) is that coercion from other than him does not persist except rarely, due to his ability to seek help from the sultan or a group of Muslims, and his ability to repel it himself with a weapon, and the rare has no ruling, so hadd is not dropped by it. Unlike the sultan, because he cannot seek help from others nor go out with a weapon against him, so they differ.
Source pages p. 164

Commentary
This passage presents a detailed ruling on coercion (ikrah) in zina, specifically distinguishing between coercion by the sultan (ruler) and by a private individual. The core ruling in the matn is that if the sultan coerces a man into committing zina, no hadd is imposed. The passage then narrates the evolution of Abu Hanifa's opinion: initially, he held that hadd is still required because the physical sign of erection indicates willingness; this was also the view of Zufar. Later, Abu Hanifa retracted, reasoning that the coercion itself is a compelling cause (sabab mulji') that is manifest, and the erection is an equivocal proof (dalil mutaraddid) because it can occur naturally without intent, as in sleep. This creates a doubt (shubha) that prevents hadd. The passage then addresses coercion by a non-sultan individual: according to Abu Hanifa, hadd is still imposed because the coercion from a private person is not continuous—the victim can seek help from the sultan, the Muslim community, or defend himself with arms. Rare exceptions have no legal effect (al-nadir la hukma lahu), so hadd is not waived. However, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the Sahibayn) hold that hadd is also dropped for coercion by a non-sultan, as the threat of death can come from anyone. Abu Hanifa's distinguishing rationale is the victim's practical inability to resist the sultan, making the coercion effectively irresistible.

Key Terms
الْإِكْرَاهُ (al-ikrāh): Coercion that compels a person to act against his will under threat of death or severe harm.
السُّلْطَانُ (al-sulṭān): The ruling authority or sultan, whose coercion is considered irresistible.
انْتِشَارُ الْآلَةِ (intishār al-ālah): Erection of the male organ, taken as an external sign of willingness.
شُبْهَةٌ (shubhah): A doubt or ambiguity in the evidence that prevents the application of hadd punishments.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa's final view, why does coercion by the sultan negate hadd for zina?
A) Because the sultan's command is always lawful
B) Because the erection is not a reliable sign of willingness when there is compelling force
C) Because the sultan is not subject to legal accountability
D) Because the victim can always resist with arms

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school distinguishes between sultan and non-sultan coercion in zina; only sultan coercion creates a doubt that averts hadd.

Confession of Zina with Counterclaim of Marriage
Ruling on hadd when one party confesses zina and other claims marriage

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why a claim of marriage cancels the hadd punishment and state the mahr obligation in such cases.

Core Idea
A plausible claim of marriage creates a doubt that averts hadd but still obligates mahr to honor the woman's honor.

When a person confesses to zina but the other party claims a valid marriage, Hanafi fiqh recognizes a potential doubt that prevents the extreme punishment of hadd. Yet the sanctity of the woman's chastity demands compensation through mahr. This lesson examines the reasoning behind this balanced ruling.

Guided Path
1
State the scenario and the ruling
Identify the two scenarios: man confesses and woman claims marriage, or woman confesses and man claims marriage. Note that the ruling is the same: no hadd, mahr wajib.
2
Identify the cause (sabab) for dropping hadd
The text says the claim of marriage 'yahtamil al-sidq' (is capable of being true) and stands from both sides, creating a shubha (doubt). Understand the principle: hudud are averted by doubts.
This is based on the hadith 'Idra'u al-hudud bi al-shubuhat'.
3
Differentiate between the two consequences
Hadd is dropped entirely, but mahr is still obligatory. Recognize that mahr is due in any sexual intercourse that occurs under a semblance of legality (shubha), not just in valid marriages.
Mahr is wajib to honor the buda' (private part).
4
Consider the ikhtilaf
Note that other schools may have different requirements for hadd when a confession is clear. Some may insist on hadd if the confession is unambiguous. Hanafi relies on the doubt created by the counterclaim.
This highlights the Hanafi emphasis on shubha in hudud.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَمَنْ أَقَرَّ أَرْبَعَ مَرَّاتٍ فِي مَجَالِسَ مُخْتَلِفَةٍ أَنَّهُ زَنَى بِفُلَانَةٍ وَقَالَتْ هِيَ تَزَوَّجَنِي أَوْ أَقَرَّتْ بِالزِّنَاءِ وَقَالَ الرَّجُلُ تَزَوَّجْتُهَا فَلَا حَدَّ عَلَيْهِ وَعَلَيْهِ الْمَهْرُ فِي ذَلِكَ لِأَنَّ دَعْوَى النِّكَاحِ تَحْتَمِلُ الصِّدْقَ وَهُوَ قَائِمٌ بِالطَّرَفَيْنِ فَأَوْرَثَتْ شُبْهَةً وَإِذَا سَقَطَ الْحَدُّ وَجَبَ الْمَهْرُ تَعْظِيمًا لِخَطَرِ الْبُضْعِ
And whoever confesses four times in different sessions that he committed zina with a certain woman, and she says: he married me, or she confesses to zina and the man says: I married her, then there is no hadd on him, and he must pay the mahr in that case, because the claim of marriage is capable of being true and it stands from both parties, so it creates a doubt. And when hadd is dropped, mahr becomes obligatory to magnify the gravity of the vagina.
Source pages p. 164

Commentary
This passage addresses a case where there is a conflict between confession of zina and a claim of marriage. If a man confesses four times in separate gatherings that he committed zina with a specific woman, but the woman consistently claims that he married her, or conversely, if the woman confesses zina and the man claims marriage, then no hadd punishment is imposed on the man. The reason given is that the claim of marriage is plausible (yahtamilu al-sidq) and is presented by both parties (qa'imun bi al-tarafayn), meaning that the mutual assertion creates a doubt (shubha) about whether the act was actually zina. Since hadd is averted by doubts, it is not applied. However, the man is obligated to pay the mahr (dower) as a compensation for the sexual intercourse, to honor the sanctity of the woman's private part (buda'). This highlights the Hanafi principle that mahr is due in cases of sexual intercourse under a shubha (doubtful contract) to protect the woman's dignity, even if the act was technically unlawful.

Key Terms
إِقْرَار (iqrār): A judicial confession, here specifically four times in different sessions (majalis mukhtalifah) for zina.
شُبْهَة (shubhah): A legal doubt that averts the hadd punishment, here arising from a plausible claim of marriage.
الْمَهْر (al-mahr): The dower or bridal gift, here obligatory due to sexual intercourse under a doubtful situation.
الْبُضْع (al-buḍ‘): The vagina or private part of a woman, considered sacred and requiring compensation for unlawful intercourse.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the Hanafi ruling, why does the claim of marriage cancel the hadd?
A) Because the man's confession is invalid
B) Because the claim is plausible and creates a doubt
C) Because the woman's testimony is stronger
D) Because hadd requires witnesses

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, a plausible claim of marriage creates a doubt that averts hadd punishment but still requires mahr to honor the woman's chastity.

Zina with a Slave-Girl and Killing Her: Hadd and Compensation
Intra-Hanafi dispute on whether payment of value for a slave-girl killed through zina drops hadd.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi majority position on hadd and compensation when a man commits zina with a slave-girl and kills her, explain Abu Yusuf's contrary view, and evaluate the key distinction between compensation for killing and compensation for damage to property.

Core Idea
Compensation for killing is blood money, not purchase, so hadd for zina remains.

This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute about the application of hadd when a man commits zina with another's slave-girl and she dies as a result. The central question is whether paying the value of the slave-girl to her owner removes the hadd punishment for zina. Abu Hanifa and Muhammad hold that both hadd and compensation are required, while Abu Yusuf argues that the payment of value establishes ownership and thus drops the hadd, analogous to theft or purchasing the slave-girl after the act.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the two legal elements
The man commits zina (illicit intercourse) and also causes the death of the slave-girl. These are two distinct crimes (jināyatān). The ruling must address both: hadd for zina and compensation (qīmah) for the loss.
The passage states the default view from the matn: both are enforced.
2
State Abu Yusuf's objection and his analogy
Abu Yusuf argues that the obligation to pay value establishes ownership of the slave-girl (like buying her), and since a man cannot commit zina with his own slave, the hadd drops. He analogizes to theft: if the thief owns the stolen property before amputation, the hadd for theft is dropped.
His qiyas: payment of value → ownership → removal of hadd.
3
Trace the majority response (Abu Hanifa & Muhammad)
They counter that the compensation is for killing (ḍamān qatl), which is blood money, not a purchase price. Blood money does not transfer ownership. Even if it did, it would transfer ownership of the physical entity (ayn), not the already consumed benefits of the vagina. Ownership is prospective, so it cannot retroactively affect consumptive use.
The key distinction: compensation for killing vs. compensation for damage to property.
4
Contrast with the case of blinding the slave-girl
The passage compares a different scenario: if the man blinds the slave-girl (damages her eye) through zina, he pays her value and hadd is dropped. Here, ownership is established in the damaged physical entity (the blind slave-girl), creating a doubt (shubha) that averts hadd.
This distinction reinforces the point: ownership in a specific damaged entity drops hadd; blood money does not.
5
Determine the operative ruling for fatwa
The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is that of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad: hadd and value are both wajib. Abu Yusuf's view is considered weak and not acted upon.
The text's argumentation supports the majority, and later Hanafi practice follows this.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَمَنْ زَنَى بِجَارِيَةٍ فَقَتَلَهَا فَإِنَّهُ يُحَدُّ وَعَلَيْهِ الْقِيمَةُ مَعْنَاهُ قَتَلَهَا بِفِعْلِ الزِّنَاءِ لِأَنَّهُ جَنَى جِنَايَتَيْنِ فَيُوَفَّرُ عَلَى كُلِّ وَاحِدٍ مِنْهُمَا حُكْمُهُ وَعَنْ أَبِي يُوسُفَ أَنَّهُ لَا يُحَدُّ لِأَنَّ تَقَرُّرَ ضَمَانِ الْقِيمَةِ سَبَبٌ لِمِلْكِ الْأَمَةِ فَصَارَ كَمَا إِذَا اشْتَرَاهَا بَعْدَ مَا زَنَى بِهَا وَهُوَ عَلَى هَذَا الْاِخْتِلَافِ وَاعْتِرَاضُ سَبَبِ الْمِلْكِ قَبْلَ إِقَامَةِ الْحَدِّ يُوجِبُ سُقُوطَهُ كَمَا إِذَا مَلَكَ الْمَسْرُوقَ قَبْلَ الْقَطْعِ وَلَهُمَا أَنَّهُ ضَمَانُ قَتْلٍ فَلَا يُوجِبُ الْمِلْكَ لِأَنَّهُ ضَمَانُ دَمٍ وَلَوْ كَانَ يُوجِبُهُ فَإِنَّمَا يُوجِبُهُ فِي الْعَيْنِ كَمَا فِي هِبَةِ الْمَسْرُوقِ لَا فِي مَنَافِعِ الْبُضْعِ لِأَنَّهَا اسْتُوفِيَتْ وَالْمِلْكُ يَثْبُتُ مُسْتَقْبَلًا فَلَا يَظْهَرُ فِي الْمُسْتَوْفَى لِكَوْنِهَا مَعْدُومَةً وَهَذَا بِخِلَافِ مَا إِذَا زَنَى بِهَا فَأَذْهَبَ عَيْنَهَا يَجِبُ عَلَيْهِ قِيمَتُهَا وَيَسْقُطُ الْحَدُّ لِأَنَّ الْمِلْكَ هُنَا يَثْبُتُ فِي الْجَارِيَةِ الْعَمْيَاءِ وَهِيَ عَيْنٌ فَأَوْرَثَتْ شُبْهَةً
And whoever commits zina with a slave-girl and then kills her, indeed he is subject to hadd and must pay the value. Meaning: he killed her by the act of zina, because he committed two crimes, so the ruling of each is applied fully. And from Abu Yusuf: that he is not subject to hadd, because the establishment of compensation for the value is a cause for ownership of the slave-girl, so it becomes like when he buys her after having committed zina with her. And it is upon this difference: the interposition of a cause of ownership before establishment of hadd necessitates its dropping, as when he takes possession of the stolen property before amputation. And for them (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) is that it is compensation for killing, so it does not necessitate ownership, because it is blood money. And if it did necessitate it, it would necessitate it in the physical entity (ayn), like in the gift of the stolen property, not in the benefits of the vagina, because they have been consumed, and ownership is established prospectively, so it does not manifest in what has been consumed due to its non-existence. And this is unlike the case where he commits zina with her and then damages her eye; he must pay her value and hadd is dropped, because ownership here is established in the blind slave-girl, which is an entity, so it created a doubt.
Source pages p. 164

Commentary
This passage presents a dispute regarding a man who commits zina with another person's slave-girl and as a result of the zina, the slave-girl dies (or he kills her through the act). The ruling stated in the matn is that the man receives the hadd punishment for zina and must pay the value (qimah) of the slave-girl to her owner. The reason is that he committed two crimes (jinayatayn): zina and killing. The punishment for each is applied fully. However, a narration from Abu Yusuf holds that he is not subject to hadd. His reasoning is that the obligation to pay compensation (damān al-qīmah) establishes ownership of the slave-girl for the perpetrator (through the concept of taqrīr – fixing the value as if purchasing). Thus, it is as if he bought her after committing zina, which would normally drop the hadd (since one cannot commit zina with one's own slave). He draws an analogy with theft: if a thief acquires ownership of the stolen property before the amputation of his hand, the hadd for theft is dropped. Against this, Abu Hanifa and Muhammad (lahumā) argue that this compensation is for killing (damān qatl), which is blood money (diyah), and blood money does not confer ownership. Even if it did, it would confer ownership of the physical entity (ayn), not the benefits of the vagina which have already been consumed (mustawfāt). Ownership is established prospectively (mustaqbil), so it does not affect past consumptive use. The passage distinguishes a different scenario: if the man commits zina with the slave-girl and then damages her eye (causing blindness), he must pay her value and the hadd is dropped. Here, ownership is established in the physically damaged slave-girl (a specific entity, al-jāriyah al-‘amyā’), which creates a doubt (shubha) that averts the hadd.

Key Terms
جِنَايَتَانِ (jināyatān): Two distinct crimes: zina and killing (or causing death through zina).
ضَمَانُ قَتْلٍ (ḍamān qatl): Compensation for killing, considered blood money (diyah), not a purchase price.
الْمَسْرُوقُ (al-masrūq): The stolen property, used here analogically for the slave-girl.
الْجَارِيَةُ الْعَمْيَاءُ (al-jāriyah al-‘amyā’): The slave-girl who becomes blind due to the perpetrator's action; ownership established in a specific damaged entity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the majority Hanafi view (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad), why is hadd not dropped when a man pays the value of a slave-girl he killed through zina?
A) Because the man did not intentionally kill, so hadd remains
B) Because the compensation is for killing (blood money), which does not transfer ownership
C) Because the owner refused the payment
D) Because the man confessed to zina

Key Takeaway
Compensation for killing is blood money, not a purchase; therefore, paying the value of a slave-girl killed through zina does not drop the hadd for zina—both punishments are applied.

Hudud — Fixed Penalties
beginner
6 min
Hadd Dropped Due to Shubha of Marriage Claim
Ruling on hadd when zina confession is met with marriage claim

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when a zina confession is met with a marriage claim and explain the role of shubha in dropping hadd.

Core Idea
A claim of marriage, if credible, creates a shubha that drops the hadd for zina, but the mahr is still wajib.

This passage from Al-Hidayah addresses a specific scenario where a confession of zina is met with a counter-claim of marriage. The Hanafi school holds that the hadd punishment is dropped due to the presence of a valid doubt (shubha), but the mahr must be paid to honor the sanctity of the woman. This ruling illustrates the strength of shubha in hudud cases and the school's care to avoid punishment where uncertainty exists.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
The text presents two symmetrical scenarios: the man confesses zina and the woman claims marriage, or the woman confesses and the man claims marriage. Both lead to the same ruling.
The confession must be made four times in separate meetings for the hadd to be applicable if no shubha exists.
2
Note the requirement for confession
The confession must be made four times in different gatherings. This is a condition for establishing zina through iqrar in Hanafi law.
Based on the Prophet's treatment of Ma'iz.
3
Understand the ruling: no hadd, mahr wajib
The hadd punishment is completely dropped, but the man must pay the mahr (dower) to the woman. This is the core legal outcome.
Mahr is usually due in a valid marriage; here it is due because of the shubha.
4
Analyze the reasoning: shubha from possible truth
The claim of marriage could possibly be true. Since both parties could have been married, a doubt (shubha) arises. Shubha prevents the application of hadd because hadd requires certainty.
The maxim 'Idra'u al-hudud bi al-shubuhat' applies.
5
Recognize the basis for mahr
The mahr is wajib to honor the sanctity of the woman's private parts (al-bud'u). Even though hadd is dropped, the man's access to the woman is not free; the value of her vulva is recognized through mahr.
This shows the Hanafi concern for the woman's dignity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَمَنْ أَقَرَّ أَرْبَعَ مَرَّاتٍ فِي مَجَالِسَ مُخْتَلِفَةٍ أَنَّهُ زَنَى بِفُلَانَةٍ وَقَالَتْ هِيَ تَزَوَّجَنِي أَوْ أَقَرَّتْ بِالزِّنَاءِ وَقَالَ الرَّجُلُ تَزَوَّجْتُهَا فَلَا حَدَّ عَلَيْهِ وَعَلَيْهِ الْمَهْرُ فِي ذَلِكَ لِأَنَّ دَعْوَى النِّكَاحَ يَحْتَمِلُ الصِّدْقِ وَهُوَ يَقُومُ بِالطَّرَفَيْنِ فَأَوْرَثَتْ شُبْهَةً وَإِذَا سَقَطَ الْحَدُّ وَجَبَ الْمَهْرُ تَعْظِيمًا لِخَطَرِ الْبُضْعِ.
ترجمہ اگر کسی مرد نے مختلف مجلسوں میں چار بار اس بات کا اقرار کیا کہ میں نے فلاں عورت سے زنا کیا ہے لیکن اس عورت نے ہر بار یہی کہا ہے کہ اس مرد نے تو مجھ سے نکاح کیا ہے۔ یا خود عورت نے اس طرح اس کے ساتھ زنا کا اقرار کیا اور مرد نے کہا کہ میں نے تو اس سے نکاح کیا ہے تو دونوں صورتوں میں اس مرد پر حد جاری نہ ہوگی۔ البتہ اس پر مہر لازم ہوگا۔ کیونکہ نکاح کے دعوی کی سچائی کا احتمال ہے اور ان دونوں کے درمیان نکاح مانا جاسکتا ہے اس بناء پر شبہ پیدا ہو گیا اور جب شبہ کی وجہ سے حد قائم نہیں ہو سکتی ہے تو عورت کی شرم گاہ کے احترام اور اس کی شرافت ظاہر کرنے کے لئے مہر واجب ہوگا۔
And whoever confesses four times in different gatherings that he committed zina with a certain woman, and she says, 'He married me,' or she confesses to zina and the man says, 'I married her,' then there is no hadd upon him, and upon him is the mahr in that case, because the claim of marriage could be true, and it stands on both sides, so it creates a shubha (doubt). And when the hadd is dropped, the mahr becomes obligatory to honor the sanctity of the vulva.
Source pages p. 165

Commentary
This passage presents a Hanafi ruling on a specific scenario where the hadd punishment for zina is dropped due to a shubha arising from conflicting claims. The text states that if either the man or the woman confesses to zina four times, but the other party claims that a valid marriage occurred instead, then the hadd punishment is not applied. The reasoning is that the claim of marriage could be truthful, and since both parties are potential spouses, a shubha is established. The principle applied here is that any strong doubt (shubha) prevents the application of hadd. However, to protect the dignity and sanctity of the woman's private parts, the mahr (dower) must be paid even though the hadd is dropped. This is a standard Hanafi fiqh ruling based on the concept of shubha, which is a factor that nullifies the fixed punishments (hudud). The commentary also notes that the confession must be made in four separate sessions.

Key Terms
إقرار (iqrar): Confession or acknowledgment, here specifically a voluntary admission of zina made four times in separate meetings.
شبهة (shubha): Doubt or ambiguity that prevents the application of hadd. Here, the conflicting claims of zina and marriage create a credible doubt.
مهر (mahr): The obligatory dower paid by a husband to a wife at the time of marriage. Here, it is paid due to the shubha that the man may have had access through a marriage-like relationship.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is the hadd of zina dropped when a man confesses and the woman claims marriage?
A) Because the confession is not complete.
B) Because the claim of marriage creates a shubha that removes the certainty required for hadd.
C) Because the man did not confess in front of the qadi.
D) Because the woman's claim is automatically taken as true.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, a plausible claim of marriage against a zina confession creates a shubha that drops the hadd, but the mahr remains wajib to protect the woman's dignity.

Zina with Slave-Girl Then Killing Her: Hadd and Compensation
Ruling and intra-Hanafi dispute on hadd and qimah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the intra-Hanafi dispute on whether hadd is dropped when qimah is obligatory for killing a slave-girl after zina, and apply the distinction between daman dam (blood-wit) and daman mal (compensation for property).

Core Idea
Two separate crimes require two separate penalties – paying qimah for killing does not create ownership that drops hadd.

This passage presents a key intra-Hanafi disagreement: when a man commits zina with another's slave-girl and she dies as a result, does he face both hadd and financial compensation, or does the compensation (qimah) itself extinguish the hadd? The dispute turns on whether qimah for killing (a blood-wit) creates ownership of the slave-girl, thus removing the violation of another's property that is a prerequisite for hadd. We will analyze the positions of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad versus Abu Yusuf, including the analogies each side uses.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Foundational Ruling
Note that the default position of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad is that both hadd and qimah are imposed because the man committed two separate crimes: zina (requiring hadd) and killing (requiring compensation). Understand that each crime is evaluated independently.
The ruling treats the acts as distinct torts with separate legal consequences.
2
Examine Abu Yusuf's Qiyas
Abu Yusuf argues that the obligation to pay qimah effectively makes the man the owner of the slave-girl retrospectively, similar to if he bought her after zina. He analogizes to the case of theft: if a thief becomes owner of the stolen property before the hadd (cutting of hand) is executed, the hadd is dropped.
His reasoning is based on the cause (sabab) of ownership arising before hadd is established.
3
Analyze the Hanafi Rebuttal (Abu Hanifa & Muhammad)
The majority rebuttal emphasizes that the qimah for killing is daman dam (blood-wit), not daman mal (compensation for property). It does not confer ownership because it is compensation for the life taken. Even if it did confer ownership, it would only affect the substance (ayn) of the slave-girl, not the already-consumed usufruct (the sexual act). Therefore, the hadd remains.
They distinguish between ownership of the substance and ownership of the usufruct; the latter is already spent.
4
Note the Exception: Maimed but Living Slave-Girl
The passage contrasts the case where the slave-girl is blinded (but lives): here, the hadd is dropped because ownership of the maimed female slave (a living substance) creates a shubha (doubt) that prevents hadd. This highlights that ownership of a living entity can create shubha, but ownership of a deceased entity does not.
This exception shows the importance of the type of ownership (over a living being vs. over a dead body) in creating shubha.

Source Text

وَمَنْ زَنَى بِجَارِيَةٍ فَقَتَلَهَا فَإِنَّهُ يُحَدُّ وَعَلَيْهِ الْقِيْمَةُ مَعْنَاهُ قَتَلَهَا بِفِعْلِ الزِّنَاءِ لِأَنَّهُ جَنَى جَنَايَتَيْنِ فَيُوَفَّرَ عَلَى كُلِّ وَاحِدٍ مِنْهُمَا حُكْمُهُ وَعَنْ أَبِي يُوسُفَ أَنَّهُ لَا يُحَدُّ لِأَنَّ تَقَرُّرَ ضَمَانِ الْقِيْمَةِ سَبَبٌ لِمِلْكِ الْأَمَةِ فَصَارَ كَمَا إِذَا اشْتَرَاهَا بَعْدَ مَا زَنَى بِهَا وَهُوَ عَلَى هَذَا الْاِخْتِلَافِ وَاعْتِرَاضُ سَبَبِ الْمِلْكِ قَبْلَ إِقَامَةِ الْحَدِّ يُوجِبُ سُقُوطَهُ كَمَا إِذَا مَلَكَ الْمَسْرُوقُ قَبْلَ الْقَطْعِ وَلَهُمَا أَنَّهُ ضَمَانُ قَتْلٍ فَلَا يُوجِبُ الْمِلْكَ لِأَنَّهُ ضَمَانُ دَمٍ وَلَوْ كَانَ يُوجِبُهُ فَإِنَّمَا يُوجِبُهُ فِي الْعَيْنِ كَمَا فِي هِبَةِ الْمَسْرُوقِ لَا فِي مَنَافِعِ الْبُضْعِ لِأَنَّهَا اسْتُوْفِيَتْ وَالْمِلْكُ يَثْبُتُ مُسْتَقْبِلًا فَلَا يَظْهَرُ فِي الْمُسْتَوْفَى لِكَوْنِهِ مَعْدُومًا وَهَذَا بِخِلَافِ مَا إِذَا زَنَى بِهَا فَأَذْهَبَ عَيْنَهَا يَجِبُ عَلَيْهِ قِيمَتُهَا وَيَسْقُطُ الْحَدُّ لِأَنَّ الْمِلْكَ هُنَا يَثْبُتُ فِي الْجَنَّةِ الْعَمْيَاءِ وَهِيَ عَيْنٌ فَأَوْرَثَتْ شُبْهَةً
ترجمہ اگر کسی نے کسی کی باندی سے زنا کیا پھر اسے قتل کر دیا اس قتل کرنے کا مطلب یہ ہے کہ اس کے زنا کے سبب سے ہی وہ مرگئی ہے اس لئے اس مرد کو حد لگائی جائے گی اور اس پر اس باندی کی قیمت بھی لازم ہوگی۔ کیونکہ اس شخص نے دو جرم کئے یعنی زنا کرنا اور مارڈالنا۔ اس لئے ہر ایک جرم میر اس کا حکم مرتب ہوگا اور امام ابو یوسف رحمۃ اللہ علیہ سے ایک روایت ہے کہ اسے حد نہیں لگائی جائے گی۔ کیونکہ اس زانی پر قیمت کا جرمانہ لازم کرنے کے سبب سے وہ شخص اس باندی کا مالک ہو گیا۔ تو وہ حکما ایسا ہو گیا کہ گویا اس نے باندی سے زنا کرنے کے بعد اسے خرید لیا ہے۔ لیکن خود اس مسئلہ میں بھی ایسا ہی اختلاف ہے۔ ابو یوسف رحمہ اللہ فرماتے ہیں کہ حد قائم ہونے سے پہلے ملک کا سبب پیدا ہو جانا حد کے ساقط ہو جانے کا سبب ہوتا ہے۔ جیسے کہ کسی چور کا ہاتھ کاٹے جانے سے پہلے وہ خود اس مال کا مالک ہو گیا ہو۔ تو اس کا ہاتھ کاٹا جانا ختم ہو جاتا ہے اور امام ابو حنیفہ رحمتہ اللہ علیہ ومحمدرحمہ اللہ علیہ کی دلیل یہ ہے کہ اس پر جو قیمت لازم آتی ہے وہ ملکیت کے پائے جانے کا سبب نہیں ہوتا ہے بلکہ وہ تو اس کے قتل کرنے کا جرمانہ ہے۔ کیونکہ یہ قیمت اس کے خون کا عوض ہے اور اگر جرمانہ ملکیت کے ثابت ہونے کا سبب بھی ہوتا تو اس سے باندی کی ذات کو اس کی ملکیت میں لازم کرتا جیسے کہ (یعنی پوری باندی بھی اس کی ملکیت میں آجاتی ) جیسے کہ اگر چور اپنے چوری کی ہوئی چیز کا اپنے ہاتھ کاٹے جانے سے پہلے مالک ہو جائے تو اس کا ہاتھ کا شمارک جاتا ہے اور امام محمد رحمتہ اللہ علیہ اور امام ابو حنیفہ رحمۃ اللہ علیہ کی دلیل یہ ہے کہ وہ قیمت جو اس پر لازم آتی ہے وہ اس کے فعل قتل کا جرمانہ اور تاوان ہے۔ اس لئے وہ ملکیت کی موجب نہیں ہے۔ کیونکہ وہ تو خون کا عوض ہے اور اگر بی تاوان ملکیت کا سامان ہوتا جب بھی عین باندی کی ملکیت کو لازم کرتا۔ جیسے چوری کا مال ہبہ کرنے کی صورت ہے۔ یعنی جیسے چور کو اس کا چوری کیا ہوا مال دے دیا گیا ہو۔ اسی طرح باندی کی ذات اس کی ملکیت میں آگئی اور اس کی شرم گاہ سے نفع حاصل کرنے کا سبب نہیں ہے۔ کیونکہ یہ نفع تو پہلے ہی حاصل کر لیا گیا ہے اور ملک کا ثبوت استفادی ہوا ہے اس لئے وہ نفع جو پہلے ہی حاصل کیا جا چکا ہے اس میں اس کا اثر ظاہر نہیں ہوگا۔ کیونکہ وہ نفع اب معدوم ہو چکا ہے اور یہ حکم اس صورت کے برخلاف ہے جب کسی نے کسی دوسرے کی باندی سے زنا کر کے اس کی ایک آنکھ اندھی کر دی تو اس پر باندی کی قیمت لازم آئے گی اور اس سے حد ساقط ہو جائے گی۔ کیونکہ اس صورت میں اس کی ایک آنکھ والی اندھی) کی ملکیت ثابت ہوگی۔ چونکہ یہ ایک آنکھ والی ہے۔ اس لئے اس میں شبہ پیدا ہو گیا ۔ ف۔ یعنی پہلی صورت میں جب کہ وہ مرگئی ہو تو وہ ملکیت میں آنے کے قابل نہیں رہی اور ملکیت ظاہر بھی ہو تو اس باندی کی عین ذات میں ہوگی اس کی منفعت میں ملکیت نہیں ہوگی۔ کیونکہ اس نفع کو حاصل کرنا اب کوئی باقی چیز نہیں رہی ہے۔ بخلاف دوسری صورت کے کہ اس میں اس کی ذات یعنی کانی باندی باقی ہے۔
Whoever commits zina with a slave-girl and then kills her, then indeed he is subjected to hadd and upon him is the price (qimah) – meaning he killed her by the act of zina because he committed two crimes, so the ruling of each is given separately. And from Abu Yusuf it is narrated that he is not subjected to hadd because the establishment of the guarantee of the price is a cause for ownership of the slave-girl, so it becomes as if he bought her after he committed zina with her. And there is this difference of opinion: the occurrence of the cause of ownership before the establishment of the hadd necessitates its dropping, just as when the stolen property is owned before the cutting [of the hand]. And for them (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) there is the argument that it is a guarantee for killing, so it does not cause ownership because it is a blood-wit. And even if it did cause ownership, it would cause it in the substance [of the slave-girl] as in the case of a gift of stolen property, not in the usufruct of the vulva because those were already enjoyed, and ownership is established prospectively, so it does not appear in what has been used up because it is non-existent. And this is different from the case where he commits zina with her and destroys her eye: the price is obligatory upon him and the hadd is dropped because ownership here is established in the blind female slave, which is a substance, so it creates a shubha.
Source pages p. 165

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling and intra-Hanafi dispute regarding a man who commits zina with another's slave-girl and in the process kills her. The ruling attributed to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad (as the relied-upon view) is that both hadd for zina and the qimah (market value of the slave-girl) are imposed because the man committed two separate crimes: zina and causing death. Each crime carries its own punishment. However, Imam Abu Yusuf holds that the hadd is dropped because the obligation of paying the qimah effectively makes the man the owner of the slave-girl retrospectively, similar to if he had purchased her after zina. He analogizes to the case of theft: if the thief becomes owner of the stolen property before the cutting of the hand, the hadd is dropped. The response of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad explains that the qimah for killing is a blood-wit (daman dam) and does not confer ownership; it is compensation for the life taken. Even if it did confer ownership, it would apply to the substance of the slave-girl, not to the already consumed usufruct (the sexual act). Therefore, the hadd remains. The passage also distinguishes a case where the slave-girl is blinded but lives: here, the qimah is paid for the lost eye, and ownership of the maimed slave-girl creates a shubha (due to the woman being partly owned), so the hadd is dropped. This distinction highlights the principle that ownership in a living entity can create shubha, but ownership in a deceased entity does not.

Key Terms
جارية (jariyah): A female slave; a legally owned woman.
قيمة (qimah): The market value or price of the slave-girl, to be paid as compensation for killing or destroying part of her.
شبهة (shubha): A doubt that prevents the application of hadd. Here, ownership of a maimed but living slave-girl creates shubha regarding the man's right to sexual access.
ضمان دم (daman dam): Blood-wit; compensation for taking a life, which does not transfer ownership of the deceased person.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because the slave-girl is not a legal person.
B) Because the payment of qimah makes the man the owner of the slave-girl, similar to buying her after zina.
C) Because the man acted under duress.
D) Because the death was accidental.

Key Takeaway
When a man commits zina with another's slave-girl and she dies, the mufta bihi Hanafi position is that both hadd and qimah apply because the crimes are distinct and the qimah for killing is a blood-wit that does not confer ownership.

Hudud — Fixed Penalties
intermediate
6 min
The Supreme Imam and Hadd Punishments: Exemption for Divine Rights, Liability for Human Rights
Ruling with proof on the supreme imam's exemption from hadd

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that the supreme imam is immune from hadd punishments but liable for qisas and financial claims, and explain the rational proof based on the distinction between divine rights (huquq Allah) and human rights (huquq al-ibad).

Core Idea
The imam's immunity from hadd arises from his role as enforcer of divine law, not from absolute exemption.

This lesson examines a unique principle in Hanafi criminal law: when the supreme ruler (imam) commits an offense normally subject to a fixed punishment (hadd), a special exemption applies. The rule is grounded in the nature of hudud as divine rights entrusted to the imam for enforcement, which he cannot enforce upon himself. However, this immunity does not extend to claims of individuals, such as qisas or debts. Understanding this distinction illuminates the Hanafi conception of sovereignty and justice.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
The act is the supreme imam committing an offense that ordinarily incurs a hadd punishment. The ruling: no hadd is applied to him.
The imam is the ruler with no superior authority.
2
Recognize the exceptions
The immunity from hadd does not cover qisas (retaliatory punishment for murder or injury) or financial obligations (debts). These are taken from the imam.
The text explicitly excludes qisas and financial rights.
3
Analyze the rational proof (dalil aqli)
The proof is logical: hudud are the rights of Allah, and their enforcement is the imam's sole responsibility. He cannot enforce a hadd on himself because it would be futile (he would be both enforcer and subject).
This is a rational argument from necessity.
4
Contrast with huquq al-ibad
The rights of individuals (qisas, debts) are enforced by the claimant. The claimant can either be enabled by the imam to take the right or seek help from the Muslim military to enforce it.
The imam can still grant permission for qisas to be carried out.
5
Apply the reasoning to hadd al-qadhf
Hadd al-qadhf (false accusation of zina) is classified as predominantly a right of the law (shar'), not a pure human right, so it follows the same ruling as other hudud: the imam is not subject to it.
This shows how the classification determines the application.

قَالَ وَكُلُّ شَيْءٍ صَنَعَهُ الْإِمَامُ الَّذِي لَيْسَ فَوْقَهُ إِمَامٌ فَلَا حَدَّ عَلَيْهِ إِلَّا الْقِصَاصُ فَإِنَّهُ يُؤْخَذُ بِهِ وَبِالْأَمْوَالِ لِأَنَّ الْحُدُودَ حَقُّ اللَّهِ تَعَالَى وَإِقَامَتُهَا إِلَيْهِ لَا إِلَى غَيْرِهِ وَلَا يُمْكِنُهُ أَنْ يُقِيمَ عَلَى نَفْسِهِ لِأَنَّهُ لَا يُفِيدُ بِخِلَافِ حُقُوقِ الْعِبَادِ لِأَنَّهَا يَسْتَوْفِيهَا وَلِيُّ الْحَقِّ إِمَّا بِتَمْكِينِهِ أَوْ بِالِاسْتِعَانَةِ بِمَنَعَةِ الْمُسْلِمِينَ وَالْقِصَاصُ وَالْأَمْوَالُ مِنْهَا وَأَمَّا حَدُّ الْقَذْفِ قَالُوا الْمُغَلَّبُ فِيهِ حَقُّ الشَّرْعِ فَحُكْمُهُ كَحُكْمِ سَائِرِ الْحُدُودِ الَّتِي هِيَ حَقُّ اللَّهِ تَعَالَى
ترجمہ اور مسلمانوں کا ایسا امام جس کے اوپر دوسرا کوئی بڑا امام نہ ہو اگر قابل حد حرکت کر بیٹھے تو بھی اس پر حد واجب نہیں ہوگی سوائے قصاص کے یعنی اگر اس پر کسی مسلمان کا قصاص واجب ہو تو اس کے لئے اسے پکڑا جائے گا اور اگر لوگوں کا مال اس پر واجب ہو تو اس کے لئے بھی اسے پکڑا جائے گا۔ اس کی وجہ یہ ہے کہ حدود کا حق تو صرف اللہ تبارک تعالے کا ہے۔ البتہ اس کو جاری اور نافذ کرنے کا اختیار امام کو دیا گیا ہے کسی دوسرے کو نہیں۔ کیونکہ دنیا کے احکام میں وہی سب سے بڑا ہے اور اس کے لئے یہ ممکن نہیں ہے۔ کیونکہ جو بندہ حقدار ہوتا ہے۔ وہ اپنا حق حاصل کر لے گا خواہ اس طرح که امام خود ہی اس حق دار کو اختیار دیدے۔ یا وہ شخص مسلمانوں کے لشکر اور قوت سے مدد لے کر اپنا حق حاصل کرے۔ قصاص اور مالوں کی وصولی بھی اس قسم کے معاملات ہیں یعنی ان کا تعلق بھی حقوق العباد سے ہے اور حد قذف ہونے کی صورت میں ( مثلا امام نے کسی کو زنا کاری کی تہمت لگائی تو)
He said: And everything that the imam who has no imam above him does, then there is no hadd upon him except for qisas, for indeed he is taken to account for it and for financial obligations. Because the hudud are the right of Allah Almighty, and their enforcement belongs to Him and no one else, and it is not possible for him to enforce it upon himself because it would be of no benefit. Unlike the rights of men, for they are exacted by the one entitled to the right either by the imam granting him the ability or by seeking assistance from the military strength of the Muslims. And qisas and financial obligations are among them. As for the hadd of qadhf (false accusation of zina), they said that the predominant element is the right of the law (shar'), so its ruling is like the ruling of all other hudud which are the right of Allah Almighty.
Source pages p. 166

Commentary
This passage discusses the legal status of the supreme imam (khalifah with no superior) who himself commits an offense that ordinarily requires a hadd punishment. The ruling is that the imam is not subject to hadd because the hadd punishments are rights of Allah (huquq Allah) and their enforcement is entrusted to the imam alone; he cannot enforce them on himself as that would be meaningless (since he is the executor). However, this immunity does not extend to the rights of men (huquq al-ibad). Therefore, if the imam commits murder requiring qisas, or owes money to people, these are taken from him because they are rights of individuals. The rightful claimant can either be enabled by the imam to take the qisas or can seek help from the Muslim community's military forces. The passage also notes that the hadd of qadhf (false accusation of zina) is considered predominantly a right of the law (shar'), not a pure right of men, so its ruling follows that of other hudud (i.e., not applied to the imam). This distinction highlights the Hanafi principle that hudud are primarily divine rights, and the imam's role as enforcer precludes him from being the subject of those punishments.

Key Terms
الإمام (al-imam): The supreme ruler of the Muslims with no superior authority; the caliph or political leader.
حَدُّ الْقَذْفِ (hadd al-qadhf): Fixed punishment for false accusation of zina (80 lashes). The text treats it as a hybrid right: predominantly the right of Allah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is the supreme imam not subject to hadd punishments according to this passage?
A) Because he is infallible.
B) Because hudud are the right of Allah and their enforcement is his responsibility; he cannot enforce them on himself.
C) Because he is above all laws.
D) Because he can repent and be forgiven.

Key Takeaway
The supreme imam is exempt from hadd punishments because they are divine rights, but he remains liable for qisas and financial obligations as human rights.

The Ruler's Immunity from Hadd: An Exception for Qisas
States a legal maxim: the ruler is free from hadd except qisas.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the supreme ruler's liability for hadd punishments and distinguish qisas as an exception.

Core Idea
The supreme ruler is immune from hadd, but not from qisas.

This lesson examines a key principle in Hanafi criminal law: the immunity of the supreme ruler (Imam al-a'zam) from fixed corporal punishments (hudud). While ordinary citizens are subject to hadd for specific crimes, the ruler enjoys a special status. However, this immunity does not extend to qisas (retaliation) — a distinction rooted in the balance between public authority and private rights.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Subject and the Exception
The passage states a general rule (no hadd on the ruler) and an exception (qisas). Identify both clearly.
2
Understand the Rationale
The commentary explains that in hudud involving both Allah's right and individual's right, Allah's right prevails, leading to the ruler's immunity. Qisas is purely a private right, so it remains applicable.
3
Apply the Ruling
In practice, if the ruler commits a hadd crime (e.g., theft), he is not subject to the hadd punishment. But if he commits murder or causes injury, qisas (or diya) may apply.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب الوطى الذي يوجب الحد والذى لا يوجبه . . اشرف البدایہ شرح اردو ہدایہ جلد ششم یہ جس طرح حق شرع ہے اسی طرح اس میں بندہ کا بھی حق متعلق ہوتا ہے۔ تو یہ دیکھنا ہے کہ ان دونوں میں سے کس حق کا لحاظ کیا جائے گا۔ علماء نے اس کا جواب دیا ہے کہ اس میں حق شرع غالب ہوگا۔ لہذا اس کا حکم بھی ان حدود کی طرح ہوگا جن کا تعلق خالص حق الہی عزوجل سے ہے۔ تشریح قَالَ وَكُلُّ شَيْءٍ صَنْعَهُ الْإِمَامُ الذِى لَيْسَ فَوْقَهُ إِمَامٌ فَلَاحَدٌ عَلَيْهِ إِلَّا الْقِصَاصُ .. الخ ترجمہ سے مطلب واضح ہے۔
Chapter on Intercourse that Necessitates Hadd and That Which Does Not Necessitate It. ... Ashraf al-Bidaya Sharh Urdu Hidaya Volume Six: Just as this is the right of Shariah, similarly the right of the slave is also attached to it. So it is to be seen which of these two rights will be considered. The scholars have answered that in this the right of Shariah will prevail. Therefore its ruling will be like those hudud that are purely related to the right of Allah Almighty. Explanation: He said: 'And everything that the Imam who has no Imam above him does, there is no hadd upon him except qisas... etc.' The meaning is clear from the translation.

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement from the chapter on intercourse that necessitates hadd. It discusses the nature of hadd penalties where both Allah's right and the individual's right are involved. The author clarifies that in such cases, Allah's right predominates, so the ruling follows those hudud that are purely Allah's rights. The reference is to a ruling that the supreme ruler (Imam with no superior) is not subject to hadd for his actions except in cases of qisas (retaliation). This establishes a legal principle of immunity for the supreme ruler's official acts.

Key Terms
حد (hadd): A fixed corporal punishment prescribed by Islamic law for specific crimes.
قصاص (qisas): Retaliation in kind, usually for murder or physical injury.
إمام (imam): The supreme ruler with no superior; the caliph or head of state.

The supreme ruler’s immunity from hadd is not absolute; qisas remains applicable, underscoring the inviolability of private claims.
